THE BED PAGE .
The Case of Kipling — Pro:
IF I understand you, probably your two
main charges are (1) that Kipling’s debts
to his predecessors are far too great and
should be acknowledged, and (2) that the
breaking down or alleged breaking down of his
“omniscience” is a serious blot on the value of
his work,
[For (1) read simply, Kipling is a plagiarist, and the
practice of plagiary is discreditable. For (2) Kipling
falsifies fact and phrase, and false art is bad art.]
The.first charge is almost as old as the world,
and my answer is “ the thought belongs to the
man who expresses it best.” Do you really think
it a matter of great importance that “Ye Gentle-
men of England ” was written before “Ye Mari-
ners of England ” ?
A verse from each is sufficient answer—
If enemies oppose us
When England is at war
With any foreign nation,
We fear not wounds or scar ;
Our roving guns shall teach ’em
Our valour for to know,
Whilst they reel on the keel,
And the stormy winds do blow.
Britannia needs no bulwarks,
No towers along the steep,
Her march is o’er the mountain waves,
Her home is on the deep.
With thunders from her native oak
She quells the floods below, —
As they roar on the shore,
When the stormy winds do blow ;
When the battle rages loud and long,
And the stormy winds do blow.
The one is mere verse writing, and poor at that;
the other is a trumpet-call that stirred the hearts of
Campbell’s countrymen, and still continues to do
so.
[’T was “ a matter of great importance ” to Campbell,
anyway, because without the original there could have
been no imitation.]
“Indeed, if the works of the great poets teach
anything, it is to hold mere invention somewhat
cheap. It is not the finding of a thing, but the
making something out of it after it is found, that
is of consequence,” says Lowell in his essay on
Chaucer. Again:—“Accordingly Chaucer, like
Shakespeare, invented almost nothing. Where-
ver he found anything directed to Geoffrey
Chaucer he took it and made the most of it. It
w(its not the subject treated, but himself that was the
new thing. ‘ Cela m’ appartient de droit,’ Moli&re
is reported to have said when accused of plagiar-
ism.”
[Lowell’s statements are loose and inaccurate. Moli&re’s
is impudent. Both “the finding ’’and “the makihg”
are of consequence. No finding—-no making.]
Tennyson, who, of course, has often been ac-
cused of plagiarism, once said, “They will not
allow one to say ‘ Ring the bells ’ without finding
we have taken it from Sir P. Sidney, or even to
use such a simple expression as the ocean ‘roars’
without finding out the precise verse in Homer or
Horace from which we have plagiarised it.”
[Yes, when Tennyson talked of plagiarism, Tennyson
knew what he was talking about. “ Courage, my com-
rades ; let us defend ourselves against these rascally
judges ; ” wrote one of Loinbroso’s criminals on his celi-
wall.j
But to come to a poem you have specially
singled out, “ Recessional.” “ His metre is
taken from Quarles, his emotion and attitude
from Newman.” Any special metre is no poet’s
close preserve ; and it cannot be denied that the
metre chosen was an excellent vehicle for what
Kipling had to say. The other charge is more
serious, but surely his “emotion” was that of a
level-headed man in a period of popular excite-
ment, his “attitude ” that of a patriotic man ; and
for his ideas he did not need to go to Newman.
Are they not written in the Old Testament and in
the history of the nations ? “ You admit he did
plagiarise it from somewhere, then ? ” No, I do
not. For it is not plagiarism to re-vitalise old
thoughts. And what is there new to write about
some things. Gould you express penitence with-
out trenching on the penitential psalms ?
[Point was that he did go to Newman, he “ went for ”
Newman like a kangaroo for green corn. As to “ re-
vitalising old thoughts ’’—come back to the point, com-
pare the verses.]
_ It is a question whether there is not a certain
littleness about the way we criticise and carp at
our great men. Cannot we be content to take a
good thing without looking for flaws in it ? I
wonder whether on a great occasion, when the
dumb was made to speak, some critics were found
to say, “Oh, yes, it certainly is very wonderful;
the man speaks quite plainly; but what a bad
accent he has ! ”
[No; must criticise; must point out flaws—otherwise
there’s no distinction between flawed and flawless—Bad
or Pretty-Good steals Good’s credit.]
Now, as regards the “ omniscience.” I regard
this as a much less important question, as Kipling’s
mistakes are usually m details which do not touch
the heart of the matter. It does not trouble me
very much that, in an American round-house, he
would not have heard loco, used for locomotive, or
have said bogie when he meant truck. His school-
boys are near enough to the mark for most people
(a little too diabolically clever, perhaps), and the
weather-stained and worn copy of “ Barrack-room
Ballads ” in front of me was for years the close
companion of a Scotch chief-engineer in the P.
and O. service, who had no little admiration for
the poem called “McAndrew’s Hymn,” and who,
in some ways, might have stood for the portrait of
McAndrew himself. —Percival Seele.
[All details touch the heart of the matter—for the man
who comprehends them. You can’t defend K. on the
ground that you are too ignorant to be offended.]
After all, are the ethics of Art comparable with
the ethics of Life ? Can any man claim specific
rights in an idea which may have come to him in
any one of a dozen ways, from the pure coinage of
his own brain to the plain filching from another
man’s. Once expressed, an idea becomes public
property; often it is public property before being
expressed, a sub-conscious embryo of thought
which the keen observer annexes and materialises
—nobody the wiser. The BookfcUours analogy is
not permissible, is even absurd. A theft of pro-
perty leaves the victim poorer while the community
at large remains untouched. A theft of ideas
(except in the rarest cases) does not impoverish
anyone, it may even enrich ; while undoubted gain
accrues to the community. , e
[As a rule, the man very well knows whether he earned
the idea or fi ched it,-Expressed ideas do not become
public property. There ,s legal copyright in the expres
sion—laws recognition of the author’s property - A fheft
of copyright ideas may impoverish so much that you can
sue for damages-community’s recognition of justice.]
ITow many dozens of obscure writers would
never have been heard of had not Shakespeare'
honoured them by occasional exploitations ” h,
consequence they have been unearthed by inde
fatigable students to loom large awhile in the
glory of a borrowed sunshine. Amidst their
interminable garbage, the poet found pearls (per-
chance they wot not of) which he polished and
reset to the profound content of subsequent
humanity. Where lies the immorality of such an
act which benefits everybody ? In a fable recently
quoted in if. the nightingale wisely comments
that even if the moon borrows its radiance, it does
so to advantage.
' S^ he o] d ar gument of rob to build a church, and
win salvation. Wrong s wrong, whatever accidental good
accrue. J ”
Undoubtedly Kipling invigorates Flecknoe’s
verse, and the excellent closing line is
entirely K’s. “Recessional” and New-
mans poem both express current ideas
i • „ s ß lrl^ sphere of thought. “Bill
Awlur.s is a complete transmutation. Plagiar-
is oft en unconscious. In to-day’s Bulletin
U/b/1) an idea, expressed by myself in conversa-
tion two days previously, in reference to the de-
corations, is formulated almost word for word as
1 uttered it. Has the B. a prior claim to that
idea because it got there first ?
[Defence of the Flecknoe theft makes one gasp ! For
the rest, look at the facts. Certainly the It. has a prior
claim to the expression of the idea. And this coincidence
was unconscious; Kipling’s transference was conscious
and deliberate.]
Kipling must be judged, as all other workers,
by the totality of his achievement, not by the
peccadilloes that blemish it. Such judgment
[Who thought of judging him otherwise ?]
j V rn ?^L l^n^cs ’ P^ ace him us the most vigorous
and healthy of modern literary forces, but as lack-
ing that universality which alone makes for im-
mortality. —Aletes.
Dear Bookfellow. —With reference to your dis-
cussion of Kipling’s plagiarism. There are two
ways of considering the question—one, from the
point of view of the literary artist; the other,
from that of the work of art itself. In estimating
the claims of any literary artist, the fact that he
has drawn upon others must not be overlooked.
But, on the other hand, is it not the aim to pro-
duce somehow or other great works of art? and
finally must they not be judged in and for them-
selves, _as assuredly they will when all else is
buned in the past ? Then, what does it matter
whether the material was supplied by one man
and the art by another ? If every literary artist
had to start with collecting his own material he
would never get to the working of it. If Phidias
had to quarry his own marble, would he be able
also to carve statues? In the world of science we
find men doing nothing more than collecting data
for the generalisations of future scientists. Would
you, on that account, object to the result of the
labour that has been necessarily distributed ? You
may say that Ptolemy, or Copernicus, or Newton,
or Bad was not this or that; yet it is not astrono-
mers we are aiming at, but astronomy.—Sincerely
yours, L. H. S. Brodzky, The University of Mel-
bourne, 17th May, 1901.
[Parallel denied. Put it this way. “If Phidias took an
obscure sculptor’s statues, and altered or improved them,
would he deserve as much original credit as if he had
hewn his own from the marble block?” “It is not in-
dividual wealth we are aiming at, but general prosperity.
That gives the community no right to steal the loose
change from my breeches-pocket.” Astronomy and
literature make no less progress by preventing one man
from cabbaging another’s credit.]
The Bookfellow’s application of the law of literary
honesty is too severe. Many of the speeches in
Shakespeare’s historical plays are mere]y slabs of
Holinshed’s imaginative “Chronicle” done into
blank verse. The famous “cloud-capped towers”
passage resembles some lines by Sir David Lind-
say as closely as Kipling’s “Love and Death”
verses resemble Flecknoe's. Another passage in the
“ Tempest ” is paraphrased from Montaigne, and
as for plots, it is doubtful if Shakespeare ever
invented one. Cray's “Elegy” was described by
Russell Lowell as a mosaic of choice phrases
culled from older writers, and in some editions of.
Cray’s poems many of these originals were
printed in footnotes to do honour to the poet’s
craftsmanship. Burns’s stanza, beginning—
My love is like a red, red rose,
That’s newly sprung in June ; ”
is a scrap of Scottish folk-song with far less altera-
tion than Kipling gave to “ Hab any o’ yo’ seen
my Lulu ? ”
[This is the MacOallum argument that if you multiply
one thief by fifty you make an honest man. Will’ the
deuce should live Smith be excused for stealing my coat
because dead Jones stole trousers?]
Goethe declared in his autobiography that he
had written nothing original ; whatever he had
written he had learnt from other men. In fact,
this “unwritten literary law,” under which Kip-
ling is sentenced to a back seat, is a latter-day
f“ Sentenced to a back seat ” is misrepresentation—
without warrant in the original article.]
notion born of modern self-consciousness and the
multiplication of ill-instructed scribes. The
Bookfellow accepts it and carries it to an extreme,
thinking shame of Kipling for casting his “Reces-
sional ” in a metre which old Francis Quarles had
[Misrepresentation. No “shame” was thought or
ascribed on this account solely. “Shame” was attri-
buted to K. as a conscious plagiarist only ; and his credit
as an original worker was impugned by showing the
extent of his imitations—Quarles’s metre being an inci-
dental part.]
used before him. If the poets agreed with The
>Bookfellow there would soon be no more poetry.
[lf the poets all stole from one another, instead of
trying to voice independent thought, there would soon
be no more poetry.]
The metre in question, bv the way, is also the
metre of Wordsworth’s poem on Daffodils, a
[Roughly speaking, it is. Critically speaking, it is not.
Kipling got “ Recessional” metre from Quarles, not from
Wordsworth, who handles it differently from both
Quarles and Kipling.]
clever parody of which, descriptive of Sydney
trams, appeared recently in The Bulletin with-
out acknowledgment.
[As a'parody. But Kipling’s “parodies” are put for-
ward as original work.]
After all, the principal source of “Recessional”
its attitude, emotion, ideas and phrases—is
neither in Newman, Quarles, Emerson, nor the
newspapers, but the Old Testament; and its imme-
diate effect was not unlike that sought by Hebrew
seers. It changed national sentiment from boast-
ing to prayer. Granted that the prayer was but a
muddle-headed thing and only half sincere, yet it
was better than the brag.—Vincent Naylor.
[Dunno. Prefer blatant brutality to smug hypocrisy.
It profits the Boers nothing that they are sandbagged
rather than dubbed.]
Dear Bookfellow,— “Jjff,” dealing with “The
Strange Ride of Morrowbie Jukes,” has gone
mare’s-nesting. Kipling is quite correct as to the
gun-barrel measurements. Naturally, the man
who is walking out of the quicksand turns to his
own left and right all the time ; and, when he
turns backward, his right and left become re-
versed. “Jiff” should have put himself in the
other fellow’s place. The course, if plotted out as
the man would have walked it, brings him out at
a point considerably beyond and to left of his
starting-point—as Kipling meant. And he does
not retrace his path.
Again, re Victoria “importing jarrah.” Vic.
has imported jarrah for years past, millions of
feet coming in from W.A.; and Maoriland is now
doing likewise. What is more, it is going through
Sydney, the home of superior hardwoods, as wit-
ness the “ Westralia” shipment of loth June, and
the “ Klingamite” of 26th.— Jeff.

RE Kipling. Here is an extract from
Ran Francisco Chronicle of April 29,
1901—
Every time Ernest Seton-Thompson appears in a citv
for the first time up comes the discussion as to whether
or not he or Rudyard Kipling was the first in the field
with animal stories. There is no personal controversy
between these two men, for each, of course, has an exact
understanding of his position, but the adherents of both
make merry war at times over their respective merits and
rights. The truth of the matter is that many of the best-
known animal stories by Seton were in print years before j
Kipling thought of his delightful tales of the jungle, and
Kipling very frankly told Seton within the past two years
that his pathetically human story of Molly Cottontail and
her little Raggylug was the inspiration for the Jungle
Books.
the confusion and the injustice come from the fact
that Seton’s stories were not generally known until after
the Jungle Books were the talk of the young and old on
two continents. Seton had not compiled his stories in
their present artistic form until 1893. Kipling copyrighted
his first Jungle Book in 1894. Seton had been writing
animal stories for publication in magazines since 1882, and
“ Molly ” and “ Rag” were given to the public in 1888.
Not until recently has Seton seen fit to make any formal
denial of statements which have affirmed that he took his
cue from Kipling. In referring to it he said :
“ There has been nothing very definite about this talk,
and I said nothing until 1 saw a statement in permanent
form. I immediately wrote to the publisher, and in the
next month’s edition of this publication the correction
will be made. The charge was direct, that I copied my
literary style directly after Kipling. That is not very
pleasant for me when my stories antedate Kipling’s byf
years.”
I have read Seton-Thompson’s book (“ Wild
Animals I Have Known”), and, on comparing it
with the Jungle Books, think it just possible that
Kipling may have obtained his inspiration thence-;
since the root idea is the same, although tlie
styles of the two writers are entirely dis-
similar. Kipling’s work is far more elabo-
rate than that of Thompson. In the latter
author’s book one story (“Lobo, the King Sf
Currumpaw”) deals with the career and exploits
of a pack of wolves in New Mexico, and may pos-
sibly have furnished the idea for Kipling’s
chronicles of the Seeonee Pack. The similarity
of the other stories consists in their description,
from the human standpoint, of the internal
economy of animal communities.
But in Kipling’s tales the naturalist’s hand is
not, so much in evidence as in those of Thompson.
The former writes “ from the inside,” as if he had
been born and bred with his beasts (if the expres-
sion be permissible), while the latter gives the im-
pression that he has been a close observer from the
outside. In short, you are in close companion-
ship with Kipling’s animals, and they are talking
to you, but in the other case, Thompson is there
all the time pointing out their habits and
peculiarities with a lecturer’s baton. As literary
matter, the Jungle Books are infinitely superior,
and, as I have said, the only point of similarity is
the root-idea. —A.M.
[Passed only as collateral evidence that there is such a
thing as “ ’Omer’s bloomin’ lyre. ’]
Kipling’s place in literature will not be defi-
nitely fixed till his claim to speak with authority
about Indian life and character —native and Euro-
pean—is settled once for all. His latest produc-
tion, “Kim,” now running as a serial in the
Australian daily press, will serve to fix him.
“ Kim ” is a type, the son of a European loafer—
the mean white in the coloured man’s country./
The action of the story opens at Lahore,)
the capital of tire Panjab, where Kipling
put in most of his Indian life; and
if he doesn’t know his Lahore he know/
nothing. He does know it on the surface, but hi
makes some wonderful mistakes that show he has
not gone beneath. He does not seem 'to knot
that there are relics of the old Sikh aristocrat
round their one-time capital. “Kim” isintßp
duced playing with some native children. Ther/i
is a native policeman close by. Writes Kipling
“Nay, nay,” said the policeman, shaking;hjs heaii, “
do not understand your talk.” The constable (spokt
Punjabi. |
Which means that street arabs in the capital of
the Panjab did not talk the language of the
country. Then there comes on the scene Kipling’s
idea of a Tibetan lama.
“ ‘ Oh, children, what is that big house? ’ he said, in
very fair Urdu.”
Kipling’s spelling is erratic. A little later he
spells it “ Oordoo.” What is meant is the “ Urdu
Zaban” (army tongue), the Court language of the
Mogul Empire, still, under the name of “Hin-
dustani,” commonly used by those natives
who have much to do with Europeans ; and
the latter, finding it in such general use
among those with whom they have dealings,
imagine it is the general vernacular. When
they step out of the track they find out their mis-
take. Where this lama learned his Urdu is not
stated. It is certainly not spoken in Tibet (a
name he would not recognise) or Ladukb, or even
in the Pamir country. In the Pamir the people
mostly talk Persian. The Tibetans call their
country Bod, and themselves Bod-pa.
The lama drifts into the Jjahore Museum, looks
at the Bud hist monumentfs there, and makes
friends with the Curator, it 1 hen he drifts out, and
suddenly adopts Kim as hi® “chela” (so Kipling
calls it) or disciple. Kim drets a mysterious com-
munication to carry from s»m Afglian horse-dealer
to some military person at/Uraballa. He and the
lama travel by rail in a or/nvded compartment in
which there are a “Sikh/artisan” and a “Dogra
soldier.” Kipling report® in wearisome detail the
talk that is supposed to h/ive gone on among them.
The same might be put/ into the mouths of any
set of countay people tra/velling by rail in any part
of the world. ft is /not the local colour in
Kipling’s dialogue, but fthe absence of local colour
that makes him attracti/ v e- When he tries to put
in an effective touch that sort ho generally
blunders. Here is an (Instance:
Dogra, loq. : “ ‘ We of (the Loodhiana Sikhs,’ he rolled
out sonorously, ‘do not Jrouble our heads with doctrine.
We fight.’ /
‘M.v sister’s brother’s I son is naik (corporal) in that
regiment,’ said the Siklfi craftsman quietly, ‘there are
some Dogra companies tfhere.’ The soldier collapsed, for
a Dogra is of lower cast/- than a Sikh.”
The absurdity of Ah is is made plain by the fact
that the Sikh rem/unces all claims to caste dis-
tinction ; and a DcJgra is, or claims to be, a Raj-
put, the highest of all secular castes. “Caste,”
in Kipling’s mou/b, means the same as social
rank, which is noifsense. The Maharajahs, Sitidia
of Gwalior, andl Holkar of Indore, are both
Sudras, shepherd# by caste. A late Judge of the
Calcutta Suprepi® Court, was a basket-maker by
caste —still lower/ Kipling really knows his In-
dia very imperfectly. He calls all religious
mendicants “ fkquirs,” which is an Arabic
word only applicable to a certain class of
Muslim devotees. The dexterous way in which
he throws in weirds not understood by the general
reader, and oftffcn not clearly by Kipling himself,,
gives bis absurfjS stories an appearanceof authority;
but his characters, whether Irish soldiers, bazaar-
women, cartels, cooks, or beggars, are all alike—
you can find just such people in hundreds about
Covent Garden or Ratcliff highway. It is not
his accurate delineation of character, or his
power of creating situations, that makes Kipling
popular, but the universality of his types.
Kipling is l easy to read because he is common- '
place. _ He makes- few calls on the imagination,
and still ffiwer on the intelligence. You can
always understand Ins characters, because you
can always find people exactly like them among
your own acquaintances. The strange outlandish
names are hardly sufficient to disguise them.
Kiplmjpi in presenting the common-
place under rh4 thinnest of disguises.—
J.R.
In Conclusion;
BEjP'OR'E Art, Justice. A man who sweats
jins brain has just as much right to the
f credit ahd\urofit of his labour as a man
| who sweats his body. The man who
steals the work of another's brain is just as con-
temptible a thief as the man who steals the work
of another’s hands.
Whrii one man sow?, and ploughs, and reaps,
the racial instinct affirms it unjust for another to
seize Ins crop—no matter whether his implements
?,re tie product of others’ invention, no matter
whewer tho. theft is turned to beneficent uses.
Theip is strict analogy 1 between the case of the
husbandman, in that respect, and the ease of the
Tie racial instinct of justice declines to believe
that because robbers formerly flourished, and the
fret of the husbandman’s labour was frequently
stolen by others more powerful, therefore robbery
sh/uld be abetted and legalised to-day. No prece-
ded; justifies an essential injustice.
/The writer’s claim to the fruit of his labour is
n't the less valid to-day because Shakespeare stole
3/0 years ago.
/The ideas of Shakespeare’s day were in many
bspects barbarous. In Shakespeare’s day, a
/vriter stole freely. True. And in Shakespeare’s
/lay £1 per head was paid for the discovery of
/witches, who were hanged duly. We have im-
proved upon Shakespeare’s day in both respects.
But barbarous conceptions still linger. Within
the last decade Irish peasants at Clonmel mur-
dered an old woman as a witch. Within the last
month voices from Sydney and Melbourne Uni-
versities have defended Kipling’s plagiary.
Let us stamp out those barbarous conceptions.
Civilised law, based on the racial sense of jus-
tice, now upholds an author’s property in
his original work for 42 years, and defends
him from piracy. There exists an increasing
body of opinion that, where copyright is clear, the
writer’s title to his property should be as per-
manent as a title to any other kind of property.
The fact that Shakespeare and Moliere took
other people’s property where they found it does
not appear to have' had weight with the enactors
of international copyright.
Thus the opinion of international jurists sup-
ports the racial sense of justice. And the influ-
ence of the best modern writers is thrown against
plagiary. It is the man with nothing to lose who
denounces Property. It is Usually the writer with
nothing to lose who denounces property in
writings.
Because of Art, Justice.
The best economical incentive to industry is
this : that a man shall receive the reward of his
labour.
The best incentive to artistic creation is this :
that the artist shall receive the credit and profit
jof his art. .
i In a community of thieves, industry decays. In
k community of plagiarists, Art decays,
j “ Stop thief ! ” Justice cries it. Art echoes it.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Large-Eyed Woman.
There is a large-eyed woman known to me,
Broad-browed and tall and good to look upon ;
A master-mind is hers, and quietly
She takes the world’s mischances, one by one;
A coldness wraps her round like icy air,
And, fixed by her great eyes, one scarce can
note
Her bosom’s amplitude that slopes to where
A velvet collar clasps her velvet throat.
The word of fire, the passion-word that burns
Reserve’s congealment and makes hot the blood,
Is quenched by her; so quietly she spurns
The proffered heart, one doubts her womanhood.
She does not yearn as yearned her kin of old,
Nor dream of new awakenings and dawn,
But stands remote from men—her beauty gold,
Rich gold from love’s bright currency with-
drawn.
Yet, while the world roll round, and till it cease,
Tin’s tale is told, told o’er and o’er again,
Though man may love, or love not, as he please,
A woman needs must love or live in vain.
It has been said that love concerns but two —
The lover and the mistress of his heart—
But whom love maketh wise, he knows ’tis true
That in his passion all the world takes part.
The stars are far aloof, the winds are dumb,
The unsealed heart may never guess their worth ;
A bright face opes the door, and in they come—
The sun, the moon, the lights of heaven and
earth.
The power that makes us Nature’s bosom-friend,
Her second self, daemonic or divine,
Is won in earth-near moments, when we bend
To drink emotion’s lava or its wine.
The sun that takes a red farewell, and dips
Beyond the rim, makes earth our fairyland ;
The trees that see the lovers’ meeting lips
Are privy to the kiss, and understand.
The large-eyed woman may be right to hold
Herself sufficient for her own heart-needs,
But when the noon is passed and youth’s fires
cold,
How shall she greet the night hour that
succeeds ?
Those night-hours with their memories old and
stark,
Their voices of the wind-grey ghosts that roam,
And, worst of all, that picture in the dark—
The empty cradle and the childless home.
N.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.
A Prayer.
“ More light ! ’’—Goethe.
Give me to see, O God, that I
May pierce the mists that round about me lie
And find, if not Infinity,
Something to grasp and hold until I die.
Give me a truth in clear and faultless form,
Free from the scars and dints of priests,
Undimmed by blood, unsullied by the storm
Of Christians wrangling like the beasts.
Write it, O Lord, in stars across the blue
That I, my brothers, all, may read.
Dispel the dogmas dark and drear that You
Must sure have shuddered at. Oh, lead
My struggling soul to know, Lord, and to see.
Give knowledge, God, ’tis all I ask of Thee.
v.
FRANK RADCLIFFE.
An Aspiration.
Music, with the tears in it,
Through my soul is ringing ;
Moods like bodies flame and flit
Through the spirit’s singing :
Dream-birds half-articulate,
Which no charms can capture,
Come by twos and nest and mate
In a moment’s rapture.
Now I seem to be upborne
On a starry pinion
Where the poet’s hope forlorn
Has divine dominion —
Where he sees the clouds of earth
Gather light and cluster,
As babes on the dawn of Birth
Watch the visions muster !
All that thought and feeling share
In a soul’s possession
To my singing seems to bear
A divine confession ;
As within my dreaming brain
Lips of inspiration
Breathe the beauty gone again
On a new creation.
N.S.W.
R. CRAWFORD.
Irrigation is getting on in this country.
For instance, Queensland had 9648 acres of irri-
gated land in 1898, and by 1900 the irrigated area
had declined to 6969 acres.
Bananaland Govt., frightened by Cen-
tralian agitation, withdrew some of the land
(Logan Downs) recently put up for sacrifice.
Huge blocks of the public estate, however, are to
be sold within the next few weeks both in town
and country.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
Westralia and Alaoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
HI, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition of lid. postage, the subscription
to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease off in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage—lid. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of Tiie Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.

whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,

applying to MS.

SATURDAY, JULY 6, 1901.
Federal Life Assurance.
Whbj* the Federal House of Representatives
got as far as clause 43 of the Public Service
Bill—the clause which provides that every
Federal public servant must insure his life —
it naturally struck against the question of
Government v. private life assurance. Any-
body might have foreseen that clause 43
would necessarily raise this question, yet
apparently the Government hadn’t con-
sidered what course to adopt It had no
opinion ready, and nothing to propose.
Hume Cook, of Victoria, moved that there
should be a Federal Life Assurance Depart-
ment, in which every policy would be fully
guaranteed by the Commonwealth Go-
vernment ; and O’Malley, from Tas-
mania, proposed that instead there should
be careful Government audit and supervi-
sion of private offices, those which
could pass through the needle’s eye of a
rigid overhaul should be accepted under the
Act. It is difficult to see why this
should be regarded as an amendment
instead of an addition; a Government
office and Government supervision of
private offices are equally essential. But
the second proposal was apparently as
new to the Government as the first'one,
and all that William Lyne could do was to
promise that the Ministry would retire
within itself, in a manner of speaking, and
give way to deep contemplation. Appar-
ently the only idea up to then
was that it should be compulsory for
every civil servant to,pay his premiums to
some offiqe, good, bad, or awful ; yet that
the Commonwealth should give him no
advice or guidance or information as to the
best place to pay them, nor offer him any
guarantee whatever that his compulsory
policy would be worth its alleged value on
maturity.
This would be, in every respect, an un-
satisfactory arrangement. The Government
tias no right to compel its servants to pay
good money for a document which may, in
the end, prove almost valueless. In the
N.S.W. Public Service new appointees are
now required to insure in some office
approved by the Board, but as the Board
doesn’t audit the accounts of the offices
it approves, and as it approves of prac-
tically every office, the alleged safe-
guard thus afforded is worth about half
of nothing. When the Government compels
its servants to pay for a life policy
they have every right to demand that it shall
be not only the safest one that can be sup-
plied, but also the most economical. That
kind of policy can’t be supplied by any
private office, however well managed. In-
tense and costly competition makes it im-
possible.
* * • »
If the Federal Government founded a
Life Assurance Department of its own, and
compelled all its servants to insure therein,
all the cost of competition would be elimi-
nated. The department would start work
with a large and reliable connection ready
made. Its connection would be a constantly-
growing one, for the number of departments
transferred from the States to the Common-
wealth must increase, and the size of the
departments must increase also with the
growth of the nation. The Federal Life
Office needn’t advertise to any great extent.
It need pay no commission in order to get
business ; its business would be there, ready
to hand. It need pay nothing even for the
collection of premiums ; the Treasury would
collect its premiums by deduction from the
public servants’ salaries. It would require no
palatial offices to make itself known. It
could take what outside business came to it,
and probably a good deal would come in
course of time, for the intelligent man who
assures his life looks for an office which will
be perfectly safe; but, as it could dispense
with outside business, it wouldn’t need to hire
agents and other expensive appliances in
order to get it. It wouldn’t require branch
offices and branch staffs, secretaries and
managers. With the Treasury and all the
sub-Treasuries and the post-offices as its
agents, such things would be quite unneces-
sary. It could invest all its funds, except
those which went in advances on its own
policies, in State and good municipal securi-
ties. These are always obtainable and
always marketable, so it would need to keep
an infinitesimal cash - balance lying idle.
This form of investment would make it un-
necessary to maintain any large staff to look
after mortgages, advances and foreclosed
properties, aud w'ould save these heavy
periodical losses that arise through the
slumping of the property-market, so it is
quite probable that the Federal Life Assur-
ance Department w r ould realise almost as large
a net rate of interest as the average private
office does on much more risky securities.
And its expenses, with no commission to pay
and all these other economies in management,
should be less than 5 per cent, of total in-
come to begin with, with every probability
that they would get down, as the office
expanded, to 2or per cent. The Equit-
able (of England) is a mutual office which
pays no commission, just like the Federal
institution above suggested, and it is man-
aged (according to latest available balance-
sheet —that for 1899) at an expense rate of
3j per cent, on total income. The A.M.P.’s
rate is over 9 per cent. The average
expenses of the 12 existing large Australian
offices are 13j per cent, on total income ; if
all Australian offices were included it would
be rather more. And the proposed Federal
institution would have a huge advantage over
even the Equitable, for it wouldn’t require
the branches or the advertising that is neces-
sary to that office, and it would start with a
large and certain business which the Equit-
able didn’t. The Federal office, under its
novel conditions, would almost certainly be
able to pay larger bonuses than the best
existing life office, and give better security
—the security of all Australia—as well.
And the public servant, as a person with
whom life assurance is compulsory, has a
right to demand the best and cheapest kind
obtainable.
* * * *
These advantages can only be obtained
if he is compelled to insure in one specified
office. If he is simply compelled to insure
in some office, he will fall a prey, as usual, to
the horde of rival assurance agents ; their
commissions and expenses will come out of
his premium as usual ; the expense rate will
remain practically the same, and all the
economies which might be made to attach to
the compulsory system will be lost. Of
course, life assurance managers and agents
and experts will state, out of their years of
accumulated experience, that these theories
are incapable of being reduced to practice,
and. will give their unanimous opinion that
the idea of a Federal Life Assurance
Department is unworkable. Almost all the
available experts are engaged in private life
assurance, and can’t be expected to have any
other opinion of a scheme which will deprive
them of thousands of possible customers, and
set up a rival institution as well. Even that
usually stern democrat, O’Malley of the
Hat, as a man who has long been making a
good and honest living out of life assurance
commissions, can hardly take a perfectly
unbiassed view of a system which, however
good it may be for the assured, will pay no
commission to any persuasive individual in
a large hat. The only expert who can be
expected, to view the idea with general
favor is the one who sees a pros-
pect of being appointed to manage the
new office, and as he will also be an
interested party his approval will count for
just about as much as the other people s
condemnation. The question is one which
must be looked at in the light of arithmetic
and common-sense. The example of Maori-
land proves that a State Life Assurance
Department can be made a great success.
That of Queensland shows that a State note-
issue has none of the horrors attached it
which the banking “ expert ” foretold. The
State Banks of Sweden, Norway, Denmark,
&c., are evidences that State banking isn t
the wild impossibility the private banking
authority is apt to declare it. These are till
matters which the Federal Parliament will
have to consider, and they are all matters
with regard to which it will receive much
valuable misinformation from interested
persons of high commercial standing.
The Insolence of Self-Appointed
Persons.
“.I wish to warn the electors against the Independent
candidate. There is no room in Parliament for the inde-
pendent.”—One-time Labor-member Watson, M.U.R.
“ If on the high seas a British man-of-war meets a ship
which refuses to show its colors, the conclusion which
would he drawn would be that the ship in question is
engaged in some ambiguous design. In the same way the
candidate who poses as an independent, and refuses to
range himself under any banner, will generally be found
to be embarked on the enterprise of extracting funds
from the Treasury for roads and bridges for his con-
stituents.” — G. E. Rich, in S.M. Herald.
The above are fair samples of an immense body of
literature lately issued in the interests of Machine
Politics, and for the support of Machine Politics,
and the discouragement of the art of think-
ing for oneself. For many weeks past two
unspeakably insolent gangs of Self-Appointed
Persons have grabbed New South Wales by the
throat, and told it that if it dared to have any
opinion of its own apart from the opinion of See
or Lee, or if it dared to think for itself apart from
the thoughts of See or Lee, it was a traitor, a
blackguard, a pirate, an underhand person, and a
disgrace generally. These two gangs of Self-
Appointed Persons have formed themselves
into committees to which the people cer-
tainly never appointed them—and lectured the
community furiously about how it was its duty to
be wholly and completely devoted to See— or else
to Lee. They and their press organs formed tvyo
rival camps of ridiculous political bosses. One of
these held that the fifth-rate mediocrity, See,
never did anything wrong, and all his public
actions were quite defensible, while Lee never did
anything right. The other held that the tenth-
rate mediocrity, Lee, always had shown himself,
and always would show himself, a perfect legis-
lator, while every political action of See was only
another proof of his utter incapacity.
* * » *
And these two gatherings of Self-Appointed
Persons laid this alternative before New South
Wales: “ Here fire two dismally-commonplace
men. You didn’t appoint one of them to be pre-
sent Premier or the other to be Premier-elect.
One man took the billet of Premier because
another man dropped it and went away. The
other took the billet of Leader of the Opposition,
which carries with it, some day or other, the
inheritance of the Premiership, because another
man dropped it and went away. But, though you
didn’t select either of them to his high office, we,
the crowd of Self-Appointed Persons who are
running the political machine, demand that you
regard one or other of these dismally-common-
place men as a heaven-sent genius, and the
other as the last possibility in the way of a
fool—that you approve of everything one does
and disapprove of everything the other does—that
you get into a drivelling state of ecstasy and
superlatives about them, and ask for nothing
better than Lee— or else See. If you recognise
that they are two very ordinary men and that
there is little to choose between them, you are a
time-server and a hypocrite. If you think that
neither of them has any of the qualifications for a
leader, you are a ruffian. If you hold that it is
advisable to send men into Parliament who are
not pledged to blindly support every disastrous
project of one or other of these most incapable in-
dividuals, then there are no words strong enough
to express the just contempt of the committees of
Self-Appointed Persons. Lee represents the old
Reid crowd, which had visible deficits averaging
£196,838 per annum, and which had somewhere
between £200,000 and £500,000 a year of further
deficits, which it disguised by shamefully cooking
the accounts. See represents the Lyne crowd,
which had deficits for the past two years that look
like a good average of £368,000 a year, and which
also cooked the accounts. You have got to believe
that one of these parties never had, and never
could have, any serious deficit, and was quite in-
capable of cooking the accounts —you have got to
believe this despite the evidence of the Auditor-
General’s reports or any other testimony. If you
don’t, you are a disgrace to this great country.
Lee represents the crowd which, in its last year
of office, in addition to its other deficits and fakes,
used nearly £BOO,OOO of loan-money as revenue;
and See represents the other crowd which did the
same. If you want honest finance, and refuse to
support either of these spendthrifts, your iniquity
calls for the loathing of every good Machine Poli-
tician, and the utter contempt of the Gang of
Self-Appointed Persons who pull the party wires
will be rightfully poured on your head.”
• ♦ * •
The figure of speech about the suspicious char-
acter of the vessel, that is met with on the high
seas and won’t show its flag, has been worn thread-
bare. Also, it was a profoundly foolish metaphor
from the first. The Machine Politician starts
by assuming, on the evidence of his own unsup-
ported say-so, that there are only two possible
flags. He insists on his right to stop every vessel
on the political high seas and demand : “ Are you
Dutch or Greek? We have decided that you
must be one or the other. Show your flag as an
honest vessel should, and let us know which you
are.” And if the vessel is British, French, Ger-
man or American, and won’t show either of the
flags between which the committee of Self-
Appointed Persons declares that every vessel
must choose, the latter crowd goes away cursing
and says, “ This vessel is obviously a pirate with
bad intentions.” The Machine Politician claims
the right, conferred on himself by himself, to bail
up every citizen and truculently demand if he is a
Shaker or a Mormon, and to call him a truckler
and a time-server if—being neither of them—he
won’t say which of them he is. This is all very
amusing. It is also very pitiful.
* * » »
The existing Parliamentary system is built on
Machine Politics. It is based on the under-
standing that there are to be two parties in
Parliament, one of which will endorse everything
that See does, however foolish, shameful, or in-
jurious ; while the other will endorse everything
Lee does, even if it is worse than the things See
does. And if Lee tries to do something really
good it is the duty of the See party, in a general
way, to say it is bad; and if See tries to be of im-
mense benefit to the country it is the duty of the
Lee party, except in very exceptional cases, to be
convinced that his conduct is awful. In N.S.W.,
the fiscal fight has, for many years, made the
dividing line between parties unusually sharp,
and the slavish party feeling unusually strong.
The visible result isthatN.S.W. has the biggest
total debt, the most regular annual deficit, and the
largest accumulated deficit in the Commonwealth.
During the last dozen years or so it was hardly
worth trying to be honest. The man in power
knew that his party and his papers would swear
the finances were in excellent order however bad
they were, while the other party and the other
Eapers would swear they were utterly bankrupt
owever good they were. The man with most
papers, or the most unscrupulous papers, on his
side, was almost certain to win, therefore Dibbs,
the most careful Treasurer N.S.W. had for the
last fifteen years, is still held up—mainly by
reason of the loathsome Telegraph and Herald
—as the boss deficit-monger, and Reid, the very
worst one, is boomed as a kind of monetary arch-
angel. When the Lee crowd was in power
its majority refused to do anything to _ put
things right because it was its business
to swear, against all truth and evidence,
that things couldn't be wrong with the Lee crowd
in power. When the See crowd was in power,
its crowd swore the same way, with the same
result. The papers which supported each party
swore the same way also. See’s crowd or Lee’s
crowd couldn’t reject its leader’s financial schemes
and tell him that, though supporting him on other
matters and regarding him as the best man for
the office of Premier, it insisted on this matter
being put straight. As Parliamentary etiquette
goes, to admit that anything wanted to be put
straight, meant throwing out the Ministry and
Eutting the other crowd in office. That would
ave been an unpardonable breach of party
allegiance. It would have burst up Machine
Politics. The thing to do was to let the country
go from bad to worse, and manfully deny that it
was doing so. The party politician must either
be a traitor to his party, or a liar for his party.
♦ * * *
One way to begin putting things right is to
make the Premier an officer elected by the As-
sembly. At present things go something like
this. The Opposition loses its leader from some
cause or other, and has to elect a successor. It
may be a bad time with the Opposition, when some
of the leading men on its end of the political
machine are not in the House. It numbers, per-
haps, one-third of the Assembly. The new leader
—possibly the best man, or possibly only the fifth
best man, available —is elected by, say, a two-
thirds majority of the party. That means that he
gets 28 votes in a Rouse of 125 members. Then
it straightway becomes the business of the party,
and the papers which support it, to create a bogus
halo of intelligence round him—to prove that he
is the country’s greatest political genius—to show
that everything he does is right. To doubt or
criticise him becomes treason to the party and a
desertion of the party’s flag. The next election,
possibly, gives his party a majority. Say it
has 70 votes. Some of these who elected the
leader may have died or dropped out, and he
may hold the leadership of a party of 70 because
20 of them voted for him years previously, and he
Premier in a House of 125 by reason of these same
20 votes. The Machine System keeps him in
office, which is a ridiculous travesty on Govern-
ment by the People. In addition to making the
Premier an officer elected by the whole House at
the beginning of a new Parliament, it is necessary
to break up the absurd fiction by which the
Government is supposed to “ possess the full con-
fidence of the House.” Nobody in this world
possesses the full confidence of anybody without
somebody getting left. It is necessary to have it
understood that the House does not regard the
Government as infallible; that Ministers are not
expected to be gods or archangels at £l5OO a year;
that the House can amend or reject Government
measures to any extent without thereby declaring
it has lost confidence in the Ministry, unless it
declares its loss of confidence by a direct vote.
In short, it should be understood that the House
keeps the Ministry in office because it regards it
as a trifle better than any other possible
Ministry just then, and has just as much con-
fidence in it as in any other carefully-supervised
and audited fellow-creature.
* * • *
Further, there is badly wanted a system of
second and third ballots, or some other device
which will make it quite sure that no one who
doesn’t really represent a majority of the votes
polled will get into the House. Then there will
be a fair chance for the independent member. Ila
is the outward sign of a blessed, though it
may be only temporary, revolt against the
blatant insolence of Self-Appointed Persons.
Self-Appointed Persons supply the electorates
with candidates. They turn the handle of
the great cursing - machine which impresses
on the people the utter sinfulness of not
supporting these candidates. These candidates
are pledged to approve blindly of every-
thing that See or Lee does, and of everything
done by anybody on whom See or Lee may
confer a portfolio. The public is ordered to
vote blindly for the man who is going to approve
blindly of what See does, or of what Lee does.
The association of Self-Appointed Persons creates
See and Lee by a process of selection over which
the people have no direct, and very little indirect,
control. The Self-Appointed Persons issue their
ukase that the whole community must divide
itself into two sections—the one that approves
of See, and the one that approves of Lee.
The independent candidate is a protest against
this silly and sheep-like superstition. He
states his principles, or what he thinks are
his principles, and undertakes to go by
them, instead of being anybody’s blind idola-
ter, and he invites the public to do likewise.
And the rage of Self-Appointed Persons at the
uprising of certain people who don’t think that
the sun rises out of Lee’s hat and sets in See’s
tail-pocket is something tremendous tc look upon.
The Scapegoat of Hysteria.
Vic. Assembly’s expulsion of Labor-member
Findley was a typical example of mob law and a
lynching. The essence of mob law is that it dis-
penses with trial and anticipates justice. Premier
Peacock was so eager to prove liis “loyalty”
that he desired the Assembly to expel Findley on
his ex parte asseverations, in ignorance of the
facts; and the Assembly nearly did it. The
hysteria, the sheepishness, —all the concomitants
of mob law were present. And Findley, because
he was only an obscure Labor-member, was
lynched.


To any such body as Yio. Assembly must be
conoeded the right to expel any displeasing mem-
ber. But as a representative and public body
Vie. Assembly has to justify its employment of its
right. A Vic. Govt., and Vic. Assembly,
are not established to exercise judicial func-
tions ; they are not fitted to exercise
them. There is too much usurpation
of judicial functions by Australian Govt,
executives. Either Findley was guilty of an
offence at law, or he was not. If he was guilty,
his offence should have been legally proved before
he was punished. If he was not guilty, Vic.
Assembly was wrong to expel him. Even a Jingo
Ministry should not over-ride the law.
* * * *
The facts may be briefly stated. On 11th May
a newspaper, the Irish People, published a vio-
lent and abusive article against the British King.
There was little new in it; most of the statements
have appeared hundreds of times already ; some
of them seem to be essentially true. The article
was in no way obscene, as alleged. It was coarse
and unjustified ; there was some reason for its
publication, none for its tone. King Edward lias
probably used his opportunities, yielded to his
temptations, as most other men would have done
in his place. On the whole, his character seems
to The Bulletin better than that of any British
King for several centuries past ; though the
truth about Britisli Kings usually filters out
only when they are dead.
* * * *
It seems to The Bulletin that the Tocsin had
no right to appeal to the liberty of the press in
order to reprint an indefensible article. King
Edward, as a public person, is subject to public
criticism in the public interest ; and if his private
life were publicly and notoriously scandalous, in
the worst sense, then criticism of his private life
would be justified as a matter of public morals.
The Irish People did not produce any evidence
that King Edward is any worse than the average
man. It merely said abusive things, more or less
unwarranted by rumor.
* • * *
Then the British Govt, suppressed the Irish
People— a quite unwarrantable act. No Govern-
ment, in a free country, has a right to make itself
judge and jury, to supplant common justice, and
to supersede the ordinary process of law. That
is an offence against popular liberty much greater
than any possible offence which could be caused
by an alleged libel on the British monarch. It is
the thin end of the wedge of Tyranny, and
should be protested against by every man who
values his freedom. The British monarch is under
the law, or should be, just as much as the humblest
citizen. The British Govt, should have been the
last to bring the law into disrepute.
• * * *
Similarly with The Tocsin. If The Tocsin
broke the law, there was the law to punish it. If
in the opinion of Vic. Govt, and Vic. Assembly
the law was insufficient to punish it, then the law
could be amended. It is much better that 15,000
British monarchs should be the subject of alleged
libel than that popular liberty should be infringed
by one iota. And when Vic. Assembly usurps
the place of the Vic. judiciary, popular liberty is
attacked by those who should be first to uphold it.
* * * *
“ Alleged libel ’’—for, after all the fuss, that is
what it is. Until the matter has been decided by
a court of justice, it is impossible to reach that
finality of opinion which stands for judgment.
Premier Peacock, the Argus, and their cohort
have supplied, in the place of judgment, ideas,
prejudices, hysterics. And Labor-member Find-
ley is the scapegoat of hysteria.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Jingo Sacrifice.
Victoria's Legislative Assembly has once again
—as when in the boom days it got upon its hind
legs and roared “ God Save the Queen ” for the
space of five minutes —demonstrated its exceeding
loyalty to the Throne and Person, said demonstra-
tion being, as usual, at the expense of the other
fellow. The other fellow this time was Findley,
M.L.A., who—as nominal publisher of the Labor
paper, The Tocsin —was responsible for the
appearance of an article protesting against the
suppression of the Irish People, and quoting
therefrom the libellous article on Edward VII.
The Ar&US had persistently sooled on Premier
Peacock to violent action against Findley, and
the spineless Premier, having first cabled to Cham-
berlain and received his blessing, went ahead
and invited Findley to apologise. Findley
stated that—though nominal publisher—he had no
control over what went into the paper, that he
had neither written the article nor even read it
until the Argus started squealing, that he had
promptly stopped the sale of the paper and in-
tended to sever his connection therewith. Also,
he pointed out that The Tocsin made no charges
against the King, but had actually condemned
those made by the Irish People as mere abuse;
also, that the Argus itself, in its London
letters, had repeated some of the libels
without any word of condemnation. But
•this wasn’t enough. Peacock thought there
should be an unqualified disavowal and
disapproval of the sentiments, and Findley re-
plied, “ I regret that the article has given offence
to this House, and that it appeared, . . . Since,
in the opinion of the Cabinet and the House, it is
an article that should not have appeared, I am
sorry that it was published.” But Peacock broke
in that that was not sufficient, while Irvine, for
the Opposition, opined that no apology, however
abject, could be accepted. Once more Findley
addressed the House : “ I repeat, in all
sincerity, that I have no sympathy with
the article in question; I regret that it did
appear, and having taken the oath of allegiance to
the King, 1 say that oath was taken by me in all
sincerity.” He then retired, and while brave
Jack Murray, Sangster, Billson, and others
fought on his behalf the stolid Tories and the
Liberal Jingoes turned their faces to the floor, and
shut their ears till the vote was taken. Findley
was expelled by 04 votes to 17. The Bulletin
trusts that the Vic. Jingoes are proud of their
work; but, while they are still pluming themselves
on their loyalty, it would like to point out one or
two things. Nobody suggested that, as the
paper—of which Findley was merely nominal
publisher—was the offender, its owners should
be punished by a stiff fine. Nobody suggested that
The Tocsin should be suppressed. Nobody hinted
that the writer of the article should be made to
face the music. Nobody wrung from Peacock
the contents of the cable he sent to Joseph the
Screw-maker, or his reply. Murray- tried hard,
but the kookaburra was as close as “a sealed
oyster.” Wherefore The Bulletin concludes
that it wasn’t the Tocsin, or that paper’s editor,
or the person really responsible for the re-publica-
tua cf the alleged libel that the Jingoes were
after, out Findley -the democratic represen-
tative of Melbourne City—and they want to
have nim at any cost. If the Tocsin had libelled
King Edward, surely the Victorian Law was
strong enough and wide enough to have reaohed
the libeller. As it is, the Tocsin proceeds on its
course while Findley is sacrificed—an offering to
the self-esteem, the alleged dignity, of Vie. As-
sembly—-a House that has tolerated unspeakable
persons in its chair ; that was bossed by boomster
Jimmy Munro and by Patterson; that lost its
mace and sent the detectives hunting round
bawdy-houses to find it. The Dignity of the Vic-
torian Assembly ! !

More Federal Capital.
Senator Smith, of Westralia, last week moved
that the Federal Government should take over
1000 square miles of land to form a Federal Terri-
tory and capital, and be the sole and permanent
landlord of that area, so that the increased land
values created by the building of a new city with
the people’s money may remain the people’s
property, and not spread over the Federal border
to be grabbed by the private landlord outside.
For such a purpose 1000 square miles is an ab-
surdly inadequate area, though it is far better
than the miserable minimum of 100 square miles
mentioned in the Constitution Act. But even
this very moderate proposal aggravated the land-
syndicate gang and their sympathisers to great
bitterness, and it proceeded to demonstrate the
impossibility of the idea on the ground of expense.
Sydney Telegraph declared the proposal was
“ quixotic.” And Senator Jawbone Neild :
Pointed out that they could not find a thousand miles
of suitable territory in New South Wales for Federal
Capital purposes that would not involve the resumption
of so large an area of private property, which no member
and no self-respecting Government would be likely to look
at. (Cheers.)
Well, supposing the Federal Government, instead
of resuming 1000, resumed 5000 square miles.
If the site was in the Monaro district that area
might include the great and rising towns of
Cooma, Bombala, Delegate, Nimitybelle, Bibben-
luke and Buckley’s Crossing. Cooma and Bom-
bala together are valued, lock, stock and barrel, at
£200,800 (Cooma £218,100; Bombala £72,700).
This is the municipal valuation. The other places
have no municipal valuation, but they, and a few
other hamlets, would about make up another
Bombala. They might run up the valuation of
the town property to £370,000. If Eden, Bega, Can-
delo, Cathcart and Wyndham were thrown in the
town property would be worth less than a million.
Much of this proposed area of 5000 square miles—
the mountain part of it—is Crown land. But if
the whole of it was private land at an average
value of £2 an acre it wouldn’t cost over six
millions—unless the Government allowed itself to
be shamefully swindled in the process of re-
purchase. By adopting the Canadian banking
law, under which the banks are required to
hold at least 40 per cent, of their cash re-
serves in State notes, and by making the note
issue a Government monopoly, as in Queensland,
the Commonwealth could get the perpetual loan,
without interest, of twice that sum. And when it
built its Federal city on this bare land, the area
which is now worth £6,000,000 would soon be
worth £12,000,000. The theory of the Neild crowd
is that no self-respecting Government would make
a profit of £6,000,000. The theory of the Tele-
graph is that for the Commonwealth to make that
profit, instead of leaving it to private owners,
would be “quixotic.” The theory of the private
steal crowd in general is that, though private
landlords can afford to own this property, and
can get a decent return out of it without the ad-
vantage of any new city in their vicinity to make
a big increase in values, the Commonwealth can’t
afford to own it even with that advantage ! The
Commonwealth can’t afford to make money, in
fact, lest it shall be ruined. The very large
item of expense involved in the creation of
a Federal city will be the same whether the
Commonwealth takes over 100 square miles or
5000. The only difference will be that in the
latter case all the increased rentals created around
the city—created by the expenditure of the Com-
monwealth’s money—will go to the Common-
wealth, and help to pay for the city; in the
former case they won’t/ And the private-steal
crowd laments that, if the Commonwealth gets
these increased rentals, it will go broke. The
transaction which The Bulletin proposes—the
absolute resumption of 5000 square miles—is no
heavier a contract, in proportion to the resources
of the Commonwealth, than the Sydney city re-
sumptions were, in proportion to the resources of
N.S.W. And, if these latter resumptions are
quixotic and ruinous, and all that kind of thing,
the Sydney Telegraph’s big boss, Carey,
shouldn’t countenance them by taking a salary as
a member of the Resumption Board.

The Rage of Victoria’s 48 Rich
Landlords.
The Peacock Government met Victoria’s State
Parliament with a long programme of reforms,
but the one which obscured all others was the
proposal that a Convention should be elected by
the people to draft a new State Constitution.
Since then, the anger of the House of 48 Rich
Landlords has left a streak of sulphur over the
land. The House of Landlords has a creed which.
goes into three clauses, and these are the three :
(1) The Legislative Council is a perfectly satisfactory
body, with which the country is quite content.
(2) Any proposal to let the country have a chance to
freely express its contentment, and to sav clearly and
distinctly that it.wouldn’t be without its 48 Rich Land-
lords on any account, and to thank them cordially and
vote for their indefinite continuance, is a sin, a curse, an
outrage, a blot on the Constitution, and an invention of
the Devil.
(3) The reason why it isn’t necessary to let the people
have a chance to say how thoroughly satisfied they are
with the Upper House is simply that they are satisfied.
For the Upper House to say that the people are satisfied
is all right. To give the people a chance to say it them-
selves would be a disgrace, and would wreck the country
in six places
The Council’s special organ, Melbourne Argus, is
specially violent on these points. That heavy
mass of literature sets forth the position thus :
The Council exists for and represents all classes, ail in-
terests. If it be, in any sense, the special guardian of
the interests of property it is so only so far as these
specially concern the prosperity of the whole community.
... It has done its work so well that its constituents
have been generally satisfied.
And yet Peacock only proposes to let all classes
have a chance, in drafting a new Constitution, to
express their thorough admiration of the Council,
and to endorse the Argus’s assertion that they
are generally satisfied with it. And the Argus
is the maddest paper in Australia at the idea of
being allowed to put its assertions to the proof.
Yet even the Council has ideas about reform—or
what it calls reform, and it is faintly proposed
in its Halls of Sleep that the franchise should be so
widened as to give practically every male citizen
a vote at Council elections. This is the sort of
joke that the Council revels in. At present only
landowners, and only landowners on a very large
scale, are eligible to sit in the Council. There are
130,000 electors, mostly democrats, but they are
allowed to choose their M’s.L.C. from among a
small class of about 2000 —almost all bitter Tories.
And the Council proposes, by way of reform, to
let about 260,000 electors select their M’s.L.C.
from among the same 2000 bitter Tories. The
situation is about the same as if, instead of 130,000
Orangemen being allowed to decide which R.C.
priest shall represent them, and assure the coun-
try that they are all good Catholics who go to
mass regularly, the right of selection shall be ex-
tended to 260,000 Orangemen, other things re-
maining the same. If the House of Landlords
has a vestige of faith in its boasts of its own
popularity, even among its present constituents,
there is an easy way to prove it. Let it leave the
franchise as it is, and simply make every
one. of the existing 130,000 electors eligible to
sit in the Chamber which is said to represent
them instead of having 128,000 of them dis-
qualified as. at present. Let it represent even
I roperty fairly, by giving its propertied cousti-
™eri£S a * ree , f° choose their own members.
Ihe fact that it dare not do this is good evidence
that it is the best-hated gang of bigots in Aus-
tralia, and that it is aware of the painful fact.
The Adelaide Tramway Trouble.
For years Adelaide Advertiser, the daily news-
paper owned by Langdon Bonython, an
ambitious man with a small body and a big hat,
has been considered a democratic organ. It gained
this reputation mainly because Kingston adopted
it as his mouthpiece, and also because Bonython,
at best a man of only ordinary intelligence,
whooped for Kingston, apparently as a means of
advancing himself. The Tory crowd has again
and again alleged that most of the democratic
leaders in the Advertiser were written bv
Kingston. But Bonython’s knighthood came
along, and then the seat in the House of Repre-
sentatives, and now the democratic zeal of the
self-ADVERTiSER seems to have cooled. It recently
came out as an advocate of the Snow tramway
grab, a scheme which Kingston has again and
again denounced. This is the sort of thing the
Advertiser publishes nowadays (leader, June 1):
The nationalisation of the tramways is certainly a
chimera. Even those in authority who have given the
scheme some countenance as an alternative to munici-
palisation in the event of the local bodies failing to
agree, were careful not to hold out any strong hope that
Parliament would adopt their proposal; and this cautious
tone expresses a just sense of the difficulties in the way.
Tramways, if a public, are so plainly a municipal enter-
prise that there is no real chance of inducing a majority
of Parliament to pledge the public credit on behalf of a
costly scheme of improvement. Parliament, while will-
ing to facilitate a Greater Adelaide, will assuredly object
to substitute State for municipal action in a matter of
purely municipal importance ; but even were there the
desire to follow a different and less reasonable policy
than this, no Parliamentary action can be taken which
would provide electric tramways for Adelaide and its
suburbs within a reasonable period. Nearly five years
must elapse before the tramways could be taken over
unless there were a quite unthinkable cutting of the
statutory contract with the companies. For these
reasons nationalisation must be dismissed as iin practic-
able, and assuming that immediate municipalisation can-
not be agreed upon, the only alternative to private enter-
prise with provision for ultimate municipalisation on just
conditions is the indefinite continuance of the present
obsolete and unsatisfactory system.
The Advertiser is, therefore, with the Tory
Register, now an advocate of the Snow Monopoly
Bill, under which the community will hand over
its street tramway rights -to Snow’s London
syndicate for 40 years—an unspeakable scheme,
which even the Corporation of Adelaide, once
favorable, now declines to support ! And the
Advertiser’s alleged reasons are: (1) That
State-owned tramways are impossible, though
they exist in Sydney, where the fares are the
lowest in Australasia, and where they return 4J
per cent, a rate which pays all the interest on
borrowed money and leaves a handsome surplus ;
(2) That, though Snow’s syndicate can buy up the
tramways without waiting till the present conces-
sions expire, it is quite impossible for the State to
do so; (3) That, therefore, the State would have
to wait nearly five years (that’s all) till the present
private companies’ concessions expire. And,
having thus defined the largest period that it
could have to wait as five years, the Advertiser
yells about “an indefinite continuance of the
present obsolete and unsatisfactory system
—i.e., the unsatisfactory and obsolete system
created by private ownership the policy
which the Advertiser wants to perpetuate for
another 40 years. Judging by the experience of
Sydney, if S.A. Government took over the Ade-
laide tramways and created a new, up-to-date
system, they would not only pay interest on cost,
but yield a handsome profit to help cover the
deficits on other public works, and thus relieve
taxation. But though the Tory press can always
see hundreds of good reasons why unpayable
public works should be left to the State, its anger
when the State threatens to undertake one that is
certain to be highly payable, instead of leaving it
to a syndicate, is like unto the rage of 18,000
hungry lions.

Concerning' Coupons.
Premier Peacock’s most recent nightmare is said
to take the form of huge placards whereon is writ
in every color known to the advertising fiend—
“ Trade where you see this Sign, Collect,” etc.
And it is all brought about by deputations and
counter-deputations to the Vic. Premier concern-
ing the “ coupons-for-cash ” trouble. Some Ben-
digo shopkeepers who started a coupon co. in
opposition to the original co. begged him to legis-
late against their venture and all such ven-
tures. This though their coupon business was
paying good dividends. It seems they have dis-
covered that their opposition has had no apparent
effect on the original parasite beyond making it more
lively than it used to be, and that they are only
spreading the plague to the discomfiture of the
host—the general body of shopkeepers. Then a
deputation of housewives swarmed into the Pea-
cock’s den and vociferously demanded that there
should be no legislative interference with coupon
companies whatever; that the system was a boon
to the consumers, &c., &c., &c. Next the original
Coupon Co.’s managers asked Peacock
whether, in the event of the Govt, taking over
the business of discount stamps, he was
prepared to compensate them for disturbance.
On the whole, the Premier has been having a
violent time, and doesn’t know rightly.
where he are or what to do. Though Maori-
land and Tasmania have legislated against
private coupon companies, the circumstances
in Victoria are vastly different. In the
former provinces the big merchants carried the
heaviest legislative artillery and the coupon co.s
were mostly concerns of one-man power. In
Victoria the Coupon Co. is a limited
liability concern of large calibre that looks
too formidable to be shoo’ed off the premises
with a broom. Peacock can’t even make the
“monopoly” argument an excuse for abolish-
ing the coupon folks’ little green sticker;
the success of the opposition coupon ven-
ture in Bendigo having demonstrated clearly
that there is no monopoly whatever in the
trade. It so happens that there is no business
in the world more susceptible to competition than
the same coupon trade, but the opposition to the
established company needs to be conducted by
somebody who understands the game. As far as
Melbourne is oonoerned, the Vio. Premier maj
with safety defer legislation against coupons, if
it is true that another company is on tne tapis
in that city, with ex-Tasmanian coupon experts
in charge. Should the new co. materialise it is
a pretty safe bet that all coupons will be waste
paper in twelve months. If Premier Peacock
could find some way of encouraging the new
company without compromising his Cabinet, it
would probably be the easiest way out of the
present nightmare, and his smile would split the
welkin as of yore.

A Case for Inquiry.
“Frank Croson” writes about an old and sus-
picious ease :
A band, small but persistent, still tackles the thankless
task of trying: to release Mrs. Cecile Andersen from the
gfaol where she has been lying for nearly seven years,
the latest move takes the form of a petition asking an
inquiry into the evidence and a reconsideration of the
verdict and the evidence now available fully justifies
said request. The evidence given at the trial in ’94
showed that the room in which Cecile Andersen and
John Fraser talked for some hours before the shooting
was well-lit and open to the inquiring glance of every
passer-by ; that two shots were heard almost simultane-
ously ; that those immediately on the scene found two
bodies lying some feet apart ; that Mrs. Andersen’s cape
la.v over, and entirely covering, the right side of her head
(where the bullet entered) ; that there were no holes in
said cape ; that her wound was such as to cause instant
insensibility (Dr. Ohas. Ryan’s evidence); that Fraser
was shot on the left side of the head, behind the ear, the
course of the bullet showing he must have been shot at a
distance of Sin. from above and behind (Dr. 0. H. Mollison’s
evidence); that Mrs. Anderson was 6ft. in height, while
Fraser stood 6ft. 3|in. It was also sworn by Fraser’s
relatives that he was right-handed. Against this latter
assertion those who agitate for an inquiry hold sworn
statements from reputable citizens, who lived in Mrs.
Andersen’s boarding-house along with Fraser, for years,
that he was left-handed and habitually— among other
things—carved the meat at table with the left hand.
Lately the woman’s friends prepared a petition, bearing
1200 signatures, for presentation to the Duke, but, of
course, such a thing as a possible miscarriage of justice
could not be permitted to be suggested to a Royal Per-
sonage, so petitioners were carefully frozen out. This
petition set forth that Mrs. Andersen had now served 6 \
years and had always been an admirable woman of most
benevolent disposition, etc. All which is quite true
but has little to do with the case. The point is that on
the evidence given at the trial there is grave cause to
suspect that Mrs. Andersen was wrongly convicted. The
assumption of her guilt on that evidence demands thatshe,
a woman of sft., got on a chair or couch and shot down-
wards at the 6ft. 3in. man (what was he doing ?), that she
then shot herself, causing, according to expert medical
evidence, instant insensibility which lasted for many
hours, that she then placed the revolver at Fraser’s feet,
walked back to the other end of the couch and put her
cape over her head—all in a state of unconsciousness. A
likely yarn ! Had two dead bodies been found in that
room any coroner’s jury would have promptly verdicted
murder and suicide against the six-foot man, but, there
being a nice, soft, live neck to string a noose round, the
law promptly went for that neck with both hands, and
was much annoyed when it slipped through its grasp—
if the attitude of the Crown Law- Department be any
criterion. The C.L. Department takes the view (as it
always does) that the verdict was correct; and, urged on
by the Department, Solicitor-General Henry Cutiibert
had the face to tell a deputation in ’99 that “if
they could obtain the co-operation of the Fraser family
the matter might be arranged,” which was almost as
brutal and illogical a reply as that of his successor. Agar
Wynne, that the failure of juries to convict Mrs. Fraser
and Mrs. Wright made it impossible to consider whether
Mrs. Anderson was wrongly convicted or not. It is a
choice commentary on the relative values placed by Aus-
tralian law-makers on life and property that an aggrieved
defendant in an equity suit (above a certain value) may
appeal to the Full Court and to the Privy Council, thus
getting the advantage of having all evidence sifted and
weighed three times over ; but the defendant in a suit
where the plaintiff demands, not his property but his
neck or his liberty, has but the one chance—and a mighty
poor and perfunctory one at that.
