The Bishop and the Prostitute.
Never has the press of London (writes The Bul-
letin’s London correspondent) so thoroughly
justified its claim to the title of the Daily Sup-
press—when prominent personages are concerned
—as it did over an extraordinary occurrence
at a recent public meeting to consider public
morality and the remedy. Dr. Winnington
Ingram, the new Bishop of London, presided,
and among those by his side were the R.C. Bishop
of Southwark, Sir Edward Clarke, and Mr. H.
H. Asquith. All was conventional until London’s
Bishop uprose, evidently in a condition of high
nervous tension, and, without any introduction,
abruptly started off:
I was in my brougham, driving along Piccadilly, this
evening. Now and then, I looked through the carriage
windows, and suddenly my eyes seemed to be opened
and I saw things as they really are. At first, I covered
my eyes with my hands to shut out the awful sight, and
then in the darkness the question came to me : What is
to be done? What is to be done? A little later, the
question came in another way : What would the Master
do ? and with the question a great light seemed to fill the
place, and I saw my duty sun-clear, as Saul saw his on
the way to Damascus. I stopped the carriage, got out
and, after a few minutes’ conversation, persuaded two of
the public women to come into the brougham with me.
If Jesus was right, these two unfortunates were my sis-
ters, and the only hope of their purification was in my
affection and tenderness. I took them to Fulham Palace
and they are going to stay with me. I hope to fill every
room in the Palace with such sisters as these.
At this point Sir Edward Clarke fled, much
upset, but the speaker, oblivious to all, con-
tinued :
Oh ! sisters and brothers, in this act of mine I find the
solution of all my difficulties. I didn’t know what
broughams were made for, but now I know. Thev are
made to carry the weak, the sick, and the aged ; they
are made to show honor to those who have lost the’sense
even of shame. I did not know what palaces were made
for, but now I have found a use for them. The palaces of
the Church should be hospitals and nurseries for those
who have fallen by the wayside and are perisbino-. [A
long pause.] lam not fit, dear brethren, to tell you any-
thing about morality. I have lived in shams and
hypocrisies too long to be able to help others ; but my
action of to-day shows me that the better way is very
easy to find. To those who believe in Jesus and His
Gospel there can be no other relation between man and
man but the relation of affection. Love alone is stronger
than sin—stronger than death. I therefore say to the
rich among you that it is their duty to do what I have
done and am doing. Let each one of you who is pro-
sperous take into his house some thief and treat him as
his brother, some unfortunate and treat her as his sister,
and London will need no police force, no magistrates ; the
prisons will be turned into hospitals, and the criminal
will disappear with his crime. We must give ourselves
and not our money alone.-’
At this point Mr. Asquith and Canon ScOtt-
Holland (a clerical giant) led the Bishop off the
platform, while the large and influential audience
sat breathless and paralysed. Cerebral collapse
under overwork and half-a-dozen explanations
have been put forward privately, but publicly—ex-
cluding one weekly—the silence has been thick as
a London fog at midnight. Perhaps Stead will
write : "‘lf Christ came to London.”

MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s “ Nurse.”
Her Majesty’s Italian Opera.
Theatre Royal “ A Million of Money.”
Bijou Dampier Dramatic Co.
New Opera House Rickards Variety Co.
A most deserving show is to eventuate on
the afternoon of Saturday, Gth July, at Sydney
Lyceum Theatre. Its object is to raise a fund for
the aid of those of the wives and children of
the ironworkers now on strike, who, are in
want. Mr. Anderson most kindly lends the
theatre, and many notable artists are going
to bear a hand, and make the show
well worth the admission money, irrespective
of its object. And even the person who thinks
that wages should be cut down to “2s. 6d. a week
with a corresponding increase of hours can hardly
say anything very severe against an entertainment
which is for the benefit of the women and children
only. They, at all events, haven’t demanded any
increase of pay.
* Ht * *
“The Power and the Glory,” which visited
Sydney not long ago, and is now renewing itself
at the Criterion, is recalled to remembrance as the
drama which eschews the Lost Document and the
Missing Will, and deals mostly with the blind
child that dies in a garret and etherealises after-
wards as an angel vision in the midnight sky,
making the stars and the housetops look terribly
disproportionate. The child (born blind) is so
remarkably clever that the instant it gains its
eyesight, it makes its way about the house and
about the church as easily as anyone else, and
recognises without the least difficulty articles that
it never saw before. The heroine behaves with
the usual want of sense noticeable in her kind
when she pretends to be a statue in the church at
midnight. She has just previously been threatened
by the villain, and rescued just m time, and tied
up. and had her face smacked by another lady,
and it seems incredible that she should fall
asleep immediately afterwards in a church in
the cheerful society of tombs and burglars ; but
she evidently does it. The aim of the “ Power and
the Glory ” is even more than usual to hang a good
deal of spectacular display on a very thin thread
of story. This is especially noticeable in the last
scene where nobody speaks at all, but the hero
and heroine are shown freed from all their troubles
and raised to the giddy splendour of appearing in
a London panto.
* * * •
C. R. Stanford, as the villain, has some
excellently cynical tones in his voice, but he might
be a little more effective as the maniac who goes
about with a perpetual vision of the deceased
before his eyes. Miss Ida Gresham appears in
the first act in abject indigence, as is shown by
her graceful and neat-fitting, but collarless, brown
dress. All that a stage heroine ever does to prove
either poverty or repentance is to have her dresses
made without collars, though there is nothing
especially expensive about this item of a lady’s
wardrobe. Some dreadful humour is contributed
by a butcher who goes about at all hours of the
day and night with the same three chops glued to
his board. He never seems to deliver these chops
anywhere, and as the play extends over a period
of at least a few months, it is likely that the
householder who ordered the meat will refuse, for
hygienic reasons, to receive it when it arrives.
** # *
The long-absent and much-missed Broughs
open Sydney Royal on Saturday with a four-act
comedy, “The Second in Command.” London
papers speak kindly of the “Second in Com-
mand,” which may mean much or little, but the
fact that Brough has selected it as the piece with
which to open a new season means a great deal.
Brough is a war-worn stage veteran who seldom
makes bad mistakes. The new co. includes Mrs.
Brough, Miss Temple, Cecil Ward and a few old
names—also some new ones. Tho new ones will
be duly recited in next week’s issue, along with
any good, bad or indifferent language that the
case appears to call for.
** # *
“ The Casino Girl ” will be staged at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney) on Saturday. “Florodora”
has resided there so long that it has become
almost as much a part of the establishment as
Goodman himself, so the public will remember
with some difficulty that the endless opera of the
Scented Isle has really moved on. The “Casino
Girl” has not yet been seen in Australia. The
book is by Harry B. Smith, with whom present
writer is not acquainted ; and the music is sup-
plied by Ludwig Englander, whom he doesn’t
remember having heard of until now. But pro-
bably they are both eminent men, and their work
is worthy of them, and the writer’s ignorance of
them is therefore all the more deplorable.
* *
At Sydney Empire, John Fuller still
Struggles manfully with ti e problem of how to
squeeze a quart o ienoe ■nto a pint of theatre.
Charlie Fairing and Miss Devoe burlesque the
Relief of Mafeking and the capture of the fear-
some and slippery De Wet as well as it is possible
to burlesque anything that hasn’t yet come off.
Denis Carney, the Yorkshire comedian from the
French territories out west, has struck a very
large patch of public approval. Fuller’s foreign
talent is now beginning to come along, but even
if it didn't come his show is hardly likely to be
stuck as long as the electro-plating doesn’t wear
off his own silvery tenor, and the Sad-eyed Shriek
can keep his sorrowful optic in its present state.
*# * *
“In the Soup” is still received favorably
at Sydney Palace, though the last act gives an
impression of having violently wrenched itself
away from the other two. The invasion of the
lawyer’s office for divorces all round appears out
of gear with the rest of the performance. A mis-
take is made when the disguised waiter spills
nothing at all out of his upset dish. The hero
might well have been so excited as to have
carefully carried nothing in a covered dish into
the dining-room ; nevertheless a wooden chicken
or property turkey rolling down towards the
footlights would add realism to the scene.
“In the Soup ” deserves much praise, though the
last act, as before mentioned, gives the impression
of being the head of one animal on the body and
legs of another.
** * *
“The Favorite,” a drama with some
hypnotism and a good deal of horse in it, pro-
gresses favorably at Sydney Lyceum, and the
conduct of the people who have poison dashed
away from them, yet continue accepting it, is, as
usual, inexplicable. The lost child turns out to
be heir to a good stableful of horses, proof of his
identity being given by a piece of blue ribbon
which he wore the day he was stolen, and which
turns up again after long years. The ribbon is
not even found on the child, presuming a boy
were in the habit of wearing anything of that
nature so long ; it is merely given to the mother
by an irresponsible person, and alleged to have
been found on the infant. It looks exactly like
any other piece of blue ribbon, too. In this play,
even more than usual, the curtain has a habit of
falling on a beautiful row, and when it rises again
everybody seems to have forgotten that particular
trouble. Nothing is really satisfactory or finished-
looking till the large blue policeman appears at
the last, and conveys away the villain in an un-
mistakable manner for doing something just about
as bad as the things he did in all the previous acts,
without any notice being taken of him. Miss
Kathleen Duggan has ceased to sing her ditty,
but a dog called Laddie appears on the stage in a
casual manner. Laddie does not do all the things he
is billed as doing on the posters, and some nights
he does not cross the boards at all. He seems to
have had a row with the manager, or to have been
otherwise unreasonable.
*# # *
The “Merchant of Venice” was staged
for a few nights at Sydney Royal by Mr. F. M.
Alexander, Miss Edith Tasca Page, and a few
people of no great importance. Mr. Alexander’s
conception of the Jew who used his sole as a
grindstone in a public manner made very much
for ferocity. His Shylock was more violent and
shrielty than usual, and his anger was loud and
fierce when he was denied his enemy’s meat. A
Shylock with more dignity and pathos about him
would be more natural. It was a period when
Israel had learned to subdue its emotions —when
its hawk-like nose came down over its beard in a
dissembling smile, and it only raged inwardly
while it was under the Christian eye. The Shy-
lock supplied by Mr. Alexander was a mixture of
the pawnshop and the bravo. It was a fine piece
of work, yet it seemed to suggest a strong financial
warrior who advanced money with the security in
one hand, and a drawn sword in the other, and
who didn’t mind whether he charged a regiment or
60 per cent. Miss Tasca Page did her part well as
Portia ; and Jessica, after excusing herself at the
window for eloping in boy’s dress, eloped across
tiie stage in ordinary dress, as if the stage ward-
robe was limited. In the casket scene there
were some nice girls in evening dress, and
they cheered Bassanio considerably when he
chose the right box, one lady waving her handker-
chief at him as if he were a departing mail-boat.
J. Dorahy was really good as Gobbo, and various
people called Lorenzo and Bassanio and Gratiano
came on the stage occasionally, looking very well
in red and yellow and green legs respectively.
** « *
Sydney Tivoli is strong in Australasian
artists just now —the acrobatic Leopold Brothers
and Arnoldi, the clever juggler, were all born in
the land of the drought and the kangaroo. John
Coleman has some new songs, and Whelan, the
Australian mimic, has started to give the Brough
co. a lift up by imitating Robert himself. He
also appears as a Messenger from Mars, and re-
forms an imaginary Horace Parker inside three
minutes.
** * *
A photo, which appears in this issue
represents a young American actress, Miss Elsie
Lander, who opens at the Victoria Theatre, New-
castle, on Saturday, Cth July, under the manage-
ment of Mr. Fred. E. Hiscocks and Miss S. G.
Oliver. During the past three years Miss Lander
has been touring Australia with Wybert Reeve
the late Grattan Riggs, W. Anderson, and other
managers. Judging by her photo., she is fair to
look upon, and if her talents and her company are
in keeping with her outward semblance things
should go well.
** * *
When Musgtove started his plucky grand-
opera campaign in Melbourne, people who ought
to have known better expressed surprise at his
not changing the bills more rapidly. The same
complaint is made about the Italian opera season.
Why aren’t the unknown works “Otello,”
“Fedora,” and “La Boheme” produced right
away ? The answer is—Why isn’t this country,
like Italy, stocked with operatic chorus singers ?
New works have to await the education of the
chorus before they can be produced. The prin-
cipals of the Italian co. are ready to perform six
different operas in a week, but the chorus thus far
has not got the music of any six operas on the tips
of tongue. Next year the manager who comes
along with a new co, will have a valuable lot of
choristers ready for engagement, better voices,
though not better versed in the music, than
Lyster had at his command 20 years ago.
* * *
The second biggest crowd of the season
was drawn to Her Majesty’s (Melb ) on Saturday
by “ Pagliacci ” and “ Cavalleria Rusticana, the
two brief operas which came as novelties to the
Princess’s, some eight years ago, in conjunction
with the beauteous Blue Ballet.
“ Pagliacci,” by Leoncavallo, and “ Caval-
leria,” by Mascagni, are musical matters of
peculiar interest. They took the world by sur-
prise in ’9l or thereabouts. They were accepted
as first samples of a new, up-to-date, short-story
opera which would monopolise the market and
create a general dislike to the heavier works of
the older composers. Well, the samples have
retained their popularity ; it is possible that they
will live to be classics. But they are practically
alone in the world. Mascagni and Leoncavallo
seem unable to repeat their brilliant successes,
and no other short-story composer has scored any
success worth mentioning. Moreover, the world
is doubtful as to whether it really wants a large
number of meaty dramatic operas equal in merit
to those popular samples. Wagner, who repre-
sents long opera at its longest, has gained ground
since the comet Mascagni flashed his fiery tale;
and the brightest of Verdi’s works are still going
strong.
• • • *
“ Pagliacci ” is a flimsy little story with
novel effects in it. The villain of the piece is a
clown who bangs a drum outside a tent, and the
injured husband is another clown. Also, the
second tenor is a harlequin, and the prima donna
plays columbine in the show, which doesn’t
prevent her from acquiring a guilty passion
for a red-nosed villager. The chorus folks are
gaudy rustics who jubilate exceedingly at the
arrival of the four mummers in a cart,
and afterwards put 3d. per head into a
tambourine for the encouragement of travelling
drama. They get a good three penn’orth in this
case. The married clown varies the monotony of
his painted mummery by stabbing his nice young
wife with a stage dinner-knife, after which he
assassinates the red-nosed Lothario, and remarks
that the comedy is finished. “Pagliacci” is not
so neatly constructed or naturally tinted as
“Cavalleria Rusticana.” Everybody knows the
story of the erring spinster and the giddy matron
and the young man from the wine-shop. Mrs.
Potter and Bellew produced it in a purely
dramatic form during their last visit to Australia.
Besides, the sympathetic Intermezzo serves to
make people interested in the opera even if they
haven’t seen it staged.
* * * *
The company which introduced “Pag. ’
and “ Cav.” to Australiain daysof yore was picked
for the parts. The present company gives per-
formances not quite so realistic, but thoroughly
satisfactory, nevertheless. Tenor Dani, who
plays the husband in “ Pagliacci ” and the dandy
lover in the other tragedy, scores rather heavily
each time. His appearance when he wants to look
pretty is the appearance of Henry Plimmer.
Also, he acts much the same as Plimmer
would, and his voice fills the bill. It is
a pleasing tenor—a light robusto. For some un-
accountable reason the Melbourne press notices
agreed to lay stress upon its alleged “sweetness,”
as though it were suited to “ Sally in our Alley.”
As a matter of fact, Dani is never quite so sugary
as Salvi, but his notes are younger. Bozzoli, the
baritone, sings well and acts fairly in both operas.
Signora Sapio is a valuable Santuzza (in “ Caval-
leria”), and a new lady, Signorina Vaccari, ap-
pears as Nedda in “Pagliacci.” She is a very
young new lady, with a very pretty little voice
and very little Italian 'character in her acting.
Anyone would take her for an Australian. The
operas are artistically mounted, the orchestra
excellent (though the accompaniment in the soft
passages is rather too heavy), and the chorus quite
at home with the music. So much at home that
some of them sang what sounded like an English
translation of the lines. Un Ballo in Maschera ”
is in rehearsal.
*# * *
“ The Green Lanes of England,” wherein
Dampier wrecks his constitution with rum and
delivers sound moral precepts from under the
influence of liquor, is a drama that will probably
occur for the last time at Melbourne Bijou this
(Thursday) evening. Fridays at the Bijou are
devoted to the Bard, and Saturdays seem to bring
a weekly change of bill as regularly as the
grocer. Therefore the good points in Dampier’s
well known sketch of Gentleman George are
hardly worth mentioning as a matter of current
interest. They are here to-day and probably gone
to-morrow, along witli the respectable paternal
curse of old man Edmund Holloway. The curse
in question is a slow and weighty effort which no
ill-regulated daughter can approve of, but as an ex-
ample of how a stern, fat-headed parent ought to
curse, if nothing but a curse can relieve
his feelings, Holloway’s anathema is all right.
Also, it tends to justify the action of two
green-lane villains, who conspire to drop the
cursed daughter into a river. When a plump and
melancholy girl has experienced such awful
language as befalls Miss Lily Dampier, she is
prepared, as a rule, to wish that Death would
remove her from the drama, yet she objects to
being drowned when the opportunity offers.
Hamlet and the Ghost are due again to-morrow
(Friday), and the name of the next melodrama
will be duly stated in the ad.
»* * *
Bland Holt intends to change his drama
at the Royal, on Saturday, 6th. ‘‘ln London
Town ” has flourished for several weeks, and its
charm for the multitude is not quite as young as
it was, so the blind man will wallop the lame boy
for the last time on Friday. “ A Million of
Money ” is the forthcoming bill, and it will be
produced on a scale of suitable splendour.
«. * * *
A ,tall black bear, who raises a bottle to
his mouth and drinks the contents with great in-
telligence, also two bro\vn bears, whose education
is not so far advanced, are the latest arrivals at
Melb. Opera House. They have a gentleman in
attendance named Winschermaun, but Winscher-
maun contributes nothing to the gaiety of the
performance, lie doesn’t even attempt to drink
from the bear’s bottle. Little Eric is another new
turn. The Dartos and Austin Rudd are leaving
this week, and a considerable reformation of the
programme is promised for Saturday.
w* * *
On Saturday next (6tli) Melbourne Prin-
cess’s will break a long, cold silence. Miss
Jennie Maynard is to open the theatre for a flying
season of farcical comedy, society drama, senti-
mental comedietta, &c., and the first bill
of the season will be “ Nurse,” preceded by “ A
Bit of Old Chelsea.” Miss M., who was a
quite adorable little lady the last time Melbourne
saw her, appears as the dangerous female in the
white cap and apron, and Henry Ludlow, late of
London and other cities, is another special feature
in the cast. After Miss Maynard’s season of 12
nights, the Vic. Opera Society will come along
and produce “Girofle-Girofla.”
** * *
Williamson’s No. 2 “ Florodora ” co.,
lately performing at Bendigo, compares very
favorably with the No. 1 crowd. Fred. Graham
in Lauri’s part, Cecil Engleheart as a charming
Dolores, and Rose Musgrove as Lady Holyrood
are particular successes. The latter, however,
seems to have overtaxed her strength, and will be
off for a holiday this week if she isn’t already
resting.

Seems that Titheradge is to play Aubrey
Tanqueray to the Paula of Mrs. Pat. Campbell.
London will get Tith’s performance of this classic
comedy character seven years subsequent to his
i 4 creating ” it for Australia.
It is in Victoria, once leading democratic
State of Australia, that this recrudescence of
bigotry takes place ! And Reynolds's News-
paper, which sells by the hundred thousand in
England, is suppressed as treasonable in Victoria,
on the thin pretext that it contains indecent adver-
tisements ! Taken with Premier Peacock’s truck-
ling cablegram to Chamberlain, asking endorse-
ment for a suppression of the Tocsin, these facts
give fresh evidence that the Australian breed of
Jingoes are baser toadies to royalty than the
British Jingoes themselves. This business of the
alleged libel shows how rotten is the machinery of
local bureaucracy in the democratic view.
Rev. George Hay, having separated him-
self from the Presbyterian Church, had performed
numerous marriages at certain Sydney “matrimo-
nial bureaux,” and the regular parsons’ trade-union
—having signally failed to have said bureaux
suppressed by law—made afterwards, through the
Crown, a desperate attempt to prevent Mr. Hay’s
registration as a person entitled to celebrate
marriages, and to obtain at the same time some
judge-made law to the effect that the bureaux in
question should not be allowed to exist. N.S.W.
Supreme Court, however, has, by a majority,
granted a mandamus directing Mr. Hay’s regis-
tration as a minister entitled to celebrate mar-
riages ; and the Judges, though somewhat
deprecating the purely business-like methods of
the matrimonial agencies, declined to say that the
consequent facilitation of marriages is an evil in
itself.
Some of these days or nights it will
come: A comparatively light and under-
powered steamer will try to reach or get
away from Manly across the huge beam sea
raised by a heavy easterly or S.E. gale ; her
machinery will suddenly go wrong and she will
drift ashore on Dobroyd or Middle Head, and a
hundred or two people will be doomed to death as
certainly as if they went into the Gap on a Dun-
bar night. Then the A.M.P. (if the steamer hap-
pens to be the morning or evening “business”
boat) will have to shell out the biggest
cheque it has ever disbursed in all its life.
The nice, swift, corky little s.s. Manly and her
passengers had a marrow-freezing escape the other
night, when something went wrong with her
engines during a howling gale. She was within
an ace of drifting onjto the big Bombora or on to
Dobroyd. Her 50 passengers mainly owe their
lives to the prompt and nerveful assistance
rendered by the captain of the company’s big
steamer Brighton, who managed to get a line
aboard, and, after it had parted more than once,
towed the distressed boat pretty well out of
danger to a spot whence the Manly, unable to
hold on, was allowed to go ashore on a soft bit of
sand. Sooner or later, the man who has gone
through prize seas at Sydney Heads on his way to
Manly always arrives at the conclusion that the
only sort of ship he would care to cross the Heads
in during that sort of weather is a heavy, ruffianly,
powerfully-engined, whale-backed ocean-tug of
the sort which could take in 500 tons of coal and
steam right out of Port Jackson to sea in the teeth of
a Dandenong gale. Manly will never be worth liv-
ing in until it gets, to meet such occasions, a tram
right through from Sydney.
There’s a tinkle of bells in the air
As we think of the sweet Ballet Blue ;
And a vision enchanting
Of Bartho is haunting
The bardlet who loves her anew.
When lightly she turns on her toe—
A sudden sublime pirouette,!
The shows known as “ rotten ”
Are quickly forgotten,
But good things we never forget—
Never, never!
The good things we never forget.
There are ninety spry girls on the stage,,
Preparing to welcome the Bride.
They warble a chorus,
Then —Bartho’s before us !
She came with a fairy-like glide.
The date of the year’s ’93 ;
We are “ long way from forty” as yet.
0, the past is the present
In memories pleasant
Of things that we cannot forget.
Fill your glasses,
To the good things we cannot forget!

The Cobar Chesney mine, Cobar, have lately
placed an order with the Austral Otis Engineering Com-
pany for a pair of powerful windinir engines to haul from
2000 feet. These engines are rapidly approaching com-
pletion, and will be delivered about first of next month.
The same firm of engineers have also supplied the Occi-
dental mine, Cobar, with a handsome pair of winding
engines, as well as a boiler. This machineiy was delivered
in very quick time and should give great satisfaction.*

Gentleman having only a limited time at their
disposal can obtain the practical instruction in assaying
and metallurgical work, necessary to qualify them for
obtaining good positions, in the ore testing works of Gil-
flllau and McCreery, Stock Exchange Buildings, Melb.*

If you want to borrow money, go to N.S.W.
Mont de Piete Co., Ltd., 74 Castlereagh-street, 74.
Eustace Bennett, Manager.*

About the best Brand for Sheep now in the
market is Kemp’s Branding Liquid. It goes further and
is considerably cheaper than any other. It will dissolve
in the scour, and should certainly be used by those who
hope to get a good price for their wool. E. Rich & Co.,
Sydney and Brisbane, hold stocks.*

The Insurance Record of 20th July, 1900,
writes with reference to the Colonial Mutual Life Assur-
ance Society, Limited : “ The Society’s transactions
during 1899 are calculated to afford considerable satis-
faction to all connected with the institution and the
management are to be congratulated upon having brought
affairs to so successful an issue during troublous and un-
settled times.”*

John W. H. Piper, C.E., M.A.1.M.E.,
M.Am.I.M.E., &c., Australian representative of Cutten
Bros., Dunedin, mining and dredging engineers, is leaving
for London about the end of August to establish a Home
Office for the firm, the increase of foreign business
necessitating this action. Mr. Piper will be pleased to
undertake any commissions that may be entrusted to him.
Equitable Buildings, Georg e-street, Sydney.*

Messrs. Plummer Love and Co., 249 George-
street, Sydney, are large suppliers of all mining requi-
sites. They are sole agents here for the now celebrated
“ British” brand of explosives, detonators and fuse ; also
for the Taylor Horsfield Improved National Rock Drills,
Air Compressors, etc., etc.*
* * * •
All Barrier Mines remodelling their mills on up-
to-date lines include a number of Luhrig vanners. The
South and Junction companies each have about a dozen,
no slime treatment plant being now considered complete
without these excellent machines. In Queensland tin
concentration is the latest accomplishment, the Irvine-
bank Mill having introduced Luhrig Vanners with success-
ful results, the example having been followed by the well-
known Coolgarra Tin Mining Co., who are installing a
number of Luhrig Vanners in connection with their new
Tin Dressing Mill. Messrs. W. &J. Lempriere, Melbourne,
are the general agents for the Luhrig Appliances.*

Norman Godkin, of 31 Queen-street, Melbourne,
is prepared to purchase or develope, with option of
purchase, ‘‘big prospecting shows” (either gold or
mineral) in any part of Australia. Properties offered
must be subject to inspection. Cable address: “ Option,”
Melbourne.*
* * * *
Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-

Broken Hill Prop.’s coke-ovens at Bellambi,
N.S.W. coast, have been started ; the output is only for
the supply of the co.’s smelting furnaces at Port Pirie.
For some years past the coke bill has run from £90,000 to
£140,000 a year. Recent supplies have come mainly from
Germany, with a little from Wales. Eight years ago the
co. made an effort to use N.S.W. coke ; but it was not
persisted in on account of the excess of ash. The im-
ported stuff’s ash percentage runs from under 5 to 9 ; the
local from 7£ to over 14. Defective washing (or no wash-
ing at all) and old-fashioned ovens were said to be
responsible. The co. has now its own pit and plant.

S.M. Herald says:— “lt appears that the
Commonwealth civil-servants who have not already
assurance or pension rights number only about 1500.
It is held that the formation of a Department of Govern-
ment Assurance to deal with such a small number of
policies is a step that ought not to be taken without the
fullest inquiry and consideration.” That is to say, the
Federal Government Life Assurance Department would
have 1500 clients to start with, and could rely on issuing
about 1500 policies in its first year (probably far more for
the public servants under its control must increase greatly
before the year is up) apart from any business it might
do with the public. The Australian Widows’ Fund, in its
seventh year, had only got so far as to issue 622 policies as
its 12 months’ work. If the average premiums paid to
the Federal Government Life Assurance Office by each of
its 1500 sure clients only averaged £4 per man all round,
it would have a premium income of £6OOO in its first
year, apart from business done with the public. The
A.M.P. was in its seventh year before it collected £6OOO
premiums. It was in its twelfth year before it had 1500
policies on its books. It was 20 years old before it
issued 1500 policies in one year. And though the pro-
posed Government Life Office might start with only
1500 reliable clients, it would get more every year as
it took over new departments, as its own departments
grew larger, and as old officers (insured in private offices)
died off, and new ones (who would be insured in the
Government office) took their places. And all this would
be in addition to the business it would do with the public.
On the Herald’s own showing the Federal Government
Life Office has a gorgeous show. Lyne is the man who is
thinking hard and feeling very dubious over the matter.
Lyne is a director of the Citizens’ office, which started
with very much humbler prospects than those that lie
before tne proposed Federal office. Does Lyne feel any
doubts—especially on dividend day—whether it was worth
While starting the Citizens ?
* * * •
A conference of Australian bankers has kindly
drafted a banking law for the Commonwealth, and they
have offered their unbiassed opinion as to how the
Commonwealth should order things. The bankers desire
that the Queensland State note issue be abolished ; they
want no Federal note issue and no Government Bank. They
suggest that every bank should be allowed to issue notes
to the full amount of its cash reserves, which means that
the note issue may go up to about six times its present
amount. They also suggest that, though a bank may issue
notes at a hundred branch offices in a State, it shall only
be liable to cash them in gold at one office—its chief office
in the State capital. This would certainly be an
advantage to the banks, for the amount of gold it
would then be necessary at branch offices would
be reduced. And the banks offer to pay a tax of 2 per
cent, on their note issues.
All these proposals are good enough in a way ;
if the Federal Government is going to lose a splendid
opportunity, it may as well lose it that way as any other.
In Canada, they order things differently—though not so
differently as they might do. There, the banks are required
by law to keep at least 40 per cent, of their reserves in
Government notes, and, at latest advices, they held
Government notes to the extent of £4,000,000. Conse-
quently. the Government gets a perpetual loan of
£4,000,000 without interest. This is saying nothing of
Government notes in circulation. Also’ the banks have
£10,000,000 of their own notes in circulation. If the
Canadian Government had taken over the whole business
of note-issue, therfore, instead of taking half-measures,
it appears that it might get a perpetual loan,
without interest, of at least £10,000,000, allowing
that it kept a cash-reserve of £3,000,000 or £4,000,000
against its paper money. And there is no visible reason
why the Commonwealth Government should not do this,
and thereby find the money needed to acquire 5000 sq.
miles for a Federal territory, and build a Federal city
and get all these splendid assets absolutely gratis.
Sydney Telegraph lays it down on its own un-
supported authority that “ The Canadian system to hold
half the banking cash in coin and half in Commonwealth
noteswould be impossible in Australia. At present our Aus-
tralian banks hold over £20,000,000 in coin. The Federal
Parliament, if it legislated to take away half that coin
and to give the banks £10,000,000 in notes which would
not circulate to the extent of above one-tenth the
amount, would be locking up £9,000,000 of bank money,
and the banks would suffer very severely." This is a wildly
absurd view of the case. Under the proposed system each
hank would have to keep, say, half of its cash reserves in
the Commonwealth in State paper. If the B.N.S.W. had
£4,000,000 at its various offices in the Commonwealth
£2,000,000 would be cash and £2,000,000 Government
notes. It is simply proposed to enact that the gold
which is locked up and lying idle, and the Government
notes which are locked up and lying idle, shall be of equal
amount. One would be no more locked up than the
other. One would circulate just as much as the other.
The banks’ aggregate coin reserves don’t circulate to
one-tenth their amount- just as the Telegraph says
the notes wouldn’t; it is because they don’t circulate
that they are reserves. If the B.N.S.W.’s cash reserve
went down through the despatch of £1,000,000 of gold to
London, and it had £2,000,000 of Government notes left
against £1,000,000 in gold, it could circulate 1 500,000 of
these notes, or, if they wouldn’t circulate, or it didn’t
want to circulate them,’ it could demand £500,000 in gold
from the State in return for £500,000 in notes, and so
square the matter. If there was a run on it, it could pay
out gold and Government notes equally, or it could pay
out gold and as the proportion of notes grew larger in
comparison with what gold remained, it could cash the
surplus notes at the Treasury. There could be no locking
up under these conditions. Where the Government
would come in would be just here : The total cash reserves
of the banks vary to only a small extent, though the re-
serves of individual banks vary a great deal. The total
amount of Government notes in circulation would remain
much the same, though the amount held by any one bank
might vary; and a very small reserve of gold in the
Treasury would meet any possible demand. The balance
of the note issue would represent a large perpetual loan
without interest. The banks wouldn’t lock up a penny
of the coin that is in use ; the operation would only affect
the coin not in use.
Of course if the Australian people don’t like this
idea they have a perfect right to disapprove. In that
case the money which would thus be made available for
Federal Government purposes can easily be raised by loan
instead, and an extra income tax of 3d. in the £, or an
extra id. in the £ land-tax will be quite sufficient to pay
the interest.
* * * •
The Victorian Local Government Act makes it
illegal for any local governing body to close its financial
year with an overdraft, and holds the councillors per-
sonally responsible for any such overdraft. It is a simple
and dead-sure way to keep these institutions going
straight, and Parliament expresses its approval by keep-
ing the Act on the statute-book. No Vic. Govt, would
dare to propose its abolition. At the same time no Vic.
Govt, dares to enforce it. So the salutary law remains,
but every year a measure is hustled through to the effect
that those councils which have broken the law during
the preceding 12 months shall, just for this once, be re-
garded as not having broken the law. Why not have
the courage to either repeal the law, or enforce it ?
• * • •
An eminently wise proviso in the Bill dealing
with Acquisition of Property for Federal Purposes is to the
effect that all State property taken over will be paid for
either in 3 per cent. Federal securities, or by the Federal
Government taking over a corresponding amount of the
State debt. If the Federal Government borrowed the
money and paid cash for post-offices and the like (which
were built with borrowed money) the average State would
just treat that cash as ordinary revenue and use it to pay
off the old deficit, or something like that. The idea of
using the money obtained by the sale of the post-offices
or other works to pay off the debt incurred in their erec-
tion wouldn’t occur to the average Treasurer.
I # $ y
Federal Treasurer Turner, in his Audit Bill,
lays it down that the interest on Federal loans is to be
charged to the half-year in which the interest is incurred.
Which is Turner’s mild, business-like expression of
opinion upon Reid’s Cash Basis dodge, whereby the in-
terest for the half-year to 30th June (when the financial
year closes) is charged to next financial year, so that
there is always carried forward a huge unpaid and un-
provided-for liability.

Goldsborough, Mort and Co. had still another
re-arrangemsnt—its third—during the past year, and
still another heavy writing-down. Not only the share-
holders but also the debenture-holders suffer this time,
£493,740 being written off the B debentures, and nearly
£400,000 off capital. In all £887,736 was made available
for the purpose of writing down assets, and the manage-
ment is confident that the co. is now on a “ sound foot-
ing.” The balance-sheet hardly bears out this sanguine
view of things. The A debenture stock (£1,427,050) re-
ceives full interest (4 per cent.) hut there isn’t nearly
enough money left to pay the full 3 per cent, on what
remains of the B debentures—they now stand at £740,610,
£493,740 having been written-off. In fact, on the remain-
ing B stock only 15s. per cent, is paid. The progress of
Goldsborough, Mort and Co., since the second re-arrange-
ment in 1895. is shown in its balance-sheets as follows :
The results may be summarised thus: In 5j years the
B debenture-holders have only received 1$ years’ interest
and have lost £493,740 of their capital. Net earnings
will require to increase by about another £18,500 before
the co. can pay full interest on its debentures, despite
the writing down, and they will require to increase about
£46,000 or £47,000 before there will be sufficient to pay 5
per cent, dividend on the much written-down capital,
saying nothing of putting aside anything substantial for
reserves.
The gross earnings are less than in 1900, and
less than in 1896. Apparently, they are also less than in
1897-8-9. In these years it seems that, by a different
system of book-keeping, three months’ interest accrued
on A debentures was deducted from the gross profits on
one side of the profit and loss account, and only one
month’s interest appeared on the other side of the ac-
count, while in 1900 and 1901 there was no such deduc-
tion, and the whole year’s interest appeared among the
outgoings. If this is so, the situation, supposing a uni-
form book-keeping system had been adopted all along,
would have been something like this :
The reduction in gross earnings is more than com-
pensated by the decline in expenses through economies
and the repayment of a £70,000 mortgage, and through
a saving in interest by repayment of some A deben-
tures. The net result is £932 better than in 1897, and
£5092 better than in 1900 ; also, it is a very great im-
provement on 1898-9. At the same time, Goldsbrough,
Mort and Co. still earns a good deal less than nothing per
cent, for its shareholders, and it seems rather premature
to decide that it is on a “ sound footing.”

In 1892 the numbers added up to 28,839,425. Very nearly
half of Queensland’s wealth in live stock has vanished in
eight years, all through the unfitness of a very large pro-
portion of this country for agricultural ml pastoral enter-
prise-more than half has disappeared, 4 if the deteriorated
condition of the survivors is taken into account. Yet the
Freetrader still assures us that manufactures don’t really
matter. The “great producing interests,” which seem
to consist very largely in driving demented bags of bones
over dry plains, are everything.

The indignant American gentleman who took
proceedings against the Independent Order of Foresters,
alleging that its chief boss, an Indian with a very' long
name, and a clique of his friends, made serious and reck-
less misuse of the assurance funds, was badly beaten
when his case came into court. Judge Blanchard decided
that all the charges had been fully met, and that the
plaintiff had no evidence whatever to show. This
leaves the question as to whether the funds are anything
like sufficient to keep the institution in a state of sol-
vency just where it was. The decision is that what funds
there are are carefully invested and well taken care of.
Apropos to the above, The Bulletin received a
letter recently from the local office of the 1.0. F., and
found its notepaper still decorated with the old
misleading comparisons between its own rates and
those alleged to be charged by Australian offices. The
following is a sample of how it represents things :
Policy for £lOOO.
Total cost per quarter Average quarterly
Age last in 1.0. F.— Premiums Premium with
biithday. (ceasing at age 70) and Profits in
Court dues. Australian offices.
20 .. £2 16s. 3d. .. £4 19s. 2d.
30 .. £3 17s. 6d. .. £6 7s. 6d.
40 .. £5 11s. 3d. .. £8 12s. 6d.
50 .. £9 7s. Od. .. £l2 6s. Bd.
These look very beautiful and cheap and all that, but in
reality they are something very much akin to lying, or gross
misrepresentation, or getting the public’s premiums under
false pretences. The things that are suppressed are
these : (1) The 1.0. F. pays no bonuses ; it compares its
without-profit rates with the alleged with-profit rates in
Australia; (2) The Australian average rates are not
so high as represented; the A.M.P. is an office which
charges high rates and returns big profits, and even it
only charges £4 18s. 4d. instead of £4 19s. 2d. as alleged
above. (3) The Australian offices give surrender values ;
the man who can’t keep up his payments can return his
policy and get a large proportion of his premiums back.
The 1.0. F. gives nothing back in such cases ; (4) The 1.0. F.
also charges entrance fees and medical examination fees.
(5) The above rates are for £lOOO. But the average
policy is for much less than that. And the
1.0. F., in premiums and court dues, charge more
for a small without-profit policy than the most expen-
sive Australian office does. Its rates for large policies are
cheap ; its rates for small ones are exorbitant. Yet out
of this exorbitant charge so much goes in paying expenses
and “ court dues” that there is less left towards paying
the policy at maturity than in the Australian office—a
case of paying more money and getting far less security.
This is about how the case stands at age 20—the propor-
tions are much the same at all other ages.
Policy for £lOO without Profits—Age 20.
1.0. F. A.M.P.
Quarterly payment .. .. .. Bs. ljd. .. 7s. Gd.
Of which the A.M.P. appropriates for
expenses and the 1.0. F. for expenses
and court dues, about .. .. 3s. 4£d. .. Is. Id.
Leaving to accumulate towards paying
the policy .. .. .. 4s. 9d. .. 6s. sd.
That is how things are as regards the small policyholder,
and there are 10 or 20 of him to one large policyholder.
And when he insures in the A.M.P. he takes a share in an
office which, by its last valuation, should have had
£16,379.150 on hand to cover the present value of all
liabilities under its policies and which had £17,022,045-
surplus, £642,895. If he insures in the 1.0. F. he takes a
share in an office which, at the date of its last valuation
required to have £7,159,342 on hand to cover the present
value ot all liabilities under its existing policies, and
which had £518,724 —deficiency, £6,640,618. The 1.0.F.’s
sole hope of being able to pay more than a fraction of its
policies was that most of its policy-holders would fail
some day to pay their premiums, and lose their insurance
and every penny they had previously paid. The insurer
who wants to deliberately take a share in a shortage like
that deserves to get it.
* » * *
Ed. Bulletin.—“ Wild Cat” has not shown its
usual keenness in commenting upon the Bendigo Cash
Coupon Co. in last week’s issue. This co. was formed
(according to the secretary’s statement) to crush the
System, and so the figures published being biassed,
should have been more critically examined. The co., in
its first six months, issued 3,000,000 coupons, and
redeemed 800,000 ; therefore the clever Directors wish to
make the public believe that the 2,200,000 will not come
in, and the value is therefore reckoned as profit. A bank
might just as well credit its note-circulation as profit
account. The average time coupons remain in circulation
is about six months, but the Directors of the 8.0. Co.
knew that half of the unredeemed coupons were in the
hands of the traders (not yet issued to the public) while
the remaining half were in the hands of the public, w’ho
need some time to collect the minimum redeemable num-
ber. You also err in saying that the B.C. Co. makes a
profit on the goods given in exchange. It does not give
goods, but redeems in cash, giving 2s. per 100 coupons,
which it has sold for 2s. 6d. It is these misrepresenta-
tions and reservations which prejudice people against the
Coupon System, which, if honestly administered, is a
boon to the community.— Fair Play.

A poor, squalid, undignified rotv about the
Invoice appears to follow every spontaneous out-
break of “loyalty” in this country. There is
always somebody scuffling about 2d., or even less,
on the steps of the temple, or trying to collect
l|d. under the shadow of the great god Jingo
himself. The latest clawing-match about the
Bill is painfully cheap and nasty. The story goes
that when fat Jenkins of the Beard was arranging
(or the trustees were arranging with Jenkins)
for the visit of the Yorks to Melbourne Exhibition
Building, it was decided that the trustees’ room
would do to receive them in. Whereupon the
trustees refurnished the room at a cost of £4SI,
and laid in £Bl worth of spirits, £l9 worth of
cigars, and some wine; alarming total, £596.
The liquor included nine cases champagne, two
cases brandy, one case port, one case ale, one
case porter, one case dry gin, and many
other drinks. (It wasn’t even reasonably certain
at the time that the Yorks would visit the build-
ing at all, though they did call in accidentally for
a few minutes). The trustees said that their war-
rant for all this expense lay in the fact that fat
Jenkins waved his fat hand and said, “Do
the thing properly.” Jenkins said he didn’t
wave anything ; that he merely offered to borrow
some new furniture if necessary. In the course of
the sore-headed wrangling about the sordid Bill
the way one gentleman calls another a liar is pain
ful to listen to. The trustees bailed up State
Premier Peacock with their invoice, and Peacock
cast them off as if they were a mouldy green
umbrella. Then they interviewed Federal Premier
Barton, who indignantly asked them if they
expected the Duke and Duchess to consume £9C>
worth of alcohol (including a case of dry gin), and

The little Jook is to get an educational
trowel when he lays the foundation stone of some-
thing or' other at Hobart. The blade of the
deeply-honored implement will represent the map
of Tassy, with rivers and coast-line accurately
scaled, and the mallet is being modelled from a
eucalyptus-flower, the stem serving for handle.
Who’d have thought of Hobart having an inspira-
tion in silver trowel grovel !

As soon as the Jook had left Melbourne
people began to remark a slump in the Argus’s
r< erence for Royalty. The Tory journal pre-
s mtly seemed to recover some of its lost hysterics,
and now allegations of eloquence and “charming
condescension ” and so forth are credited to
H.R.11. in the headings of its cable news from
Maoriland. But this awakening from a dull
stupor of disappointment to a proper Argus sense
of duty to the Throne has merely emphasised the
suspicion that somebody on the Argus made up
his mind to accept something which didn’t happen,
when the Jook was on the spot. He had dreamed
of a bended knee and a bauble, and the gentle,
pressure of a royal hand in his. But the dream
went by and stupefaction set in, and that also
passed in its turn. Everything passes here below,
except a Radical measure in Vic. Leg. Council.
The Argus boss picked up the broken pieces of
his Hope and bade the policy of the newspaper
be of good cheer. All the same, there is probably
a limit to the Argus’s endurance. It can’t go
hoping on for ever.
The visit of the Duchess of York and her
Duke has not served to strengthen the loyalty
even of the globular swell in Australia, chiefly
because the second-rate public personage didn’t
get a look-in. The other day, in Yic. Assembly,
T. Bent was speaking of the Royal Couple as the
“ Dook and Dookess” but Peacock who is trying
to live up to the Windsor uniform he bought for
arose at the massive man, and

More English geography: “The Duke
and Duchess of Cornwall and York,” says the
Sketch, “are likely to have an especially in-
teresting time at Sydney, the beautiful city of'
which Victorians are justly proud.”
* * * *
In Maoriland, as elsewhere, the returned
cohentingenters are “slumping badly.” M.L.’s
fourth and fifth shiploads, back this month,
tumble right up against the tail-end of the Colossal
Crawl, and will get killed in the crush. Thedemand
for “ light ” work for the poor returned 15-stoners
didn’t come off, and the interesting heroes have
had to doff girl-snaring kharki for lumper’s moles
and navvy’s knee-straps. Seddon has apparently
exhausted all the Jingo vote, and hasn’t even
enough to spare for the Volunteers. No camps have
been held this year at any of the big centres save
Wellington, and there the local paper complains
pathetically that although six or seven weeks have
elapsed since camp struck, the Defence Dept, has
not yet paid its accounts.
* * * *
Adelaide Advertiser, June 27 :
When our sons were called upon to fight, we dramati-
cally declared we should act very differently towards the
returning brave. They would he the recipients of a
nation’s tender care, and the noble deeds they had done
would always be held in admiring remembrance. . . .
Eighteen months ago, every man who volunteered for the
war was a laurel-crowned hero. Now, those who have
come home again seem to be regarded in the light of en-
cumbrances. What has become of our former pride and
patriotism ?
The Advertiser’s recovery from its debauch is
taking the same form as that of the rest of the
Jingo press. The “Encumbrances,” by the way,
are now celebrating their return to Adelaide by
advertising in the ’Tiser for any job they can get,
and dozens are walking the streets -shabby and
penniless.

The same old story. This time, from
Lyttelton (M.L.) Times :
The carelessness of the Govt. (Seddon’s Govt. !!) is only
a reflection of the indifference of the public. When the
early contingents took the field, they were literally bur-
dened with “comforts” of one kind and another. We
should be afraid to say how many cases of clothing and
luxuries were sent to them last year. But now
Kitchener’s appeal is ignored, and the men are left to get
through a severe winter with none of the assistance con-
sidered so necessary 12 months ago.
It turns out that after all the big Maori
shivoo at Rotorua wasn’t too huge a success. The
weather wasn’t over-good; the beer had been
turned on too long before the Juke arrived ; and
the Maoris got “the spike ” because he persistently
refused to appear in uniform. Worst of all, he
offended their notions of etiquette by neglecting
to thank them personally for their exertions. One
influential chief sums up H.R.H. as “Ver’ ’fraid
(afraid) man I tink—too frighten talk.”

From a letter written, by a Victorian war-
rior, named MTntosh, to the Mansfield paper :
We had some great sport looting the farm-houses ; we
lived on pork and fowls all the time we were out, and any
amount of oranges and watermelons for dessert, but we
are not allowed to touch any personal property, such as
money and jewellery. I reckon I will he an expert thief
when I return to Australia, as a man has to look out for
himself over here, and “nick” everything he
hands on.
No wonder some of the returned contingenters are
a bit slow in getting to work again in this country.
“ H.P.” : A phase of the S’African war hitherto
unnoticed. Writer knows at least one c-on-
tingenter whom the awful mess over there has
practically ruined. Before the “ call to arms,” he
was a hardworking young fellow, not afraid of
anything from wood-jamming to pick-and-shovel
work. Now, though back six months, he point-
blank refuses to work. Loafs on his parents, with
a turn amongst the rest of his relatives, for a
change. He was not “invalided home,” either,
but came back bigger and stronger than when lie
went away. There are probably many such. An-
other legacy for us.
* * * *
Two high-class young Maoris, one a Uni-
versity graduate and barrister, were lately refused
admission to the new baths at Rotorua, on the
score that they were “Natives.” When beautiful
phrases like “the Noble Maori,” “No Race
Feeling in Maoriland,” “Fellow Subjects of our
Beloved King,” come into contact with concrete
facts like the above, it’s bad for the phrases.
* « • *
Premier Seddon had an anxious time at
the Rotorua shivoo. There were Maoris there
from all parts of the country. Tribes that had
regularly eaten one another met in amity for the
first time in their history, and when the waipiro
began to flow there were indications that the .Took
might see the biggest scrap in recent Maori his-
tory. Seddon had a Maxim battery sent up just
to show the Maoris how the thing could shoot,
and when it arrived the detachment took it down
to the lake and made the water bubble and hiss
under the rain of bullets. The Maoris said
“ Ouwhee ! ” or something to that effect, and took
the hint. Since then there has been a regular
epidemic of pneumonia. It was raining most of
the time, and when the Maoris went back to the
mat and the blanket after several years of the
unpicturesque, but serviceable, sac suit and hard
hat, they caught colds by the hundred. A good
many deaths have already resulted —sneezed to
death to make a ducal holiday.
* * * *
The other s ; de of the story. Maoriland
Public Works Minister Hall-Jones, who refused
to give up his hotel-rooms to accommodate some
of the York suite, says he has occupied the rooms
in Searle’s hotel for years, and it was not pro-
posed to turn him out until after he had been
assured he would not he disturbed, and when it
was too late to get rooms elsewhere.

From the Undertaker's Review :
The undertaking trade is unique in being the only one
that has cause, from business, apart from political, mo-
tives, to regret that war has actually broken out. ... We
are somewhat inclined to ask if some scheme could not l>c
devised whereby the relatives of tiiose who fall in battle
might have the privilege of securing the transport and
burial of their loved ones to the home of their birth,
where, with such honors as are their due, they might be
laid to rest on their native soil.
Just think of it! —thousands of coffins that weren't
ordered for the loved ones who fell in Africa !
The thought might make even an undertaker sad
Just contemplate the calmness of the proposal
that shiploads of corpses should be carried from
Africa in order that Mr. Ghoul may have a good
time ! Yet that is what happened as regards the
Phillipines. Present scribe saw at San Francisco
a transport which had brought back from the
islands in one cargo the bodies of 700 American
soldiers!

MORNING AND EVENING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Happy eyes a-glint with laughter,
Cherry lips a-tune with song,
Feet a-music making after
Golden bubbles with the throng I
Morning is the time for singing—
All the day before us there ;
Evening, leisure hours for stringing
Beads of sorrow with a prayer.
Eyes grown bright with passion burnipg,
Pouting lips a-glow with youth,
Hearts a-throb with wine-fed yearning,
Seeking more for faith than tr uth !
Evening is the time for pleasure—
All the night before us there ;
Morning—just the time to measure
Stray strands of the best girl’s hair.

* * * *
Sergeant-Major De Passey, who went to
S’Africa with two S.A. contingents and while
there was made a lieutenant for excellent service
in camp and in field, has been reduced to his
old grade again by the Stay-at-Home Review
veterans of the Commandant’s staff, because they
do not consider him “toney ” enough to associate
with them. There is intense indignation in Ade-
laide over the matter, especially among the men
who fought with and under De Passey, who is
looked on as much better fitted for the duties of a
commissioned officer than many of his titular
superiors.

* * * *
One of those Victorian factory-owners
referred to by G. H. Reid in his election cam-
paign as employing six boys to one man, was
heard from t’other day in South Melb. He only
employed five boys to ten men, but the law said
that was three too many, and invited him to say
why he shouldn’t be fined. His explanation
showed him to be a close student of the remark-
able gentleman with the eye-glass and the Yes-
No. He said one of the three was office-boy, one
printer’s boy, and one fed the dog. The Dry Dog,
no doubt.

“Vulcan”: Wrifceswre “ the early-closing
farce in Sydney ” :—
Called at the optician’s t’other Wednesday (half-holiday)
for repairs, but finding they could not be done in .the
morning said I’d call next day. “ Oh, call this after-
noon,” says he. “But you’re closed,” said I. “Well,
the door is,” he replied ; “ but you can knock ! ” A self-
satisfied Government fondly pictures opticians’ men
enjoying a hardly-earned half-holiday, nevertheless.
Know, also, of a shoddy-drapery house where about 20
employes (mostly young women) work from 9 a.m. till 6
P-m. at half-a-crown per week. Out of these daily nine
hours they are supposed to have one hour for lunch, but
if they get 20 minutes they are lucky. Recently they
have been preparing for a “ sale,” which obliges them to
stay back till between 9 and 10 p.m. They will be kept
at this for two or three weeks while the sale is on, and
A TELEPHONE INCIDENT.
for a week or so after it is over. For this extra work
they receive not a penny extra pay—they are not even
given “tea-money”—they are not given even tea! A
simple sum shows them to work about 70 hours for half-
a-crown—6o half-pence— think of it! not a half-penny
per hour! How the unfortunate girls exist after paying
for fares and a 7 o’clock pie occasionally is as great a
mystery as the pie itself. Of course the police are down
on this sort of thing with both feet—witness the quantity
of old-woman-who-employs-only-herself that is run-in for
selling the surplus banana to a greedy youngster after
hours. Don’t look further than the State of N.S. Wales
if you again want harrowing details of sweating and
sweaters.

A Bulletin correspondent:—
Although the N.S.W. Early Closing Act is now in force
the amount of “working-back” that still exists unde-
tected by the law is really disgraceful. One of Sydney’s
leading costumiferes keeps her girls working till 10 and 11
p.m. during a busy season, and even grumbles at giving
the extra tea-money—sixpence—while she hauls in a big
cheque from a fashionable customer anxious to attend
festivities in a new gown. Most of these girls are afraid
to complain for fear of losing their chance of a livelihood
—not that the salary is enormous either, for one of those
girls, after two years’ experience, is now getting 6s. a
week.
Translation of a Melb. Chinese suicide’s
farewell letter: —
I tell you, like diving in water and dying, I go out of
the door and have no resource. Who cares about my
body ? I cannot avoid seeing Yim Wong (the King of
Darkness) during this month. . . . Who would think
that in this land I would scatter my family ? At first I
anticipated to do well in this country. Who could know
it would turn out like this? At home every day was
well; after leaving home every day was adverse. ... I
was foolish to come to this gold country. At home I was
pure and in repose. Tell my father and mother not to
grieve. Heaven caused me to be a male. Heaven neither
forgives nor pities me. 1 now die, and it is no matter. . .
My bitterness and sorrow are hard to bear.
Tread gently on your vegetable-John ; you may be
killing a poet.
“Yril” : The terrible infant has often
been written up; what about the terrible adult ?
I was at a “swagger” little afternoon tea in
Brisbane lately, and the talk ran on gypsies
and fortune-telling. Said the hostess: “My
' father’s first wife and my mother were
great friends. Shortly after the first
wife’s marriage my mother, then a
mere girl, was visiting at her house.
Well, one afternoon, father and
mother, whilst walking, were met
by a gypsy woman who wanted to
tell their fortunes. Mother would
not have hers told, but father did.
The gypsy said : “You will marry
the pretty lady who is with you,
but death will part you.” They
told father’s wife, who was aw-
fully amused. But three months
after father’s first wife was killed
in a carriage accident, and he
himself died five years later.”
Everyone was impressed with the
yarn, and said, “ How extra-
ordinary.” Then the query of a
small, soulful lady, with big eyes,
fell on the assembly like a large, cold
door-key dropped down the spine :
“ And tell us, dear. Was the gypsy
right ? Did he marry your mother ? ”

Jtfo writteu examinations as a test of
fitness for promotion, the following remark
by a N.S.W. Public School teacher may point
a moral: “Some fellows,” he said, “are too
mean to tear up a book, but I’m not. Why, I’d
tear one up that cost a guinea, if I wanted some
bits of it in with me.”
Lawyer Grey, the Geelong forger, em-
bezzler, fraudulent trustee, and general humbug
in general, has been sentenced to five years’ hard
labor on one charge, which sentence was seemingly
intended by the Judge to cover all the charges
that might be brought against his fallen brother-
in-the-law. The punishment doesn’t fit the crime
nicely and easily—in fact, it is an absurdly tight
fit, yet it represents an advance in the direction
of comparative justice for roguish solicitors. And
considering that Grey’s crime was quite an old-
established fact before the Vic. Crown Law Dept,
got shamed into issuing a warrant for the man’s
arrest, the result is more satisfactory than was
expected. There is now a bare possibility of a
Victorian member of the legal profession being
hanged if he commits not less than, say, six
murders without extenuating circumstances. But
perhaps any murder by a learned amalgam would
he in itself an extenuating circumstance.
• * * *
Broken Hill has had a lady barber for
about a year. She was “off-sider” for hubby,
and now carries on the business with apparent
satisfaction to herself and numerous customers.
Sydney Catholic) Press (its machinery
was blessed by the Cardinal when it started) is the
latest paper to follow John Norton’s example of
printing sensationally-“spicy ” matter in the good
old guise of Moral Reform. “Girls and their
Boys! Company-Keeping ! The Road to Ruin!”
is the enticing heading of an article in that paper’s
current issue (23/6/’l). It contains a graphic
description of the doings of the girls and their
“boys” when spooning on the benches in the
parks. “The endearments common to sweet-
hearts,” moralises this consecrated purifier of the
social atmosphere, “follow as a matter of course,
and with the kisses and caresses the sweet bloom
of maiden modesty is blown away.” “If it ends
at this it is bad enough,” continues the Con-
secrated Press, “but in too many cases
this is only the prologue to many shameful
chapters. ... So they sit on the benches
in the darkest shade for hours at a
time. Human nature is only human nature.
Time, place, and opportunity are leagued against
virtue.” No doubt all this is true enough, but
why in the name of Morality does the Press
linger so over details calculated to fire the imagi-
nation of the girl still burdened with that “ bloom
of maiden modesty,” and make her curious as to
how and where she can have it blown off while she
waits ? This sort of godly literature is hardly
calculated to subserve its ostensible object. The
Bulletin will presently have to start a really
smart religious paper—one that’s not too trans-
parent.

• * * *
M.L. pressmen have a healthy Journalists’
Institute composed of working pressmen and
editor-proprietors. Recently Wellington branch
carried a resolution to register the Institute under
the Arbitration Act—which means that disputes
with employers can be referred to a tribunal
presided over by a Supreme Court Judge for
compulsory settlement. This fell like a bomb
upon M.L. newspaper proprietors. Dunedin
branch threw out the same resolution ; but
Wellington says that if Auckland, Christchurch
and Dunedin don’t fall into line it will register
on its own.
A “She-Cat” sends from Melbourne thi»
good-humored and pointed letter: —
Dear Bulletin.—Some of your contributors appear to
enjoy anathematising the “ she-committee ” of Melbourne
Women’s Hospital with more vigor than veracity—to say
nothing of charity. It seems almost a pity to deprive the
writers of their cherished delusion that the “ tabbies ” in
question devote a day (some of them more) per week to
the work, solely with a desire to “ gloat ” over the mis-
fortunes of their erring sisters. Imagine experiencing
this joy weekly for 40 years—as some of the committee-
members have done I The committee’s principal offences
appear to be : Ist, the questioning of applicants for ad-
mission ; 2nd, the alleged “ branding ” of unmarried
mothers. Now, the most searching of the questions are
(as enclosed form shows) stipulated for by the Govern-
ment as a condition to the payment of the annual grant.
Also, as the applicants are far in excess of the available
accommodation, great care has to be taken that the most
indigent are not passed over in favour of those with
“ brass ”in their pockets and on their foreheads. And,
even with all the questioning, the institution is fre-
quently imposed on by those well able to pay—to the
detriment of those who cannot. As to the alleged
“branding” of unmarried mothers, there is really
nothing of the kind. But, as the Hospital was primarily
intended for married women, it is but fair—now that
single women are also admitted —that the former should
still have their wedded prefix on their cards; especially
as they constitute over two-thirds of the total number of
patients. At all events, the “Mrs.” has been thus used
for 44 years, without objection from the inmates them-
selves. _ Also, any attempt to deprive the married women
of the indication of their status would unquestionably
result in a violent counter-agitation. Briefly put, the
case stands thus. We cannot honestly label the un-
married mother “ Mrs.” and we have no right to deprive
the married one of her proper designation.

“C.H.R.” discourses on an essential part
of the Royal show :
Have studied industriously all the leading illustrateds
re the coming of the Duke, and conclude that the two
angelic beings in the twin pot-hats who sit in the out-
rigger behind the sacred ear are the only objects who
have hitherto implicitly obeyed the camera fiend. No
matter what the scene—what the point of view—back,
front, sides, above or below—there sit those cheru-
bim, undismayed. The Dook in one page looks like an
old broom-handle surmounted by a cochin-china rooster,
and the Duchess, something between a book-case and an
old-fashioned hour-glass, while the princes and lords and
things are mere splashes in the dead of the surface. Yet
there they smile out of the waste, that lovely pair with
the white pants and the tall hats. They change not.
They have been to Brisbane—this scribe saw them. They
are on the water now ; they will he in Hobart soon. I
wouldn’t give a straw to see the show if York sacked
those two angels in the hats. They must be twins, or
shave with the same razor.

Thus a writer in the Ayr (Lowerßurdekin,
Q.) Chronicle, describing a race meeting:— “The
attendance of the white plantation-hands was
very small but the colored gentlemen were very
much ‘ en evidence,’ and it seemed as if half those
present were Islanders, Chinamen or Japs.” Now,
the Ayr Chronicle is a pro-alien-labor organ and
therefore not likely to exaggerate in this respect.
The L.B. district is run for the benefit of a
southern syndicate of sugar capitalists which some
years ago squashed the efforts of the small farmers
to obtain a central mill there, and has been busy
ever since filling the district with Asiatic farmers
and laborers, weeding out dangerous white demo-
cratic voters, and otherwise acting in the manner
of capitalism run to seed. It was this district
that supplied the latest victim to leprosy—a white
child of 10 years who contracted it by frequenting
the Chinese shops of Ayr and the Kanaka quarters
on one of the sugar farms.

Writes “ Smilax " re the great Auckland
fire:—
The Grand Hotel, Auckland, unlike most N. Island
buildings, was not constructed of wood. It was one of
the finest houses in Auckland, and was a substantial
brick structure described as having every modern con-
venience, and presumably the usual precautions against
fire, and was by no means “an ancient wooden public-
house.” The occurrence seems to be another proof that
modern buildings, unless watched properly, are no safer
than the old ones.

FLIES IN OINTMENT.


The Charm of MARK FOY’S is the Price.
One of the keynotes of Mark Foy’s success is the working
up of the Men’s and Boys’ Outfitting Trade m
To young gentlemen in offices, students, apprentices, and others,
who wish to present a good appearance, we call special attention to
MARK FOY’S SMART SUITS for BUSINESS YOUTHS.
Every youth on entering his business career desires to be smartly
dressed, but the stern necessity of rigid economy with small
salaries necessitates care in spending the coin. . . .
