284 GEORGE STREET, SYDNEY.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, July 1, 1901.
Mr Dear Artn*t,—
Cares still infest the day, I suppose, but after
eight they fold those supposititious tents, and the
remainder of the night seems to be filled with
waltz-music. A lot of women I know began the
winter with physical-culture exercise, but dropped
it in favor of the fun and physical exercise to be
got out of a combination of a male partner, a band,
and a waxed floor. All the cycle clubs have given
balls. At some of these, I’m told, there was not
a single wall-flower, so well did the committees
manage the output of tickets. Bagpipes skirled
at tlie Scottish ball, uniforms whirled at the
military hops, girls danced with each other at
innumerable school-dances, most of the Some-
bodies and Anybodies have had dances in
their very own ballrooms, or they’ve driven
Pa mad by clearing out the dining-room
and hustling its furniture on to the ver-
andah. There’s been a “ Bachelor Girls’ ”
dance that sounds attractive at Needs’
Rooms, and the Artists’ Society Ball was a very
“hairy” one. The hairiness was in the coiffing
of the women—not the artist-women, mind. And
the male artists’ hair was short as even Lord
Roberts advises soldiers to wear theirs. The
long-woolled male in the Society’s pasture was not
an artist at all. Ever so many women who go to
artists’ dances and “at-homes” make frights of
themselves —on these occasions only ; they think it
Art-like. The idea is, of course, to please the
artists, who, they note, rarely put sleek-haired
women in their pictures.
The most important charity ball this season was
the Fresh Air Leaguers’. The charity will profit,
but would have profited more had Society not
spent all but its housekeeping money in Duking.
It was a young people’s ball, and the reason why
there were some wall-flowers was that the letter
inviting a drove of the Brooklyn’s officers mis-
carried or was delayed. So 14 young men waiting
for that letter and growing desperate as the night
waned, at length took headers—into their bunks.
Dressing at the F.A.L. b ill was good, and there
was a great display of single-stone diamond neck-
laces. Mrs. See’s was pre-eminent in sparkle.
Miss Littlebridge Harris’s was hardly a glitter
behind. Mrs. J. T. Thompson were turquoises
with her white-and-gold gown. Miss Ettie Hor-
dern’s eau-de-nil frock had a beautifully and won-
derfully-sequined bodice. Mrs. Richards and
Mrs. Cohen, the treasurers of the ball, were hand-
somely gowned. Misses Richards wore Paris
frocks of white lisse. Mrs. Caro (hon. sec.)
wore an oyster-white brocade, the flowered pat-
tern outlined with silver sequins.
Red is coming in with great eagerness. So glad
are people now to say “Get thee behind, black
and violet. Hail red ! ” that even a red rose isn’t
a horror to them as of yore. Parisian women
are wearing re 1 co its with colored skirts. At the
Brooklyn “At Home” there was quite the latest
Paris material on a returned wanderer —hailstone
grenadine—black grenadine with a red spot.
Another guest, who wore a fawn tailor-made with
green sdk vest, her hair tightly forward from the
back of her head, and on it a flat to jue with ripe
cherries, was pronounced by an authority on dress
to be on the very top rung of smartness.
York gossip continues to dribble me-ward. Of all
the (unconsciously) humorous printed proposals for
welcoming the Yorks t ie most comic was made by
a man in Auckland, M.L. Now Auckland city
has a fine view of its fine harbor, and this genius
suggested that, as the Op'iir steamed up the
water-way, all citizens should repair to their front
windows, and with the family looking-glasses
flash a welcome to their Royal visitors. M.L. is
very loyal—loyal to the dregs—of May’s tea.
She “was induced ” to attend a bazaar at Welling-
ton in aid of a hospital for incurables, and whilst
there drank a cup of tea. The cup and saucer,
spoon, and dregs were later put up to auction, and
after brisk competition kocked down for £5 ! I
wonder if M.L. will let its royal-welcome-obelisks
go at ss. each. I trow not! But one of the most
curious bits of Yorkiana is a table of the number
of times the Duke applauded the pro-
gramme of Sydney State Concert. It may
interest the performers. Item 6. Over-
ture (Tannhaiiser), Sydney A. Orchestral
Society, was rewarded by the Duke with eight tiny
slaps on the buck of his left hand. All the other
items were applauded by gently tapping the palm,
and perhaps we can judge which item pleased
H.R.H. by the number of taps. Item 7. Song.
“The Message,” Mr. P. Newbury, It! tips. Item
8. Song. “Creation’s Hymn,” Belle Cole, 12
taps. Item 10. Song. “Let me wander not
Unseen,” Dolores, 10 taps. Item 11. Hallelujah
Chorus, Philharmonic, 9 taps. The Liedertafel
when singing shut out the compiler’s view of the
Duke, therefore he couldn’t record the applause
they got. Haven’t some persons a mania for
noting trifles ! You remember the witness at
the last big Sydney railway-accident inquest who
had, with a stop-watch, timed the speed of every
one of his railway-jourpeys, and solemnly eutered
up the results in a note-book !
This isn’t quite all an age of harsh commercial-
ism. When the Oruba was leaving on her last
voyage two brothers made a long and painful
leave-taking of each other on the wharf. He who
was remaining behind was dying of consumption.
The liner drew out from the wharf, and the
departing brother, leaning over the side, strained
his gaze on the sick one until the agony of pro-
spective and, possibly, final parting, got to such a
tension that he jumped overboard and started to
swim for the wharf. Ropes were thrown to him,
and he was hauled in safely and his luggage
hastily collected and dropped into a lighter. One
is glad to think about a thing like this not write
about it.
“Good Clean Bedds-Sixpence a Night.” Until
& week or so ago that intimation used to hang
over a George-street North door, and I, being
Duchess worn, feel inclined to hang round its
memory because it is so infinitely removed from
things royal and ducal. The extra letter in beds
seemed to make them worth sevenpence a night.
You could be fed on the premises to four
courses, wine included, for ninepence.” Probably
after the wine you’d see three d’s in the bed.
Anyhow, right at the zenith of the York-worship,
bailiffs entered this Hotel Cecil, and I made the
acquaintance of the “clean bedds ” on the kerb-
stone. Stalactites a yard long, of dirty grey
fluff, drooped from the wire mattresses, the wood-
work was mined by white ants, weird moss was
on the blankets, strange lichens on the pillows.
How different from the “ bedds” on the Oplnr.
A good story is told of Lord Ranfurly, Gover-
nor of Maoriland, in connection with the recent
reception at Auckland, of the Duke and Duchess
of York. To get the grounds around Govt.
House trim for the expected royalties, a gang of
prisoners was employed and they did the work
smartly and well. Coming on the scene of their
operations as the time for the arrival of the Duke
and Duchess drew near, His Ex. was
prompted to “ treat ” the unfortunate men, m
honor of the occasion. He suggested “lemon-
ade,” but the warder in charge could not see lus
way to consent. It was barred by the regulations.
Then (so the story runs) said Ranfurly, “it must
be tea and chicken. Take them inside.” In they
went and had a real good meal, and they drank
the health of the Duke and Duchess and likewise
of Gov. Ranfurly in—well, they say it wasn’t tea,
and it wasn’t lemonade, but they felt all the
better for it and none the less loyal.
A group of women doctors are trying to found a
Worn in’s Club here. I have not heard what is the
proposed entrance-fee, but the originators want not
less than 100 members, and suggest locating them-
selves in the Victoria Markets. There’s a club
there already, the Pencil Club is it hight, and ltd.
Dekker of the E. Nisws is President. Pencil Club’s
rooms are decorated even to the skirting-boards
with terrific sunsets and sea scapes, and there s
a pair of human thigh-bones hanging on the wall
amongst portraits of members and painted
plaques. A horrid legend is to the effect that the
first owner of the bones was killed and eaten by
liungry fellow-pencillers. If you don’t pay your
subscription of sixpence a week for, say, 2b weeks,
the Committee (who all paint pictures and write
verse) draw caricatures of you and put them on
the wall—that’s a hint for you to leave. As many
man as women belong to the Pencil. It is
never locked. You can go in at any hour
of the day or night, and light the kerosene-
stove (it lights with a loud bang) and read books
and papers, and find a bottle of quince jam fast
growing a green beard, some pickles, and a eol-
leetion of sonnets in male hand writing addressed
to the foundress.
There’s a lovely club—a woman’s club—just
started in London on quite new lines. Members
have their faults told them “ frankly and without
prejudice.” In short, the members try to make
each other see themselves as others see them. I
have not heard if they come armed to the meet-
ings.
Duchess of Cornwall’s Women’s Welcome Com-
mittee has made a big success of its money-
gathering. It has £l4OO in hand, and the
money will be spent on a useful purpose—the out-
patient quarters of the Queen Victoria Hospital.
Mesdames See, Hugh Dixon, and Beaumont are
the now vice-presidents elected by the last half-
yearly meeting of the National Council
Miss Rose Scott read a paper on the “Economic
Independence of Married Women,” urging that a
married woman, being a working partner in a
firm, as it were, had a right to a share of the pro-
fits large enough to render her economically inde-
pendent of her husband. The theory is good,
but amongst the working classes, and even in
the houses of some struggling professional men,
the income is too small to bear a division
that would make the wife independent. Miss
Scott further urged that a wife should have just
as independent a position inside the home as she
bad prior to her marriage. But the great propor-
tion of women have no economic independence
before marriage. They are dependent, or partly
so, on fathers or brothers or other relatives.
On Saturday night I had a look at the play that
fills the Lyceum stage, and at the audience that
packed the auditorium. Audience was of the
family-party type. Grey-haired mother with
white or pink woollen “cloud” over her Sunday
“ black.” A married daughter, and an unmarried
one with her “boy.” All had the invisible
“Tattersall” brand. You knew they had been
syndicating their shillings for years in hope of
winning a big sweep. When the two race-horses
“with the clothes on” appeared the family
groups went wild with joy, yet a string of real
racers returning from a gallop on the tan wouldn’t
stir them. Live horses or a real cab in painted
landscape does exhilarate the sporting family
group. Miss Duggan’s pink satin evening gown
and smart race-dress made the women of the
group more than ever eager to win a sweep that
they might walk in such raiment. But her low-
cut'bodice, worn when returning from a drive in
the afternoon, gave them pause. Perhaps a
woman who owns the favorite for a classic race
may wear a cross between an evening and a street
gown in daylight. Leading man Willard only
wants a black cat on his shoulder to complete his
likeness to Awful Boothby’s Dr. Nikola.
The Broughs are rehearsing “Second in Com-
mand ” I had a look at a bit of the rehearsal and
the play seems full of “go” and epigram and
rollick and mess-unifonn. Miss Vaughan one of
the new importations, sister of the noted Rate, is
a stately-looking figure, as tall as Mrs. Brough.
Miss Chatteris is a pretty, little, symmetrical
irirl grey-eyed, brown-h&ired, with a smile that II
make the stalls abject. She has been playing two
years in London. Her first part was Gabriel in
the “ Three Musketeers.”
A writer in a Melb. paper, in mentioning the
engagement of Miss Dey, 8.A., to Dr. Stuckey,
of Adelaide, remarks that 31 of Sydney University
women graduates have got married. Uiat leaves,
I believe, about 119 who are still happily un-
married. Something over 150 women have been
mangled through the Uni. since it was opened to
them about the middle of the eighties, I hey
seem to marry their kind, too —the woman with a
degree marries the degreed male. But I know a
woman in an oversea province, one who passed her
exams, with all the colors of the spectrum, who
married a township small butcher. That is, his
business is small. He’s the other way, and red.
She keeps the books, and nearly killed her eldest
infant by feeding it on the strongest beef-tea. I
suppose having stacks of beef at hand was a
temptation.
University Sports Club ball will be held at Pad-
dington Town-hall thisevening. Special late trams,
trains, and boats will run. To-night also there s
an entertainment by the Hasluck Dramatic Club
at Queen’s Hall in aid of Children’s Hospital.
The late Mr. Thomas Dalton was one of the
most unostentatiously charitable of men. For-
tunately for those who need help, his widow is as
generous as he was. It was Mr. Dalton’s youngest
sister who married John Redmond, the Irish
M.P. and orator. His niece married William
Redmond. Mrs. Mark Sheldon, of Neutral Bay,
is the only daughter of the Daltons. One of their
sons married, a few months since, a Sydney belle,
Miss Annie Nugent.
Sydney is full of Abrahams (Abraham was the
great pioneer squatter) concentrating at the
sheep-snows and sales. I saw Loyalty 111, the
other day, at Goldsbrough-Mort’s rooms. He’s a
fearsome-looking ram belonging to Mr. Gibson, of
Tasmania, and has been sold for something under
500 guineas. His coat is quite rain-proof, so
dense is it, and he carries wool everywhere but on
his horns. I suppose he has eyes, for he offered
to rusli me, but I couldn’t see them. His ewes
are also clothed right up to the horns—no evening-
party business about them. Shearing these pure
merinos must be like shearing a concertina. All
around you’ll hear such phrases as “ very even,”
“great density,” “exceedingly fine,” “elastic,”
“good clippers,” from men who have put blue-
over-alls on their city clothes, and whose methods
are not quite so streaky as those of the other
Biblical squatter, Jacob.
For the first time on record an Englishwoman
of title has married an American. Lady Sybil
Cuffe is the ewe lamb of the Earl and Countess of
Desart, and her bridegroom is Mr. Bayard Cutting,
who is secretary to American Ambassador Choate.
Lady Sybil, in her portraits, looks a good-com-
plexioned, abundant-haired, thick-lipped damsel.
You’ll see now how the practice, once started, will
spread like spinifex.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ ‘ Pagliacci ’ and ‘Cavalleria’ brought together
an enormous crowd (June 30). The ordinary first-
night d.c. gang, which at any time includes
fashionable, flashy, foreign and Israelitic elements,
was on this occasion augmented by frugal souls,
tempted by the double-bill. Also in evidence
were shoals of Philistines who came expressly to
pose as musical experts on the strength of recog-
nising ‘the Intermezzo’ without prompting.
“Mascagni deserves gratitude for composing
said chestnut. With no more ear than a hen, one
can’t help getting to know the Cavalleria ‘ Ave,’
thanks to street-musicians, etc., and familiarity
with an ‘lntermezzo’ is worth airing. _ Saturday
night I ventured to look at the time, as I
would have done even had an archangel descended
in person to conduct the ‘ Hallelujah Chorus.’ A
woman sitting near by apparently lived in a spot
unaffected by last-tram considerations, so, in
reference to the faint click of the watch-lid,
she hissed ‘ Hush ! ’ Later she volunteered in
kindly patronising tones: ‘ That lovely thing they
were playing was the Intermezzo—the ‘ Ave
Maria’ from ‘Cavalleria Rusticana.” Anxious
to live up to my ignoramus aspect, I re-
sponded: ‘Crikey! Was that the “Havey
Mariar ! ” I’ve often ’eared of it! ’ Whereupon
the high-toned hearer nudged her friends and
spread the story. Soon the whole group were
craning their necks to simper at their verdant
neighbor.
“Now that mourning is done with, theatres
look gay again. Coiffures grow more stuffed-out
and substantial than ever. On Saturday the
Cliveden widow, a study in white, black, dia-
monds and smiles, brought along her tall, willowy,
second daughter, who wore a long pink-silk sash
witli her trailing white frock.
“ Dani makes an attractive Pagliacci, and first
night got a real ovation for his ‘On with the
motley ’ solo, when he mourns and rages, and
finally steals his neck-frill to his weeping eyes.
The new hand, Signorina Yaccari, not a strong
singer nor a great beauty, looks rather a dainty,
graceful little Nedda. Silvio, her strapping
peasant lover, is represented by Oattadori, wno, in
‘Aida,’as de Benedetto’s black father, was wont
to hustle his child too roughly. As Nedda’s wooer,
lie’s still so boisterous that he has the air of embit-
tering embrace-moments by trampling poor little
Columbine’s toes. Nervous Cannonieri is rather
nice as Harlequin, the would-be peace-maker. In
‘Lucia’ as the unloved bridegroom, he was so
agitated when led round in white-satin to be in-
troduced to the wedding guests that his black
bottle-brush habitually stood on end, and every
second step he doubled over on the high-heels of
his white shoes. The * Cavalleria ’ tragedy seems
more squalid than that of ‘Pagliacci.’
“Dani, as Turiddu, is rather a conceited, dis-
agreeable young man. Somehow he and Sapio
between them raised very little enthusiasm, though
Sapio looks well as Santuzza, a dark tragic sort ol
girl far too good for her plump flirtatious fiancA
The latter, like most Italians, fits very naturally
into the role of ‘Mammy’s bov,’ though people
laugh when he sings ‘Main ma i Mam ma ! ’
“ The Madden contingent, with Col. Campbell
in command, amused Her Majesty’s audience on
Saturday night. They occupied the manager’s
box, and were so eager to look round the house
that their glasses were fixed in position when the
tardy lights at length flared up in the interval.
Five fat figures seated in a row, with glasses
glued to their eyes, were focussed by an amused
audience. Heretofore, the Waxellency’s party
have sat in the front row of the circle, turning
cold shoulders to the rest of the frou-frouers. But
it is evident the box gives more satisfaction, for,
to judge from Saturday’s show, the Family of
second importance in the State is, after all, very
human and deeply concerned about more ordinary
people.
“ A bookie’s wife took the palm for smart
dressing. She had on an eye-smarting black frock
of moonlight spangle, covered with a shell-pink
cloak of mirror-velvet, ruched with black chiffon.
A diamond-winged bird quivered on the top of a
newly-tipped golden thatch.
“ Conjuror Bertram gives us more cry and less
wool than Dante and others. Those young, live
magicians, I suppose, annexed present visitor’s
best tricks and strung on novelties as well, but the
current plump wizard is richer in patter. Posing
as a ladies’ man, he wheedles some shy she-
spectator into naming a flower for the mango
trick. When the victim blurts out ‘ red rose ’ and
her tormentor responds in honey-balm tones ‘my
love is like the red, red rose,’ everybody laughs,
for by this time ‘my love,’ i.e., the sufferer, is
like a red, red tomato. The prettiest trick is done
with big rings, ‘wedding rings worn by diaphanous
dervishes,’—such nice loose wedding-rings, the
very thing for modern matrimony.
“A French girl, Mdlle. Du.et, delightfully
formed and colored, was the belle of an excep-
tionally choice lot of charmers at the i rank
Stephen musical ‘at home’ t’other day. The
lady in question is dark as a Spaniard, with melt-
ing black eyes, crimson lips, and the tones of a
pomegranate in her complexion. Many years in
Paris have transformed the lovely child Albert
Park used to know into a brilliant woman of
fashion—although now not much more than
twenty-one.
“A Toorak maiden electrified her Sunday
callers recently with a whirling, swirling, curling
pirouette by the Dartos. A Jewish dame out
Domain-road way has set a craze for Sunday
shivoos, and Ping-pong helps to wile away the
slow Sabbath hours for the smartest.
“ A choice bit of club scandal is being whispered
here. A young ‘Johnnie’ who arrived from
London some two years ago has been eased, by
fair means or foul—ho asserts the latter—of
£lO,OOO. The card-room of the club to which the
smart set introduced the new chum, was the place
in which, some years ago, a well-known territorial
magnate, since dead, lost £30,000 in one night at
unlimited 100.
“ Two cousins of some wealth joined their for
tunes and their hands at the altar of matrimony
last Wednesday. The pair were Miss Eily
O’Brien and Mr. Sydney Spark. The hitching-
up took place at St. Francis’ Church. Congratu-
lations and champagne followed after in Menzies’
Winter Lounge. The young couple in question
owe their handsome incomes to the extensive
estate and provision of the late Pat O’Brien, of
‘ Kinkora.’
- “ A swell Society is troubled in its mind, and i>
trying to think over a difficult situation.
gentleman it knows very well has a pretty partm .
to whom he seems devoted, but the Society ha.
an inkling that this attractive person is not a
bona-fide wife, but a substitute for one in a far-ofi
country. The respectable Society has gone so far
as to demand inspection of the marriage-lines,
and pending their production, it proposes to eye
both parties with a glance of grieved suspicion.
Grieved suspicion is a thing that such societies
seem to get on very small provocation.
“The latest grievance of the smart Melbourne
girl is that the other cats don’t rush to congratu-
late her when she becomes engaged. Several
instances in point have recently occurred. A
complainer points out it can’t be —dislike foi
the man, and the matter of an honest silence fol-
lowing on aversion, for she has reached her third
engagement, and records the same conspiracy of
speechlessness on the part of less successful char-
mers. Only old ‘ married frumps ’ write felicita-
tions, and the ‘ snags ’ who want to be asked to the
wedding. ‘ The smart crowd one must ask don’t
write,’ confesses one of the Chosen.
“ A party of girls interested in the tale of the
untamed King O’Malley went up to the House of
Reps, the other night, and instead of the brigand-
swashbuckler type of man they expected to see
were disgusted to find a quiet-looking Democrat
with spectacles and a respectable beard and a very
pious, almost Quakerish, expression. He wae
wearing on this occasion his ranter’s look, was
calm in demeanor, and only interjected once.
Occasionally he shook a Minister or a member by
the hand, addressing him as ‘brother,’and always
being cold-shouldered, or palpably endured. No,
King O’Malley is not of the stuff that dreams (or
darlings) are made.

A Letter from Archdeacon Lin gar d.
Christchurch, N.Z.
February 25th, 1901.
Dear Sirs,—
I have much pleasure in testifying to the value
of Warner’s Safe Cure.
Nearly four years ago, after having tried several
remedies for diabetes without success, my medical
men consented to my trying Warner’s Safe Cure.
At that time there was no prospect of my living
above a few months. At first I took six table-
spoonfuls a day. I began to improve in health
immediately, and was soon able to undertake my
regular duties. During the last four years I have
never been without the Safe Cure in the house, at
times reducing the doses to one per diem, and
occasionally going without altogether for a month
or so.
At the present time, though not altogether
cured (at my age I can hardly expect it), I am
able to undertake the arduous duties of a large
parish single-handed and without much fatigue.
I have no hesitation in saying that my present
fairly good state of health is due to Warner’s Safe
Cure, and I shall ever feel grateful that it came
my way.
Yours &e.,
EDW. ATH. LINGARD
Archdeacon of Akaroa.
Messrs. H. H. Warner and Co., Ltd.
Warner’s Safe Cure is a specific for the cure of
all diseases of the kidneys and liver and for all
complaints arising from a disordered condition of
these vital organs, such as indigestion, rheu-
matism, gout, neuralgia, jaundice, biliousness,
ansemia, debility, most blood disorders and
Bright’s disease.*

old Queensland friends. The Doctor has no male
heir. His four sons have all gone —three of them
suddenly.

Batman, who was a nephew of John Batman, of

and was full of interesting reminiscences. He
leaves one son and three daughters.

Generally rumored in Adelaide that
Justices Boucaut and Bundey (latter is in ill-health)
purpose early retirement from the Bench.. Judge
Boucaut is wealthy and much interested in horse-
breeding. As regards Bundey the difficulty is in a

1904.
Ex-Speaker Bent, since his re-election to
Vic. Assembly, has once again become a promi-
nent figure in Melbourne dress-circles, where he
sits, ostentatiously and noisily munching masses of
lollies, for three solid hours. If he goes out in the
intervals it is to get more lollies. The man who
eats lollies rarely drinks.
The Republican soul of that good demo-
crat, Premier Jenkins (S.A.), revolts at the
thought of the coming grovel before the Duke,
and lie makes no secret of the gladness with which
he will see the Ophir sail away. Jenkins is a tee-
totaller, so that he shows no enthusiasm even in
signing orders for champagne and other patriotic
essences.
J. P. Stow, S’langdon Bonython’s pre-
decessor as editor of Adelaide Advertises, now
S. at Pt. Pirie (S.A.), has lately been so ill
that his retirement may come at any time. Stow,
the son of a Congregational parson, is reckoned
among the choicest yarn-spinners in his province.
Though so long a journalist, he gives people found
drunk on the Sawbath double fines.

children ; Scarfe is dead ; and Allerdale Grainger is going
to London as State Agent—and I am still making a so-
called living by writing paragraphs and rhymes.

The Hon. Eric Lascelles, brother of Vis-
count Lascelles, and of the Earl of Harewood, who
has been travelling incog, as a showman for years,
died recently at Cheltenham, Eng. “Viscount
Lascelles ” is a title not unknown in Australia.
In 1883, John Dow, alias Lutterell, alias Edward
Lord, travelled N. 9. W., under the title as a Com-
missioner authorised by the British Govt, to
inquire into the condition of the prison popula-
tion. He “had” many worthy families in the
West before he was found out.
Mr. Kater, M.L.C., who gave £lOOO to
Prince Alfred Hospital, Sydney, the other day,
lost £6OOO worth of notes very softly at Cherry
Tree Hill in the early days. He left the bag of
notes in the coach and walked up the hill, but
Lowry and Foley, bushrangers, were on the alert
and collared the lot. One of the bushrangers,
when caught, gave up his share of the plunder,
but the balance was never recovered. Mr. Kater
was then a clerk in the A. J.S. Bank.
Premier Peacock has found his Free-
masonry of service in strengthening his reputation
as a business manager. Peacock is Grand High
Cockalorum of the Vic. Freemasons, and his way
of presiding over Grand Lodge meetings and
putting things through somewhat astonishes the
men who have had previous experience of two in-
competent stammerers in the chair. William
Clarke was for years a farcical figurehead of the
Craft, and after him came hum-haw Brassey. It
goes without saying that the spry Peacock shines
by comparison with his predecessors and is pretty
much of a Grandmaster of the Masonic vote in
Parliamentary elections when he speaks for a
particular candidate.
The Queensland Ministry is determined to
keep the status of Governor as it has been.
Attorney-General Rutledge has just said that the
country will be satisfied with nothing less than a
nobleman—as if there could (on the average
sample submitted to Australia) be anything less.
Chief-Justice Griffith, however able, does not
satisfy the snob-craving to rub sides with some-
thing born in the purple. And, indeed, Griffith
himself is willing to stand aside so that he also
may rub.

Pastor John Alex. “Zion” Dowie, of
faith-healing renown (recently acquitted in Chi-
cago on a manslaughter-charge), was well-known
in Melbourne at one time, and used to deliver red-
hot sermons in Fitzroy Tabernacle? He preached
annually a sermon on the Melbourne Cup—or
“ Cup of Devils,” as he called it ; and used to ex-
hibit in glass vessels on his platform table a col-
lection of tape-worms, cancers, hydatid cysts, and
all manner of horrors, said to have been removed
from the human body by the agency of prayer.
A Fiji pressman, writing to N.Z. Times
denouncing Gov. O’Brien, cites, as an instance of
the latter’s want of courtesy, his treatment of G.
H. Reid. When the latter was on his way home
from the Jubilee Drunk, the boat touched at Suva,
and the Dry Dog man went ashore early that
morning to call on O’Brien. His Ex. was still at
breakfast when Yes-No’s feet were on the door-
mat, and he, not wishing to let Reid see him with
his mouth full of buttered toast, sent out his
secretary to explain, and say he couldn’t receive
George. Whereat, according to the pressman,
the latter “performed” considerably. This, by
the way, is one of the few recorded occasions on
which Reid was too early, instead of too late.
Tasmanian obit. : On 4th June, William
Dry Landale, 53, son of Dr. Landale, long ago of
Launceston, and nephew of Richard Dry, one of
the “patriotic six.” W.D.L. wits, a few years
ago, the best and most graceful horseman in his
State, and amongst the first flight in Australia.
Some of his feats on Echo are still talked about.
Poor Landale went the pace in youth, and in
middle age was financially troubled. His body
was found at the bottom of the Cataract quarry ;
it was the most sensational leap he ever negoti-
ated, and his last. His two brothers both found
sad ends. David walked down into Waratah
Creek, Bischoff, on a day’s prospecting, and was
heard of no more. Clarence blundered over the
edge of Launceston wharf,, and his body was
dragged out next morning. -Another brother,
Dick” also dead, was well-known in the old days
with the sports who are no more.
When the Duke of York was at Wellington
(M.L.), he made a speech about the “hero of
Waterloo,” after whom the place is named. As a
matter of fact, Wellington never pretended to be
a “hero,” or thought much of Waterloo. His
pet battle was Vittoria, which he believed
caused the collapse of Napoleon in Germany
in 1813. The consequences of Vittoria were
thus infinitely greater than those of Waterloo.
In fact, had Wellington not fought, or had he
been beaten at Waterloo, Napoleo i had still the
Prussians (only half-beaten at Ligtiv) and the
Austrians and Russians to dispose of, and sooner
or later he would have been crushed, because he
had no second army to fall back upon.
The poor wretch who runs the Way back
Advocate deserves a lot of our pity, but not all ;
save some for the other po >r devil who grinds out
the uninfluential city rag. U landler, the Adelaide
press-worker, who is serving one sentence for
criminal libel, came out for an airing to receive
another six months, t’other day. “ I didn’t see it
before it was printed,” he said. “ I was out can-
vassing and collecting, and then I had to do the
type setting. There wasn’t any manuscript ;
Andrews, who ‘ worked ’ the information up, set
it up himself. We do the work between us. I
have tried to do two men’s work and failed.” In
the circumstances it seems absurd to inflict a
heavy fine as well as imprisonment.
The late missionary Chalmers, of Papua,
was always inclined to be jocular concerning can-
nibalism, yet lie was killed and eaten after all. On
being asked (writes a correspondent of West-
minster Gazette) if he was not afraid of being
put into the pot himself, he replied, “ Not a bit.
I once went so far as to ask the cannibals of Bald
Head Point, in the centre of the sago-producing
country—most agreeable fellows—if they had any
such intentions regarding me. The chief, with a
smile (not a hungry one), said that they did not
care for white man. They had tried him, but he
was not good. Of course,” added the missionary,
“ they might prefer white man to no man at all,
but, as a matter of fact, ‘ long-pig ’ orgies are not
common. They are, like plum-pudding at Christ-
mas, very good once a year.” Mr. Chalmers
stuck up manfully for the cannibals. “ They are
really a fine set of men,” he said. “ Their houses
and temples are remarkable structures. It is in
the temples that the banquets of human flesh are
held. When a number of bodies are taken, they
are brought into the temple one by one. At the
end of the temple is a dark recess, and into this
each body is taken. After the obsequies, the
body is dragged out into the open, the head is
carefully chopped off, and the body is cut up into
pieces, and thrown into the pot for dinner. The
flesh on the skull is very carefully taken off, until
the skull is clean. The hideous trophy is then
handed over to a skilled artificer, who carves and
paints it. It is then hung up on a peg in the
temple.”

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. —Always give, in ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., exact date and exact
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
“ Bull’s-Wool ” has a dream :—
Dreamt t’other night I was appointed manager of
“Dead Finish’ Station (owners, “The Australian Broke
and Reconstruction Co.”) and received the following
letter from the manager of the co. when I took charge,
viz. :—
PRIVATE.
Dear Sir.—You have been appointed manager of our
“ Dead Finish ” Station at a salary of £100 per annum. I
give these hints for your guidance and policy during your
term of management, viz. :
1. Beware of “ Big-head.”
2. Write plenty of letters to the co. Be as vague in
your statements as possible, taking the Governor-
General’s speech to Parliament as a model. Fill your
letters up with “ Garden Stuff.” The Directors have to
make some semblance of earning their fees and your
letters will give them something to talk about at the
“ Board ” meetings.
3. When you are asked for an estimate of “ Receipts and
Expenditure ” don’t write and say that it is all bosh and
that not being Wragge you can’t foretell a season, but
make receipts as small and expenditure as bigas possible.
We may remember it at the end of the year, and search
for it in the dust-heap for comparison with results. If
results are better than you prognosticated, the directors
will be pleased that, through their efforts, they have done
better than they thought they would. If results aren’t up
to your estimate, you will have to take the blame. You
are there for that purpose. You might write me a
“ private ” letter, telling me what the station is really
like. I can then tell the directors what 1 think. It will
add to my reputation, if it turns out as you say.
4. Do exactly as you are instructed by letter. Remem-
ber that the directors and myself, with the help of sundry
under-strappers in this office, and with some assistance
from the London board, are really managing the station.
You are there to take the blame if any of us make mis-
takes. Directois don’t like responsibility; neither do I.
The directors like all the credit; I like some, too, so there
won’t be much left for you You are called “The
Manager ” ; that’s your share.
5. Don’t buy whisky and have it put down in the in-
voices as flour. If you get drunk, don’t let me hear of it.
I get a bit full at tile club, with the directors, at times ;
but we are made of different clay. If I should come up
to the station, and we get tight together, that won’t
count, but it would be wiser for you to let me get drunk.
Then you can attend to me while I am suffering a re-
covery.
6. Don’t ask for a rise in salary. It might interfere
with a rise I want myself. If you really want a rise, you
had better come down and marry one of the daughters
of our principal director. I married one myself.
7. If you want “stud rams,” write to the “Board.”
They will write to the London directors, who always
purchase the studs, knowing the least about it. If they
turn out well (the stud rams I mean) the Board will take
great credit. If they are a failure, very likely you’ll get
the sack.
8. If you don’t get dismissed for one of my mistakes, or
for one of the directors’ mistakes, or for showing that you
know more than either of us, ami become old in our ser-
vice, or a blithering idiot from trying to do what half-a-
dozen people tell you about one tiling, and if you haven’t
married our boss director’s daughter, we will most pro-
bably sack you and put a younger man in your place.
Old beggars, as you will be . then, should have saved
enough money for their latter years.
Finally, remember the importance of your position as
manager of one of this co.’s stations (the co. has only re-
constructed once). Entertain freely (at your own
expense). The co. must be well represented. And do
things handsomely (they may want to float some more
debenture-stock).' Also, don’t forget me. Tell everyone
what a clever man I am. It might do me some good.

“Gipps”: People are continually being
abused by the press and magistrates for
neglecting to cut down anyone they may find
hanging by the neck, but I can
sympathise with those people,
knowing it to be a thankless task
to interrupt any free and inde
pendent elector in the execution
of himself. I am “ off ” cutting
down the half-hanged since
my last experience. 1 was put-
ting up at a small Gfippslaml
bush pub., and arose early
witii the intention of having
a dip in the creek. On my
way to the secluded hole I
ran up against a fellow-
lodger, a long, hairy, red-
whiskered man, hanging by
a rope from a light-wood
limb, still squirming vigor-
ously. He was a morbid
man, with a bestial appe-
tite for poisonous whisky,
and he had eaten my
share of the wild duck at
dinner the day before. How-
ever, I am of a forgiving dis-
position, and I whipped out .my
knife and let him down with a
flop. He lay on the grass awhile, gasping,
and considering the situation. Then he
arose, and I prepared to receive in a proper
spirit his offers of eternal devotion. “ You
,” he said, “ why the —— don’t you mind
your own blank blank blank business '! ” Then
he struck, and my nose suddenly felt like a
lump of mud hurled against a brick fence. He
kept it up for two minutes, and I went to bed for
three days. Ere I could get about again the
morbid man with the red whiskers had gone off
in my best trousers, in the pockets of which were
£2 7s. in cash and my marriage-certificate.

“ Hennidon ” sends a tough and pic-
turesque discourse concerning Luck : —
People say there’s no such thing as luck. Well, I once
saw a man lose his life in inches of water ; and I have
known a woman die from the effects of a pin-scratch.
But, on the ether hand, in Queensland last year I fell in
with a N.S.W. man who called himself Frank Drum.
Jointly we purchased a vehicle and all attachments, and
together set out from Longreach for Rockhampton.
Camping en route one afternoon during a heavy thunder-
storm, Drum was leading the horse to water, when a flash
of lightning struck man and beast, the animal being
killed outright, but the man escaping with two hours’
insensibility. A fortnight later he was bitten on the arm
by a black snake. After hacking the wound about with
a blunt knife, sucking it, and pouring a bottle of brandy
into the patient, I set out for the nearest doctor, some 14
miles off. What with driving a poor, knocked-up horse
and the task of keeping my mate awake, it would be hard
to say which of us, on arrival, needed medical attention
the more. After searching divers pubs., at each of
which Drum contrived to force down a couple of brandies,
we at length found the medico. The wound was duly
attended to, and another bottle of Hennessy found its
way into the snake-victim, who next morning declared
himself O.K. A month later found us gully-raking on an
abandoned goldfield. Opposite the tent was a tunnel
running through a hill. Into this Drum betook himself
during my illness, and as he did not return in reasonable
ing hard by, and we set to work to unearth the mangled
remains. Had shifted tons of stuff, and were half-dead
when from several feet above a voice star-
tied us. Drum had burrowed a hole between the debris
and the solid ground, and was anxiously waiting to be
extricated. It appears that he displaced a prop, brought
doum several sets of timber, and was for the time buried
alive. After that I resolved to part company with the
death-dodger, and accordingly, while he was mooning
about the old drives, I packed my belongings and
sneaked off like a thief in the night. Last 1 heard of
Drum was from Croydon, where he had had three fingers
a,l< !: ! j u . ear . .( I . llore . or * ess ) blown off by gelignite, his
lte bein ' kllled instantly,

SETTING IN.
DEADBEAT : “ ’Struth!
We’re gettin' a real

“ Serp ” : Was once
walking in the Vic. bush
with a pal when I spied a
tiger-snake 18in. in front
of him. Sang out to him,
and his eye fell on the
reptile at the same
moment. He was carry-
ing a sharp tomahawk
in his right hand, and
made such a vigorous
jump that he sliced off
the fleshy part of his
right heel, leaving it
hanging by a shred of
skin. Threw some saplings on the snake to
hold it while I bound up the wound, but the
“tiger” got away before I had finished.
Then carried my 14-stone friend on my back 3
miles to the nearest township. It was the hardest
tramp I ever had.
SPORTING INSTINCT.
COLONEL (to outpost on the veldt): “Have you seen anything?”
PRIVATE 48,499 (formerly kangaroo-shooter at Warramine): “Yes, had a shot at two small
animals, and thought I saw a rabbit."
COLONEL : “D the animals; did you see any Boers?”
P. 48,499 (thoughtfully): “Boers! I think I did see two or three hundred.”
COLONEL (bursting with rage): “ Which way did they go?”
PRIVATE 48,499: “ Dunno, but I could swear the rabbit went that way.”
COLONEL: “-,-,- !!-!!!"
The Hot Lakes (M.L.) News drew
Government’s attention to the necessity for an
apparatus for lifting helpless invalids in and out
of the local baths, whereupon an active and in-
telligent department forwarded gear capable of
lifting five tons. It would be difficult in that
sulphurous country to find a suitable founda-
tion for such machinery. If it is put into opera-
tion we may expect to learn per cable that the
mass of cog-wheels and chains has fallen on the
chest of some invalid bather and cured him of all
his earthly ailments in one act.

cards and writes letters to herself and ostenta-
tiously opens and pores over them in a public
place so that other minxes mav see and envy.
Which reminds me of one Bill who did the same.
Tom and he were rivals for the hand of Florrie
Blank. The old people professed great friend-
ship for Tom, and much contempt of Bill. Xmas
came, and Bill got a written invitation to dinner
from Florrie’s old man. Tom was so disgusted
that he went up country. A year later, when
Florrie had gone off with a drover, he found out
that Bill had written the invitation himself.
When last heard of Tom was looking for Bill with
a genuine invitation to a lively time by the road-
side.
* * * *
Mr. Cheeseman, of Brighton, Melbourne,
has lately been to the New Hebrides, and writes
thus, in Brighton Southern Cross :
Missionaries receive £2OO per annum ; all goods are de-
livered to them free of freight, so all goods are as cheap
to them as though they lived in Sydney or Melbourne.
They have free and, in most cases, very comfortable
quarters, although the completeness of their homes de-
pends largely on their own exertions. They also travel
free of fares, including their wives and families. ... A
missionary does not ask leave, but goes away for a trip
whenever he feels a change necessary, usually to one or
other of the Australian States. . . . Mission stations are
all established on the coast, mostly within a few yards of
the beach. This system is adopted for safety. ... No
mission stations exist in the interior of the islands, where
the natives are as primitive as when the islands were
discovered. The missionary conducts a morning school
for all ages, commencing at 6.30. The school is dismissed
at 7.3 C ; after this the missionary’s duties appear to end
for the day. Each station has a staff of native teachers,
who are paid a very small salary, and appear to do con-
siderable work lying at a distance, etc. On the whole, I
think the missionary has a very good time ; very different
from those who did the pioneering work 40 years ago.
The coast tribes are civilised and safe. There is plenty of
work still to do in the interior, but those parts seem to be
approached with much delicacy. The trader is more
venturesome ; his lot is anything but enviable. In most
cases he is the only white man for miles. Most traders
have from one to three native wives, but they are not
valued as domestics. The missionary is surrounded with
fervent adherents. The trader has to attract workers by
tact and fair dealing. He must be devoid of fear, as he is
always in danger. He deals direct with the hill tribes,
who collect and deliver cocoanuts, etc.

A DROUGHT TIME BAPTISM.
MISS MEEKS (to Parson Meeks, just returned
from out-back christening): “ How did the
ceremony go off, papa?”
PAPA : “ Very well, but for the fact that there
was not a drop of water in the town.”
MISS MEEKS : “How did you manage?”
PAPA : “Oh, we put him through by dry-
blowing.”

“ D.S.A.D.” : Re ignorance of bush chil-
dren : A bushwacker brought his two youngsters to
spend a couple of days in a moderate-sized town.
Dad and youngsters strolled the street in the
evening, when one kid, looking up, said in sur-
prise : “ I’ll be hanged, Bill, if they’s ain’t got the
same moon as us ” ! Yet the bush children haven’t
a monopoly of ignorance. A newly-arrived Scotch
family took up residence here, in the Queensland
backblocks. One day a passer-by informed the
Vl’Dad that there was a telegram for him at the
post-office some miles distant. Dad told off son
Sandy to go for it. Something was puzzling
Sandy. Hte hung about the door a while. “I
thought I telt yer to gang awa’ for the telegram,”
said Dad. Then Sandy inquired if he should tak’
the dray, or if the horse was strong enough to
carry a telegram.

Re station “ tucker ” :
Employes themselves are greatly to blame that an in-
different ration is becoming general “ out back.” I know
nothing about “ inside.” Any shearers’ cook will tell
you that men “off the track” will, for a week, gorge
themselves savagely, and waste good food scandalously.
After that their palates demand dainties, and the waste
increases, if anything—jam on “ brownie,” sauce and
pickles with their meat, and so on, so long as the boss
will stand it. Sauce and pickles they consume by the
half bottle at a time as a “ tonic ” or “ appetiser,” while
plums, currants, dried fruits, and white sugar they use
as lollies. Should the cook (who is, in a measure, re-
sponsible) remonstrate he is promptly voted “ a cow ” or
“smoodger.” The bosses, generally speaking, are only
“ strugglers ” on a little higher plane than their hands,
and, soured by ill-fortune and dyspepsia, “ nark ”
quickly. Then comes the “ mean tucker.” By-the-bye,
“ Station Manager’s” list (8., 6/15/’01) wants altering for
these parts ,N.W. N.S.W.). Prices here higher. During
the drought of the past seven years we have been paying
up to £lO per ton carriage from nearest railway
“ Bungarra ” : A man in a W. A.
goldfields town, recently, having a very
bad attack of d.t’s, was promptly
gathered in by the police, and chained
to the log. During his sufferings at
the log the prisoner contemplated sui-
cide, and the police-officer, being an
obliging fellow, released him from the
log and inquired how he wanted to die.
“I want to shoot myself,” said the
man. “Right!” said the officer;
“ but, to save the State expense, you
had better dig your grave first, so that
you can sit in it and shoot.” The
prisoner dug a 6ft. grave, of the right
width and length, a few yards away
from the log, and the officer handed
him a gun loaded with blank-car-
tridge. The sight of the gun sobered
the other, and he decided not to die
just then. When finally released, he
walked up town ; and, thinking it a
pity to waste such a fine new grave,
he offered to sell it to a friend whom
he met. Then trouble ensued; and
now it isn’t safe to offer to sell a
grave to either the “suicide” or his
friend unless your own is ready.

“C.H.M.” : Re “Phoa’s” query
(8., 9/3/1) : The “conjevoi” plant is
identical with the “ conjcbah ” of this
N.Q. sciub, and is, as “ Bhoa ” says, the
most nourishing of the foods used by
the blacks. When prospecting and
short of flour I have always fallen back
upon it (after soaking it for 24 hours
in running water, then crushing on
stone). It is insipid, but adding a little
salt makes it palatable. “Phoa”was
lucky he did not swallow the raw “ conje,” or he
would have suffered intense agony.

A Girl : When the water-hyacinth came
down in such masses during the last fresh in Bris-
bane River, looking like a wedge of solid green,
several enterprising boys swam out to it, and
actually walked over it, quite oblivious of the fact
that had they by chance fallen through, their fate
would have been akin to that of a man who falls
through ice.

“ Granje ” : Have often noticed in this
(Goulburn) district heaps of grass-seeds collected
by ants (“D.8.” B. 13/4/T). The ant is a very
small one, and the seeds tiny, round and yellow.
The collections are made only in autumn, and I
always thought they were “ stacks ” prepared by
the farm-laborer ants for the home-workers to
stow away underneath.

“ Xerxes ” : Re “ Tom Collins ” (B.
G/10/’00). Only one “ irresponsible paragrapher ”
(as Tommy calls us) appeared to regard the pro-
curing of fire by friction as an easy matter. T.C.
says that the process is “among primitive races
an arduous and momentous work.” So it is with
any race. Further, he says that “no man, black
or white, can produce ignition by any means
hitherto set forth in these columns.” Which is
rubbish, because fire can be produced by hand-
friction. T.C. might as well argue that a cold
convex lens could not generate heat enough to
produce fire, or that spontaneous ignition is im-
possible. Morever, he admits that the feat is
“barely possible”—and then denies its possi-
bility !

Simple Child : (1) You are correct as to the B ier fran-
chise. (2) The Boers offered naturalisation and a vote
for both Houses after seven years’ residence and, of
course, taking oath of allegiance. The (Jutlanders who
ran the agitation wanted a vote without being naturalised
audwithout taking oath of allegiance—a thing granted in no
civilised country on earth. British residents who became
naturalised would, of course, have been liable as Trans-
vaal citizens for military service against Britain in case of
war. So they calmly demanded the privileges of citizen-
ship without its duties—they wanted to eat their cake
and have it too. (3) Just before the war the Boer State
schools were teaching 11,552 pupils—in fact, a larger pro-
portion of Boer than of N.S.W. children were attending
school. Of course, schools were taught in Dutch— you
don’t expect to find N.S.W. schools conducted in French
—but the English residents mostly sent their children to
English schools, of which there were a large number....

Tainbo : In almost all Greater Britain marriage with a
deceased wife’s sister is legal, and the offspring of the
marriage is legitimate. This state of things came about
because the British Crown gave its assent to the measures
passed by “ colonial ” Legislatures. But Britain still re-
fuses to legitimatise in Britain the offspring of such
marriages. So the«person who is legitimate in Australia
through the action of the British Govt, is illegitimate
in England through the inaction of the British Govt....

EFFECTIVE.

2ND DITTO: “Er- yes. Two-column ad.!”
Cabled that the new French submarine
boat “ Narval ” has been 12 hours under water,
but that after the first few hours exhaustion and
nausea attacked the crew. Something wrong
with the air-supply apparently ; but when the
chemist has got over that and found a way to
generate properly-diluted oxygen there will be
another difficulty to be overcome—keeping the
boat right side up when under water. When im-
mersed, by pumping certain tanks full, the boat’s
centre of gravity is raised, and its equilibrium
becomes “unstable' 1 so that the least change in
the disposition of the weights, or even a slight
movement by one of the crew, may suffice to turn
the boat on its side, or capsize it altogether. A
submarine boat, by the way, was tried in 1777 by
the Yankees, who, by its aid, came near blowing
a hole in the bottom of the British warship Asia
in the Hudson.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.

The Ways of Many Waters
Collects the dashing ballads and v erses of sailor-men’s
and wharf-men’s life which E. J. Brady has contributed
to The Bulletin and the Sunday Times, Sydney. It is
the first attempt on the part of an Australian verse-
writer to chant the life on the sea and the margins of
the sea ; though there is one horse-poem—the story of
“ How Jack Bowlin Steered ‘ Jones.’ ” The volume is
bound in buckram, with gilt top, in a size that will
into a pocket without squeezing; and the price is ss„
post-free.

The Chieftain!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Chieftain roamed his coral isle,
Where he (they said) alone was vile,
A buck robust and handsome ;
He cared no whit for Heav’n or Hell
Or souls that fall (as Preachers’ tell)
For want of Preachers’ ransom.
His limbs he stretched beneath the sun
What time his wives (he’d twenty-one)
Prepared his yams and taro ;
At times he’d rouse to hunt the boar,
Or take a stroll along the shore,
Or whale a winsome marrow.
And when the mission parsons came
To save his soul from fire and flame
With praise and prayer and Bible,
He laughed to think the Land of Gin
Should call his Land the Land of Sin,
And grinned beneath the libel.
And if at times he gripped a club
And wrathful drove his tribe to drub
His laws upon the st anger ;
Twaa but because he clearly saw—
The white man in the heathen’s maw
No heathen might endanger.
He practised well his simple code,
He paid his friend the debt he owed,
He fattened and he filled him ;
His foe ho treated much the same,
He brought him down in battle’s game,
And ate him when he’d grilled him.
His morals were a trifle loose,
He knew no legal marriage noose,
He brooked no legal bridle;
And, when his peaceful fit was o’or,
He waded ankle-deep in gore
To please an ugly idol.
At last he died —the burly chief ;
And when, amid the tribesmen’s grief,
The Preacher pale decried him,
They .split the stupid Babbler’s skull;
They made a feast and fed them full,
And laid the bones beside him.
P. LUFTIG.
Cecil Rhodes seemingly distrusted women.
No females were employed on his estate. He speci-
ally favored colored labor; his faithful henchman
was a Cape nigger named “ Tony.” “ Tony ” com-
bined in his own person a variety of offices. He
was not only valet, superintendent of domestic
affairs, messenger-in -chief and confidential agent,
but he was also cook. The “Colossus” lived in
continual dread of poison.
Chambers’ Journal gives the millionaires,
with their millions, as follows :—Beit, 100 ; Chang,
100 ; Robinson (Rand), 80 ; J. Rockefeller, 50 ; W.
Astor, 40, &c. There are 46 American million-
aires with £451.000,000 (about £9,800,000 each),
and 24 British millionaires with £350,000,000
(about £14,600,000 apiece). Protection may make
bloated capitalists, but they seem to bloat just as
badly under Freetrade.

The monster public deputation in favor of
Co-Operative coupons (introduced by Mr. Duncan
Gillies M.L.A.) established an Australian record .
over 500 people attended. The speakers were’
limited by Mr. Peacock to five, but they rep-
resented an enormous number of the public. Mr.
Nelson (representing 2000 workers in the railway
service) said : “This coupon system has taught us.
thrift, and brought pleasure to a great many
homes.” Mr. Davis (President Bnckmakers
Association) said that “ The public realised that
it is the only system by which they can get dis-r
count on all small purchases.”*

With what shamelessness does that Sydney
daily paper raise the sectarian issue whenever it
thinks any direct gain possible ! How adroitly
does it paragraphically sharpen the snake-tongue
of the clerical bigot !
PREMIER PEACOCK.
A native born he seeks applause
From Bull and all the Jingo clan,
And boasts his British pride because
He dare not. he Australian !
“ Every right-thinking person views with
horror any attempt by a newspaper to ridicule or
libel those placed in high authority, and more
especially, ” &c., &c. — So'emn pronouncement by
lvarde, M.L.A. (Vic.).
Sixty-four brave and broad-minded mem
bers of Vie. Assembly voted for the expulsion of
Findley ; seventeen against. This is what Aus-
tralia pays for the fatuous tradition that she is
more “loyal” than England is.
Alex. Wilson, the gentleman who wants
the Federal capital planted at Canoblas (N.S.W.)
is still lecturing on the advantages of building the
new city at a place where everything going to or
from the only available port will have to be
hauled nearly 4000 feet up a mountain range and
then about 2000 feet down again.
pavid Syme says the recent South Yarra
contest for Vic. Leg. Council cost the four candi-
dates £12,000. Which means that about £OOOO
flowed out of one pocket, the other three cutting-
up the remainder. Well, there is some satis-
faction in the knowledge that Moneygrub lias to
pay a stiff price for political distinction.
“The Federal Minister for Defence has
discovered that 353 men out of a total strength of
1200 were either discharged, or dismissed, or
deserted from the Australian standing
That’s right: an Australian standing army won’t
stand. But, a Leaning-Against-a-Post army would
attract nearly the whole male population.
Showing how hopeless the cobwebby Tory
cause is now in Maoriland, Lewis, the wealthy
member for Christchurch, has “chucked the job.”
He leaves politics, he says, because so little in-
terest is displayed in his party, and because, at liis
pre-sessional meeting, no one turned up to hear
him denounce the iniquity of Seddon, and the
grievous sin of accumulating a surplus.
JPctori m Premier Peacock, at the bidding
ol liis bitter enemy, the Argus, had Labor-
inember Findley, wild gave a general support to
the Peacock Grover.nnent, expelled for an offence
which the whole House knew well enough was
committed by another man. And it is quite pos-
sible, as things are going, that a Tory, with the
aicf of the Argus, will secure Findley’s seat, and
help to vote Peacock out of office.
Australia should be thankful she has got
Federation. In the big Chambers representative
of the whole country there is a strong, upstanding
manliness, in marked contrast to the slobber of the
Siate Houses. All through the Senate and Reps,
there breathes the spirit of “ Australia first, and
DIDN'T KNOW IT WAS LOADED .
here and there are members not slow to voice the
sentiment outright. When one compares the
speeches in the Senate during the past few weeks
vrtth those in Vic. Assembly on the night of
Kndley’s expulsion, it is liardly possible to believe
tn&t both Chambers were elected on the same
franchise. Even the Sargoods and Zeals who blot
to© Senate would have been more j udicial intone
rtuvn the alleged Eemocrats who sit in the State
Houses.
Despite all his King-grease, Premier Pea-
cock’s heart was plainly not in the job of expelling
Labor member Findley from the Assembly, for
* indiev was a supporter, and the Premier knew
that the seat would very probably fall to the
Opposition. Peacock would gladly enough have
let the thing slide, or taken some middle course ;
if lie hadn’t moved the expulsion the Opposition-
leider would have done so, and Peacock would
e ther have had to follow him, which would have
been an awkward position for the leader of the
House, or to oppose him, and he hadn t the
courage or the conscience.
The acceptance of the motion of Higgins,
M.H.K., that the Federal Parliament should en-
deavor to persuade the States to hand over to it
the sole control of Labor legislation, so that it may
make uniform factory laws, and laws relating to
hours and wages, throughout the Commonwealth,
is a good sign. Such a move would mean extend-
ing to the six States the best existing factory law,
and even it would probably be improved. Premier
Barton accepted the motion cordially, and it was
passed without a division. The Federal Parlia-
ment is living np to its repute as the most Demo-
cratic legislature Australia has yet seen.
Maoriland gives additional security to
wage-earners by its new Wages Act, which pro-
vides that persons letting contracts are to
retain and be responsible for 25 per cent, of
total contract price for 80 days after com-
pletion of contracts. If the contractor’s
employes are not paid they can get this
fund attached for wages. The small,
mean contractor, who used to collect
the price of the contract and then
bolt, leaving his workmen to lament
about their unpaid wages, and
wh.o, by this economy, was able
to undersell the honest contractor
that paid his men in full, is practic-
ally extinguished by this law.
Au election sign. N. S. YV.
Premier John See took with him to
Grafton 80 pairs of blankets.
Brisbane city corporation h-ul a £40,000
overdraft before the Duke came, and through the
expenditure (quite illegal, it would seem) of some-
where between £7OOO and £BOOO on decorations its
debt was well on to £50,000 when he left. There
is no idea of levying an extra rate and paying the
bill. The grovel of the parents is visited on the
children. Thirty years hence, probably, a new
generation will still be paying about £3OO a year
interest because its dead progenitors once saw a
duke.
Ishinael Grainger’s seat in S. A. Assembly
goes to Verraii, a Miners’ Association man, run as
a Laborite, though independent of the city party.
The miners’ vote went solidly for Verran ; the
farming vote just as solidly for Allen. So while
at Moonta (miners’ centre) Verran polled 1018 and
Allen 240, at Alford (typical farmers’ centre)
Ven-an polled 0, and Allan 74. Much the same
thing happened at Gawler (Barossa) a fortnight
before; and the result has been to revive the
grumbling about the S. Australian plan of cutting
up the country into two-member electorates.
As a cheerful tergiversatin' Sydney D.T.
is monumental. Where, during the current
N.S.W. State elections, its large-t/pe “Selected
Liberals ” were being opposed by unselected
“Liberals,” the latter were daily anathematised
for their traitorous persistence in ignoring “the
Party ” selection, and were assured that ghastly
failure awaited them at the poll. When, however,
one of the allegedly-despised candidates is scared
off the nomination-paper by these predictions the
D T announces the fact next day with pleasing
remarks about the suddenly-magnificent character
of the disheartened and bluffed-ofif candidate,
“who would doubtless have commanded a large
vote,” &c. Wonder how the scratched politician
regards these post mortem eulogies !
Pointed out by a Sydney
dailv paper , correspondent that
Lyne’s Federal Public Service
Bill would simply make the said
Public Service a preserve for rich
men's sons. “What man of ordi-
nary means can afford to educate
his boys to an age of 16 to 18
years for the great reward of £4O
per annum? Yearly rises of £lO
bring the Civil Service aspirant
up to £l6O in 12 solid years. A
boy goes in at £4O per annum, and
has to maintain a decent appear-
ance, and, probably, pay board.
The thing cannot be done ! We
will not get the smart youths to
aspire to starvation in this glorious
employ. Why not, “it is further
asked/’ adopt the system of the
Imperial Government, in. say, the
Mint, and give a lad, after exam-
ination, a subsistence, £lOO per
annum, and so let the sons of
respectable middle-class people
have a chance.”
Apropos of Federal Defence,
there is an agitation in Melbourne
just now foran Irish regiment. Said
agitation wants to be squashed
flat; and the same flattening process wants to be
applied to existing Scottish regiments. When
Australia gets its Federal force together there
will probably be N.S.W., Victorian, Queensland,
&c., regiments, just as there are English, Scottish,
Welsh and Irish regiments in Britain. But to
subdivide our army according to the divisions of
our own country is one thing; to further sub-
divide it according to the divisions of a country
at the other end of the globe is something else.

Without in any way seeking to commit the
Government to any particular system, he might say
that he felt strongly in favor of the decimal system
of coinage and the metric system, not only for Aus-
tralia, but for the whole of the British Empire. If
Australia followed Canada in the steps she had taken,
it might be reasonably expected that an added force
would be given to the movement. Englishmen doing
business with foreign countries found themselves at a
great disadvantage if they did not understand the
metric system. He supported the motion for another
reason.

Since Ballance’s death, Seddmi’s Govt,
has travelled far. The sight of “Sir Joseph”
Ward and “Sir John” Mackenzie is enough to
make the sturdy Ballanee wriggle in his grave.
Strangest of all, Cadman and Walker allowed
themselves to be degraded with a C.M.G.-ship.
Nemesis, by the way, was not long in overtaking
Ward. The day after his knighthood he received
a wire from three M’sP., expressing their sorrow
and indignation at the leader of the Democratic
party accepting a bauble ; and he is likely to hear
more of it before long.
The best point about Bigjoim Forrest’s
ten years’ Premiership of Westralia was that he
strongly believed in the Government carrying out
its own public works. There is strong evidence
that his party will now fall an easy prey to the
Boodle, or private-concessions, crowd. Piesse,
the ex-Minister of Railways, has been elected
leader of the Forrest remnant, and, although in
some respects an able man, he represents all that
is reactionary in politics. Present indications
point to a long, hard fight between the Reform
party and the boodlers, but the latter will be
ignominiously routed as soon as an equitable re-
distribution of seats scheme is secured.
Since Maoriland reduced its inland postage
rate from 2d. told, the increase of business has
been marvellous. The new system has only been
six months in operation, and the first year’s
loss was estimated at £BO,OOO. It promises
now to be only £30,000, and, judging by
the monthly increase of business, the second
year’s loss is likely to be nil. Still it
doesn’t follow, as a matter of absolute certainty,
that quite the same results would ensue in Aus-
tralia. With cheap postage and something cheer-
ful to write about people will write. With cheap
postage and only the drought to write about they
may write—or they may not.
The new Leake Government met the
Westralian Assembly with a total following of 21,
while the Opposition—the old Forrest gang—
numbered 24. So the new Government is in a
minority of 3. Yet the Leake minority represents
a vastly larger proportion of the voters on the
roll than does the Throssell majority. The voice
of the People is with it ; the voice of the Cattle
and the Empty Acres is witli the Opposition. If
Leake can'push through his measure for. the re-
distribution of seats according to population, and
then hold another election, he will probably have
a crushing majority. But the Cattle members
will defeat the Bill if they dare.

Defence Minister Forrest has proudly
published a return showing the number
of troops under the Commonwealth, which
is set down, inclusive of rifle-clubs and ex-
clusive of cadets, as 62,000 men. Big John
altogether omits to state the number of
modern weapons at the disposal of these
troops, 'i here are no magazine-rifles, and a
very large proportion of the anna would be hope-
lessly outranged by the rifles of any invader. The
rifle-club members number about 30,000, two-thirds
of whom are in Victoria ; they are armed with
Martinis, made in the early 70’s. To say there
are G2,000 troops at the disposal of the Common-
wealth sounds very reassuring—suggests, io Jact,
that 200,000 Britisli soldiers would find it difficult
to make decent headway here ; but when these are
reduced to a mob of men armed, some with hope-
lessly-inferior weapons, and the rest with obsolete
ones, it is like reckoning-in the archers among the
effective troops of Chowland.
“Oocy” writes in a dispirited tone of
voice:— •
There isn’t a Government that could be fo ■ I in tic-
tona that would live a week if it came forward with a
scheme of direct taxation (Turner came forward with a
scheme of heavy direct taxation, and earned most of his
scheme, and his Government lived more than five years,
which knocks that prophecy kite-high.—Ed. B.). If the
Victorians had not been on the spree for the six months
since the Commonwealth started they would have seen
the awful financial chaos that must come and come
before long. Listen to what we have done. Interest,
&e., written off irrigation works, about *2,000,000 ; con-
cessions to land-selectors, in reduction of rents and pur-
chase money, say, £400,000 ; Old Age Pensions, over
£300,000 per annum : Boer war, £120,000; Common-
wealth festivities, £100,000; Reclassification of Public
Service, about £05,000 per annum before long. Apart
from this list, some of which, of course, is approximate,
there is the contribution towards maintaining the Federal
Government; and every day Duggan at the Lands,.
M'Culloch at the Public Works, &c., &c., are throwing
away public money and sources of revenue with both,
hands. After the Peacock crowd comes the Deluge.
Mr. Frank Adams to The Bulletin from
Walgett
I find it hard to understand why you advocate the ex,
tension of the N.S.W. Western Division leases, said leases
composing an area of 97,000,000 acres. Might 1 urge you
to give this question further consideration, in the inter-
ests of the rising generation; which, if the proposal is
carried out, will find when it comes to manhood, that it
has been deliberately robbed of its inheritance. The
Central Division lands will he exhausted long ere the
children of to-day are eligible to become landowners, and
when they come to that time, they’ll find the land has
been deliberately put out of their reach until their life is
really over. 1 submit that this extension cry has been
got ui> by banks, financial companies and other monopo-
lists. As to the value of the land, let me cite the case of
Gingie Station (a W. Division Holding of 21)0,000 acres)
five lilies from Walgett, with a frontage to the Barwon
River This run abounds with edible shrubs, and
the saltbush is of the finest description For years past
thev have shorn 120,000 sheep, last year being the lowest
for some years, when only 68,000 were put through,
feel convinced that when Tub Bulletin goes into the
facts it will find that by the extension-of-lease idea,
the rising generation will he robbed of its birthright to
satisfy the landed monopolists and others of that ilk.
The bulk of the Western Division lands cannot be sur-
passed for richness of soil, and to extend these leases at
random will be simply criminal on the part of. those
responsible.
“ Kiwi,” from Maoriland : —
The Federation Commission has finished its “delibera-
tions,” and its report will be published soon after Parlia-
ment meets. Steward, one of the political nominees to
the Commission, addressing his constituents a few days
ago, vigorously condemned the proposal %hat Maoriland
should “ lose her identity,” and made no attempt to con-
ceal his antipathy to Union. .Labor-member Millar, of
Dunedin was more discreet. In addressing his constitu-
ents lie touched lightly on Federation, remarking that, as
he had just appended his signature to the Report of the
Commission, it would be impolitic to express an opinion
on the subject just then. There is no doubt that the Re-
port will be adverse. The members of Commission have,
since their return from Australia, made no effort to con-
ceal their belief that M.L. is destined in the great creative
plan to play a great Lone Hand. The N.Z. Timbs. Pre-
mier Seddon’s official organ, lately published an article in
course of which the editor apprised the public that lie
had been “unofficially” informed by the Commission
that the Australians had simply rushed headlong into
Federation without considering the great question of
Finance.
The latter statement, at all events, is not far from'
the truth. The other alternative, judged by
many past experiences, was to stay headlong out
of Federation without considering the great ques-
tion of Finance. Most of these States haven t
given any serious consideration to the great ques-
tion of Finance for the past ten years anyhow.

A Letter from Deniliquin,
Dear Sirs, —
Tattersall’s Hotel,
Deniliquin, N.S.W.,
28th February, 1901.
Some years ago I suffered greatly from
liver complaint. At that time I was living in
Melbourne, and was able to get the best medical
advice. The doctors all seemed to differ as to
the nature of my complaint, and prescribed
various medicines, which gave me no relief what-
ever. Hearing of Warner’s Safe Cure, I decided
to give up doctors and give that medicine a trial.
From the first bottle I derived benefit, and con-
tinued to improve in health rapidly, until I was
quite cured.
Yours, &c.,
AUGUST NORD.
Messrs. H. H. Warner and Co., Ltd.
Warner’s Safe Cure cures all disorders arising
from an impaired or diseased condition of the
kidneys and liver, such as indigestion, rheu-
matism, anaemia, blood disorders, biliousness,
neuralgia, gout, gravel, general debility, and
Bright’s disease.*
READ WHAT . . .
VITADATIO
. . IS DOING.
HYDATIDS
AGAIN CONQUERED-
Croydon-avenue, Croydon,
May 18, 1900.
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir, —From childhood I have been a great sufferer,
and was treated by eminent physicians for liver complaint
and jaundice. After suffering for 16 years the most ex-
crutiating agony I was tapped by Dr. Fortescue and Dr.
Markey (House Surgeon at Sydney Hospital) in 1871, and
my disease was proved to be hydatids on the liver ; but
with all the tapping my disease grew again, and I was as
bad as ever. About every four weeks I would have a bad
turn, and would be prostrate for twelve hours at a time.
I could eat nothing, and almost lived on bread and water
to see if I could get relief.
Last February I was advised to try VITADATIO, and
glad I am for that advice, as it has thoroughly cured me.
I am better now than I have ever been in my life, and I
will be glad to answer any questions relative to my case.
Yours faithfully,
(Miss) FANNY LIGHT.
For further particulars,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.

Victorians new acting-minor Judge Johnston
had 22 prisoners before him at the recent general
sessions, all charged with larceny or similar
offences. Seven were acquitted, and, in sentenc-
ing the remainder, Mr. Johnston seemed to have
fixed on 18 months as a sort of standard, varying
the penalty here and there in accordance with- no
discoverable principle. One unfortunate, for his
third offence, got four years; another, for his
ninth, the standard 18 months; another, a first
offender, the same ; two others, also first offenders,
and public servants, 18 and 15 months apiece. A
man who descends to petty larceny when in a per-
manent billet at £325 a year doesn’t, perhaps,
deserve much pity ; still, 18 months, in addition
to loss of billet and pension-rights, for the theft of
a few pounds, is somewhat weighty. Then Mr.
Johnston suspended, during good behaviour, a
sentence of three months, on a lad of 16, for com-
mandeering a bike, and made up for it by award-
ing three cumulative terms of a year each, with 27
days’ “solitary,” to a lad of 17, who, after escaping
from Bayswater Reformatory, broke into about a
dozen houses in as many days. Practically, the
lad was a first offender, and might have been re-
claimed had he been sent to the State Reforma-
tory, but sundry J’sP., assisted by his acting-
Honor Judge Johnston, made that hopeless by
“putting him away” for about five years alto-
gether. Evidently, Judge Johnston is a man
with an able-bodied respect for Property and its
alleged rights, but, as a reclaimer of criminals, he
has a lot to learn.

Sleath, absent-minded like the rest of the
warriors, went out to White Cliffs, 60 miles from
the returning officer’s quarters at Wilcannia,
without signing his nomination-paper. The over-
sight was only discovered on the afternoon the
nominations closed ; whereupon Sleath did a re-
cord drive into Wilcannia, over a vile bush track,
in under five hours. The average coach “going”
on the Western tracks is miles an hour, stop-
pages included.
As was expected, no witnesses, other than
mine officials, were present to give evidence when
the Royal Commission arrived in Broken Hill to
inquire into the South mining disaster, and two ad-
journments had to be made in order to whip up a
few testifiers. But the Miners’Association, resolving
that the inquiry was a farce, declined to take any
part in it; and the relatives of the victims inti-
mated that, as they were contemplating civil pro-
ceedings, they would rather not say anything just
yet.
N.S. W. ex-Prernier G. H. Reid, speaking
of the See Government’s recently-acquired steam-
yacht, bought to replace a worn-out vessel for
which Reid solemnly proposed a vote of £lO,OOO
“to supply a new hull ” —he was going to build a
new vessel round the old engines :
The “bargain” reminded him of another. “There
was,” said George, “ a married couple named Mr. and
Mrs. Poodles, and Mrs. Poodles was the plague of
Mr. P.’s life, because she was always buying such wonder-
ful bargains. One day she bought a big brass plate with
the name of‘Simpson’upon it. Mr. Poodles wanted to
know, in the name of all that was reasonable, to what
use this brass plate was to be put. ‘ Well, you know,’
replied Mrs. Poodles, ‘ I hope it will never happen, but
you know, you might depart this life, and I might get
over it, and I might happen to meet a gentleman of the
name of Simpson, and if I happened to marry him see
how useful it would be !’ You see,” concluded the Dry
Dog One, amidst roars of laughter, “the Government
has an ample explanation ! ”
