This is the sort of article you would expect to see,
not in »leading journal of “the freest empire in
the world,” but in the Persian SPECTATOR of the
time of Xerxes, or the Carthaginian Examiner
about the date of the Second Punic War.
From The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent : .. „,
Re Tanquerey of Paris and his world-wide free crayon
portraits" swindle in your issue March 23. Even if a
dozen prominent Australians came forward in response to
your invitation and produce splendid pictures they ' have
received for nix it will prove only thatTanq. is 'Salting"
“down under” as he did London, &c. When the first
complaints arose here Lady Randolph Churchill stood by
T., producing a “ splendid picture of her swollen-headed
son Winston—Winnie for short? Tanq. had salted
the best person in England for his purpose—a fascinating,
pushing, high-society ’Murkan woman. The thousands
robbed since paid a myriad times over for this and other
free pictures, but Tanq. still flourishes alongside the
“ hidden Spanish treasure ” and the _ Klondyke gold-
brick ” swindles. The saddest thing in civilisation to-day
is the fools that abound—ferry-boat loads of fools, ihey
irritate “us honest men,” for we can’t take them
down,” and others do so before our eyes.
Everything that! can be used as an argu-
ment against the Old Age Pensions Act is eagerly
seized by the Melbourne dailies, and leaded and
leadered. The last grievance is that of an old
man who, leaving the shelter of the Benevolent
Asylum for 10s. a week and liberty, failed to
finance his little capital successfully, and knew no
better than to spend 3s. on a room, and the other
7s. on 14 square sixpenny feeds per week, and not
so much as a bit of baccy or a pint of beer, and
who went back and begged, like the prodigal old
sinner that be was, to be indulged once more with
the Dives-like luxuries he had been wont to enjoy
in tho asylum. There’s no accounting for tastes
or actions. In Hobart, a few years ago, a man
actually got “ over the garden wall ” into the
gaol—broke in —having failed to qualify for ad-
mission in the legitimate way. He was, of course,
a “softy,” but so must the other old fellow be.
Why, 10s. is 40 drinks and free counter lunches !

Swallowing a sword is a
trick not conducive to health
or longevity. But it is not
more injurious than the hasty
swallowing of half-masti-
cated food washed down by
gulps of tea or water. Hasty
eating is the fall of health.
Sooner or later it must result
in indigestion, or some other
form of stomach disease. For
the cure of the diseases of
the stomach, and allied
organs of digestion, there is
no medicine so effective as
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills. Mr. Vine is a well-
known greengrocer, residing
in Denison-street, Kingston,
Sydney. He has a few

The Bile Bean Manufacturing Co. publishes a
book entitled ‘‘Character and how to read it.” The
many ways of reading character are described in a con-
cise, yet comprehensive form, and it cannot fail to prove
interesting and instructive reading. From a typo-
graphical point of view, it is exceedingly satisfactory,
the blocks are well cut, and special mention must be
made of the picture on the back of the book. The Bile
Bean Co., 39 Pitt Street, Sydney, offer to send a copy of
this interesting work to any address free, the only con-
dition being that you mention the name of this paper and
enclose a penny stamp to pay postage.*
New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Co. reports,
for week ended June 28th, that no alteration in values of
wheat has eventuated since previous week. The slight
improvement at the beginning of the month brought
about an effort on the part of holders to quit consign-
ments at the then ruling rates, but the shippers, who
were responsible for the ephemeral rise, had secured
enough for immediate wants and grimly refused to rise
above 2s. BJd ; F.A.Q., 2s. 7d. to 2s. Bd. ; medium and
chickwheat, 2s. 4d. to 2s. sd. per bushel.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The three days’ Grand National meeting at
Flemington starts next Saturday (6th) with the Hurdle
Race in the centre of the programme. Looking at the
figures for the past five years, no reasonable punter can
fancy a heavy-weighted horse, except on the strongest
possible evidence of its being much better than the
best we have had since Emmalea got home in 1595.
As for the chance of Record Reign winning with
12st. 121 b. up, present scribe can merely say that
he doesn’t know of any particular reasoii why
the Maoriland horse should be worth backing at
the weight. Up to now the G.N. Hurdle Race has
never been captured by a 12-stoner, and only twice has a
12-stoner got a place. One was Ringwood, in 1884 ;he
carried 12.7 and finished third. The other was The
Yeoman, in 1888, placed second under a 12.1 bur-
den. But they were proved to be great stayers. Ring-
wood subsequently won an Australian Cup, and Yeoman
ran third in a Melbourne Cup. Where is the encourage-
ment to suppose that Record Reign or Cavaliero (12.7)
can put the aforesaid performances into the shade ?
• * * *
Out of 15 animals placed for the Hurdle Race
in the last five years, 10 have been lightweights—from 9
to 10st., and only two out of the remaining five have
carried as much as list. Perhaps the heavyweights
were specially unlucky, or of lower “class” than this
year’s lot. Present scribe reckons that the handicap this
year is just as fair on paper as it was in previous years,
which is the same thing a; saying that it favors the
lightweights. As for “ luck,” it stands to reason that
the heavier-burdened nags which are not allowed to
get away in front at the beginning of the race
must run a greater risk of accident than the
isolated leaders. “ Luck ” favors the ones that are least
likely to be brought down by the falling of others. This
paragraph was about to commit itself to a selection of two
candidates—Goldfinder and Rackstraw—for the nice juicy
stake of next Saturday. They ran second and third for
the same event last year, carrying 9st. 8 and 9st. 7 respec-
tively. This year they have 10st. 3 and lOst. 5, a differ-
ence of 31bs. in Goldfinder’s favor as compared with the
previous handicap. Both are better treated than
Aquarius, who beat them last year, and both of them, if
all right on the day, will be worth backing. Rackstraw
ran well in public the other day. Goldfinder is said to
have gained stamina since last year, when he was re-
garded as a “ good thing.” So this was how the writer
felt:
GOLDFINDER .. ..1
RACKSTRAW .. ..2
But Rackstraw seems to have hurt himself on the track,
and at time of writing the somewhat lame gee-gee was a
probable non-starter.

No less than five different sets of handicaps
(Canterbury Park, Hawkesbury, A.J.C., Melbourne and
Caulfield Cups) were made public in Sydney on Tuesday.
With three different officials engaged in the work—
Alessrs. Daly (A.J.C.), Dakin (Melb. Cup), and Vowles
(Caulfield) —marked differences were looked for, but they
are not so pronounced as might have been expected.
F’rinstance, Metrop. handicapper sums up Hautvillers as
31b. better than United States, and the Caulfield allotter
brings them together. Malster, Hautboy, George
Frederick, Kinglike, Revenue, Lucknow, La Hogue,
Bruntwood, Rock Gun, and several others are rated 21b.
higher at Caulfield ; while Paul Pry, Sequence, Kulaui,
Paradox, Warrior 11. and Simile have each 31b. more ;
but Wakeful, Record Reign, Duke of Kent, Horace,
and Fighting Mac. are slightly better in the Caul-
field event than at Randwick. Aurous has 101 b.
more at Caulfield than at Randwick, so has Hoop Iron ;
and Bengonia, Rabato, Acumen, Industrious, Klimo, and
Cretonne have 71b. Ibex, Black Watch, Andorra, Rock-
leigh, and Dahomey have each 51b., and Khaki, Sir
Leonard, Diadem, The Strutter, and Acrasia, 41b., more
ayont the Murray. In the cases of La Carabine, Gauleon,
Reka, and Allinga, the respective adjusters agree exactly
Most marked instance of Caulfield differing from Mel-
bourne Cup allotments is Paul Pry who has 81b. less in
the latter, but this was only to be expected after his
repeated failures over the long distance. liymettus has
71b. less in Melbourne Cup, and Sequence and Aurous,
61b. ; while allowances of 51b. each have been conceded to
Rabato, Canada, and Womerah. Then, again, San Fran,
Wakeful, Record Reign, Revenue, Acumen, Bengonia,
Nymagee, and several others are asked to carry 41bs. less
over the longer distance, while lower allowances, which
are justified by the extra half mile, are conceded to La
Carabine, Gauleon, Malster, Hautboy, George Frederick,
Gunga Din, Ingliston, Kinglike, Miltiades,Vocalist, Kalaui,
Paradox, Warrior 11., Ohio, Ampier, Musket, Hautvillers,
United States, Clanside, Grasspan, Honeybee, Klimbo,
La Hogue, Acrasia, Pegasus, and others. Instances in
which Mr. Dakin’s imposts are heavier than those of Mr.
Vowles are not numerous. Most noticeable is Lord
Trenton, who has 51b. more in Melbourne than in Caulfield
Cup. Carbinier has 31b. more, so has Brise de Nuits,
Lord Rudolph, Lucknow, Simile and Benvolia. Footbolt,
Royalty, Otwaj', Patronage, Horace, Gosport, Bruntwood
and a few more are also given increases, but nothing
worth kicking against. Among the cases wherein A. J.C.
and V.R.C. handicappers have differed are April Fool and
Street Arab ; in the Metropolitan former is allotted 8.13,
while in the Melbourne Cup he has 81b. less ; but while
Street Arab is believed to be properly appraised at 6.10 in
the Metropolitan, he is asked to carry 131 b. more in the
two-mile event.

“Teddy” Knight’s reported “crock,” “cripple,”
&c., Prince Carbine (by Carbine-Taihoa) scooped the big
thing of Rosehill meeting, Saturday. Well backed in
“shops” all day Friday and Saturday morning, when
long odds were available, a big load was placed on the
course, consequently the “close” crowd behind the
operations landed a big stake. Some reports told of a
rattling good trial done a week before at Moorefield,
while others scoffed and had it that “ the Prince,” as a
result of the aforementioned test, was to be fired
and turned out. Truth is, the nag wants a heap of look-
ing after through developing splints on his fore legs. So
gingerly must he be dealt with that a gallop now and
again is all his trainer dare do with him. His un-
commonly-good wind does the rest. It’s two years, or
thereabouts; since Prince Carbine previously won a race.
Lady Jewel made a similar run to the one which
snatched victory from Ferryman at Randwick, and but
for jockey Parker's grit Prince Carbine would have been
beaten. Owner “Teddy” Knight, generally stoical,
looked very scared as the pair flashed past the po3t on
Saturday.

“Gun” : Cramped prices offered by Mo. at
Sydney meetings give punters no hope of recovery unless
they run big risks. A single fiver on any nag will often
bring him down two or three points. Instance Beauclere,
in Rosehill Granville Stakes on Saturday: tens were
available till a move was made to back him, when he
dropped to 2 to 1 quick as a flash, and started 5 to 2
against, though the “ field ” totalled 15. Ranjitsinghji
opened at fours for the Welter at same meeting, but a few
pounds shortened the prices to twos and 7to 4. Beauclere
finished in the ruck and Ranji got home in a canter.
Like cases have been plentiful for some time. The totali-
sator with its inevitable partial eradication of Mo. is a
consummation devoutly to be wished.

Ed. Bulletin. — By the time the Common-
wealthers realise that the Senate’s Post and Telegraph
Bill interferes seriously with the freedom of Australians
it will be too late to successfully agitate for a modifica-
tion of its provisions. What the House of Reps, means
to do remains to be seen. Want of knowledge of the
far-fetched measure has led the gullible public to believe
that, beyond “ Tattersall’s ” consultations, betting and
racing and sporting generally will go untouched. Just
look at clauses 54 and 55. No. 54 gives the Postmaster-
General or any Deputy power to direct that any postal
article addressed to the promoter of any sweep, racing of
every description, fights, games, sports and exercises,
&c., either in his own or ah assumed name, shall not be
delivered. Clause 55 says : “ Any postal matter ad-
dressed as in clause 54 shall be opened and imme-
diately returned to the sender, or if it was not origi-
nally posted in the Commonwealth, shall be returned
unopened to the proper authorities of the Colony,
Possession, or Country where it was originally posted.”
All this is directed at “ Tattersall’s ” consultations; still,
letters will not be delivered to racing-club secretaries
concerning races, neither will any be delivered to betting-
men, bookmakers, or anyone else mixed up in racing.
The rich “ Tattersall’s ” club of bookmakers in Pitt-st.,
Sydney, will be denied its sporting letters. Byron
Moore, of the V.R.C., and Clibborn, of the A.J.C., will
not be able to get entries through the post, or any letters
bearing on racing. It seems that the Federal elements
behind Mr. Drake, P.M.G., are of the hypocritical order,
and that they want to wipe out all classes of sport,
especially horse-racing, and create a church-loving com-
munity by act of legislation. As for “ Tattersall’s ” con-
sultations, what better guarantee could the people have
that they are conducted with fairness and honesty than
the fact that the whole conduct of the sweeps is under
the Tasmanian State Legislature, the Under-Secretary of
Trade and Finance having direct control and supervision'?
“ Tatt.” Adams lodged either £lO,OOO or £20,000 with the
Tas. Government for the faithful carrying out of the con-
sultations, and, so far, no person seems to have yet
revealed any real cause for complaint. The consultations
are known and esteemed throughout the world, let alone
in Australia.
• • •

fill the breach and work the thing for all it is worth,
■Dapping their fingers at the law the whil«.
* • • *
Clever Tas. pug. “ Albury ” Clifford, at Sydney
Gaiety A.C. last week, again fell before Bananaland
rough-and-tumble slugger Harry Phillips. Clifford was
winning all the way, when Phillips hurled a windmill
right-hand swing on the back of his neck and sent him
flat, face down, till long after “ out” sounded. The Tas.
man had bad luck stopping such a hot thing, which was
an obvious fluke. When they fought before each got a
right cross home just at the same instant and both
sprawled, Clifford beyond recall, but Phillips with just
enough vitality left to struggle up by the ropes. They
may meet again, when Clifford should surely win, as he’s
undoubtedly the better fighter.

T lalf-caate Maori Ruenalf and N.S.W. man
M oil, at Sydney Golden Gate Club. Saturday night last,
sup died about the poorest mill Sydney has had for
months. M’Ooll, willing, tough, and hard-hitting enough
for the best company, shapes too open, swings and slashes
blindly, and trusts to chance. The Maori mostly acted in
a scared fashion, evidently mindful of their last turn in
New England. Twice in the third round Ruenalf
floundered on the boards for the full lOsec. and four times
through the fourth 3min. took refuge on the floor from
M'Coll’s mad, bull-like rushes. This was enough for the
M.U. lath, who “ wiped ” just when onlookers thought he
was toeing the mark for the fifth round. Ruenalf is
clever and has ample physique, but lacks grit. He could
have gone on a lot longer on Saturday night. When the
crowd hooted and boohooed their disgust Ruenalf said
something about knowing when he was licked.
• * * *
Yankee “ Kid ” Parker and “ Snowy” Sturgeon
fought at Sydney Gaiety A. 0., Monday night, before a
house packed and packed again. From floor to “ flies ”
weltered a sea of faces, which far exceeded that at the
Oribb-Dunn struggle in the same place. Parker, sturdily-
built, heavy-muscled, looked the pink of condition, ana a
weight-lifter, wrestler, or such, more than a boxer.
Sturgeon, trained to the moment, the best set-up of all
prominent Australian boxers, seemed the ideal athlete.
The fight proved a scuffling affair from the jump,
exciting enough, but lamentably lacking as a dis-
play of science. Parker, short in stature and reach,
was very open, and met many stiff punches. Sturgeon
failed to show skill except as a foul worker—wrenching,
wrestling, and throwing while the thronged house v—o-d
its disgust in jeers, jibes, and groans. Once Parker
fouled after being fouled himself a dozen times, nd the
referee called a halt to caution the Yankee, after a owing
the other’s offences to go unheeded. Early in the tnird
round Parker smothered Sturgeon, floored him, and
looked like turning the tide, but could not continue.
The end came after about Bmin. of actual fighting. Two
heavy right crosses on the “Kid’s” unprotected jaw
were followed by a third that floored him beyond hope of
rising to time. A disappointing thing from the outset.
* « * *
Tweedie-M'Gowan bruising match at Kalgoorlie
(W.A.) was a stiff punching affair of 20 rounds and sent a
big house frantic. Tweedie hasn’t the strength and
hitting power of M‘Gowan but is lots cleverer and dodged
and ducked in fine style, mixing things as freely as t’other
chap whose great game is mixing. Last two rounds were
regular curtain-raisers. M‘Gowan desperately risked a
lot to reach some vital spot, but the Sydney Rocks news-
boy was alive to the situation and kept right “off the
grass.” The referee declared a draw, which the house
unanimously endorsed by sustained applause. When
they met a year or two ago at Brisbane, Tweedie had a
big pile of points to his credit, but grew careless and in
round 13 caught a beauty on the “ point” that laid him
out beyond recall. “Cocker” never forgot that experi-
ence, hence the futility of the South Melbourne miniature
giant’s attempt to get a knock-out punch home.
# * * *
At Kalgoorlie (W.A.) t’other day “Tut” Ryan
won from Bill Doherty, previous holder, in three rounds
the much-hawked and not over-great distinction of
Australian heavyweight champion. Said that Doherty’s
friends are not satisfied and another match is
sure to result. That was the way with the Doherty-
Felix and the Doherty-Dooley affairs. Though beaten
at first, Doherty was top-dog the second time in each
instance. Reasoning on this “Tut’’should go under in
the second match.
* * * *
The latest battle (May 30) for British bruising
supremacy is another sign of English pug decadence.
Yankee middleweight O’Brien met English heavyweight
Chrisp (then champion of England) at Newcastle, before
30Q0 people, and within three rounds Chrisp’s prospects,
barring the remote possibility of a knock-out, seemed
hopeless. Out-generalled and out-fought, he could do
nothing against O’Brien’s incessant prod, prod, prod. In
the 11th round one on the point settled the Britisher
completely. Stated that O’Brien could have won in less
than half the time had he cared to do so. His left was
irresistible, while his wonderful footwork, fine defence,
and admirable knowledge of distance kept him out of
danger.

Retired world’s champion sculler, Bill Beach,
made another swoop upon Sydney’s pigeon-shooting com-
munity t’other day, and finished runner-up in the Federal
Champion Cup with 14 kills. He whacked three sweeps
and, all told, brought down 28 birds, using the second
barrel only four times. “ Old Bill ” looked as good as
years ago, and expressed himself fit enough to take on
Canuck Gaudaur again if opportunity offered.
Pigeon-slayer Eales, in London, on May 23,
secured absolute possession of the £4O Challenge or
“ Thursday” Cup, conditions of which exacted three suc-
cessive wins. In the third event, Eales had 11 succes-
sive kills and whacked the pool of £63 with another,
whom he ultimately beat in the shoot-off. Previously,
Eales was one of 22 in a £3 sweep and performed
brilliantly from 33yds. until-a miss in the 12th round eft.
’Frisco champion Nauman (31yds.) and Vernon Barker
(31yds.) to fight it out. Barker lost liis 13th, and the
’Murkan won.

Sculler Towns, after almost luring Jake
Gaudaur out of that snug retreat at Hat Portage
(Canada) is likely to miss his game for a while, as ’Ome
oarsman Sam Emmett challenged Jake for £250 a side,
and offered £5O expenses if the Canadian would row on
the Thames. This being a comparatively soft snap it
was thought likely that Gaudaur would come. Emmett
(only 2 years less than 40) stands 6.14, and scales 12f
stone. Having beaten only third-raters, how he can have
a hope with Gaudaur puzzies one much. It’s long odds
that Towns, Barry or Sullivan could be backed for a big
amount against Emmett. Towns wanted to make a
match, but Emmett's backer said they wanted Gaudaur
and none other. Emmett’s most recent feats are account-
ing for the ex-British top-notcher Geo. Bubear, and Geo.
Drummond last year.

Estimated that, in all, between 12,000 and 15,000
witnessed the four senior grade Rugby matches in Sydney
on Saturday. Glebe just beat Balmain by 7to 6. ’Varsity
finished 8 to 6 better than Newtown after being behind
till near the close of full time. Previous match was a
draw, neither side scoring. Eastern Suburbs literally
romped over North Sydney, notching 21 to 6 : and South
Sydney pulled up with 10 to Western Suburbs’ 3. Bal-
main’s ill-luck is as pronounced this season as last.
Though playing well, and occasionally succeeding in
giving acknowledged strong combinations a close call,
they’ve not won a single match yet. Last season Balmain
went down religiously, match after match, till near the
end, when they came with a rush, and did some big
things, much to the surprise of all followers of the game.
Will history repeat itself this year?
* • * *
Champion bowlers Bradbury and Hogue (pic-
tured last week) are not recent developments. Brad-
bury (“Champion of Champions”) has been playing
eight years, and is hon. sec. of Victoria Park Club. His
spoils last three seasons include Victoria Park’s Champion
Medal (99-’Ol inclusive), Champion of Champions (both
years—this and last —of its existence), and skipper of an
undefeated rink in present season’s pennant matches,
scoring 206 to 101 against. Hogue took to bowls seven
years ago when president of now defunct Glebe Club. His
best work occurred lately, when he appropriated City-
Club’s Novice Handicap, and Champion Gold Medal,
and was one of four that survived the “ Sandeman ” rink
tourney, also secured second position in the “ Williams”
prize competition, and won N.S. VV.B.A. Championship.

breeders’ Association Show pretty well equalled the his-
toric canine who yarded the blow-fly' into a pickle-bottle.

• • • *
Apropos Mr. E. P. Simpson’s success in N.S.W.
golf supremacy'. That athletes do so well at it is ample
proof of the thoroughly athletic nature of the game.
E.P.S. was a top-notcher when amateur running flourished
in this State years since ; he was a front-rank rower in
the salad days of Sydney' Rowing Club, and few of our
amateurs use the cue more skillfully than he. Mr.
S. Gollan, the M.L. sport now in England, is a fine athlete
—as evidence his enduring row with scullers Towns and
Stevenson on the Thames recently, as well as other feats.
Mr. Gollan went ’Ome with the M.L. golf premiership,
and since got away with one of the classic golf events on
old St. Andrew’s links from some of the best. Another
proven fore-front athlete was Mr. Gillies, also a recent
Australian golf premier.
* * * •
Mr. Mark Moss, who died recently, was to Vic.
bowls what Mr. John Young is to bowls in Sydney. Mr.
Moss bowled for over 40 years. West Melbourne Club of
the old days stood near the junction of Latrobe and
William-sts., and the deceased gentleman was its presi-
dent. He and Mr. John Young started inter-State
matches 21 y'ears ago. As president of the V.B.A. Mr.
Moss for 10 years entertained on Windsor green veteran
bowlers who had had 20 years’ experience in the game,
and frequently provided valuable trophies for all classes
of competitions.

“Old Timer”: Sydney bruisers know as little
of the MQ. rules as most officiating referees. They won’t
stand in-fighting-absolutely the best feature in the busi-
ness. Any man who attempts quick work after breaking
from a elinch is vigorously howled at. The referee yells
“ break fair,” and he who goes back furthest with hands
spread like wings is cheered for “ extreme fairness.”
Rats ! Rules don’t require that; it’s simply “ break ! ”
and quickest home scores ; but that can only be done now
at imminent risk of disqualification for foul work. In
Victoria they fight right because the referee of the old
Melbourne Athletic Club knew his business, and kept
the crowds and the pugs properly educated. Last
genuinely-organised body' controlling glove fights in
Sydney was the York-street S.A.O. Club, dead 10 years
ago. Since it ceased the Mother State capital hasn’t had
a properly-qualified referee. The Cribb-Dunn fight is
hanging-fire at present through the apparent impossi-
bility of securing a capable man.
• * • *
“Old Timer”: Lately deceased ancient Sydney
cricketer, Mr. T. H. Lewis, was 73 and an officer in the
N. Surveyor-General’s Dept. He and his brother
O. were once fore-front cricketers and represented
N.S.W. against Vic. in ’57 and ’5B, and one or other in
’59, ’6O and ’6l. O.L.’s forte was underhand bowling, at
which he secured excellent averages.

“ Flat ” : Sydney “ double-shop ” swindles are
getting worse than ever, which is saying a heap. With
cronk scratchings and an “understanding” between
trainers or owners and the layers of the odds to a “ bob ”
or upwards, ’tis small wonder that the stay'-at-home sec-
tion of Mo waxes fat and beams “childlike and bland ”
upon his victims. Often punters take 2’s to 1, or “evens”
something that appears to have a mortgage on one par-
ticular event, and, though it' goes to the course, it is
taken out at the eleventh hour in accord with the owner-
shop contract. The army of lambs to be fleeced appears
as strong as ever—most of them chasing the ignis fatuux
of getting back their own. There’s a law to stamp these
sporting parasites out completely. Why isn’t it en-
forced ?
• * * •
Latest anent condition of whilom top-notch
English trundler, Johnny Briggs, is that the poor fellow is
in Oheadle Asylum, playing imaginary cricket most of the
time, usually bowling, and appropriating anything handy
for a ball.
Ten members of the British House of
Lords own 325 licensed public houses in London.
Earl Derby is at the top with 72.
A gruesome story comes from Frankston
(Vic.). A child, two years old, was playing with
a dog, when the latter suddenly snapped off the
child’s nose and swallowed it, then bit it’s victim’s
cheek so severely that the sight of one eye is
likely to be lost. Moral: Never let young chil-
dren “play” with dogs. A child’s idea of play
is generally teasing, and a dog is usually a bundle
of nerves.
“F.C.” : Findley makes the fourth member
to be escorted tothe big front doorof Vic. Assembly.
On April 21st, ’69, C. E. Jones was expelled, and
six days later J. S. Butters followed him, on the
famous “Mint Sauce” charge. Ballarat and
Portland promptly sent both back. Then, on
Aug. Ist, ’76, the just-dead Jas. M'Kean was
violently ejected for speaking disrespectfully of
his fellow-members. Before that—on July 25th,
’67, G. J. Sands was declared unworthy to sit.
He saved himself from expulsion by resigning a
month earlier. Singular that Charles Gavan Duffy
snavelled the vacant seats of both M‘Kean and
Sands. Findley, just ejected, puts up for the
vacant East Melbourne seat, but his advisers have
stronger hearts than heads. If he and the Labor
leaders threw themselves whole-heartedly into the
Melbourne fight he would probably still hold the
seat. Already there are two Liberals and a Tory
to split the vote ; while a Labor-man has only
Buckley’s chance at East Melb.
The Secret History of the Coningham
Case, as told by “ Zero,” is out, and probably by
this time occupies a place of honor on the book-
shelves of Rev. Dill Macky. It is in many ways
an amusing and instructive book. In the first
place, it is a Detective Story of Real Life, in
which the issues involved were not pa,]try stolen
jewels, but the honor of a high dignitary of
the celibate clergy of the Church of Rome, and
the reputation of a man and a woman.
Secondly, it is interesting because it con-
tains facsimiles of letters from the parties
concerned and from many others —human docu-
ments all of them, and not mere formal epistles.
But its chief charm to the student of human
nature is the open, unhesitating, even hilarious
style in which the story of the counterplot against
the Coninghams was carried out. The Coning-
hains and their backers were clever, and tortuous,
and subtle. They constructed a case that
formed a hook upon which the people whose
nightmare is the Scarlet Woman might have
hung a thesis that would have—if their luck had
been good—scared the Pope out of the Vatican
and driven Cardinal Moran and Dr. O’Haran and
all the R.C. clergy to take refuge in the caves
in the rocks; but they fought against a church
that knew her business many centuries before they
were born. The book is well written and well
brought out, and may, in days to come, form good
material for a future Australian Zola or Mrs.
Humphrey Ward. Finn Bros., of Pitt-st.,
Sydney, are the printers.

ORDER TO-DAY.
CALLAGHAN & SON, 395 GEORGE ST., SYDNEY.
B.H. Block 10—just now in lower water than
it has been for many a day—strikes a welcome patch of
carbonates, nearly 50 per cent, lead, at the 615 ft. level,
They hadn’t expected this splendid fluxing ore to run
down to the depth, and came on the shoot unexpectedly.
General-manager Warren, who has been with the co. from
the beginning, leaves at the end of the month (July) ;
Assistant-manager Grayson has already left. The direc-
tors don’t propose to go outside Australia for a successor.
Of course, in view of the lead-slump, the office of
assistant-manager is abolished.
* * * *
General Manager Warren has had a rare ex-
perience in connection with his resignation from B.H.
Block 10—the mine which has paid over £850,000 in divs.
since he took charge. The resignation of Warren and his
assistant went in at the same time, and in the circs, there
was nothing for it but to accept both. Now, however,
practically the whole of the officers and miners have peti-
tioned him to apply for re-appointment; and another big
petition went to him from the town of Broken Hill,
Under Warren, accidents have % been extremely rare, and
that influenced the workmen most; while his grip of the
mechanical side of the sulphide problem mainly in-
fluenced the Broken Hill people.

Last year Broken Hill town council asked the;
local mines to pay £17,000 municipal rates ; and the
matter is still somewhere m Equity. This year, notwith-
standing the lead slump, the demand has gone up to
£18,225. B.H. Prop, is asked for £6300 ; Central (Sul-
phide Corporation) for £4950; North (which has had to
shut down because it can’t pay its way), £630.

Mining at Talgai, Warwick (Q.), has just now
one of its periodical fits of briskness. Alleged that a co.
is about to put up a 20-stamp battery to crush Big Hill
and Queenslander quartz. Big Hill has a reef about Bft.
wide that goes, right through, about sdwt.—free milling
stone. The Queenslander is a unique reef, both because
it wasthe first reef ever worked in Queensland (dating back
to the early'oo’s), and because, after a crushing of 90 tons
for UOOoz., it was abandoned, and remained so for about
20 years, on account of a blank coming in. Depth of old
working, 85ft. ; value of gold, £3 15s. Writer was down
the old workings years ago, and thought it a pity to
see this pretty little, almost vertical reef, with its clean
slate walls, lying idle.
* * * *
Western Creek, 30 miles north of Clermont (Q.),
is the scene of another reef discovery. The wires have
been set going and more or less glowing aocounts tele-
graphed throughout Queensland. As a prospecting show
the new find looks good, and a bit of a rush has set in ;
but the Western country is waterless and the nearest
battery nearly SO miles off.
* • « •
Blair Athol coalfield, Peak Downs (Q.), is a bit
of a geological curiosity. Approximately, a sq. mile
covers the coal-bearing area ; on three sides of the field
payable gold has been found almost up to the sandstone
formation, and on the fourth side an intruding curl of
basalt comes in, about four miles broad. Beyond the
basalt, slate again asserts itself, and has been mined for
thousands of ozs. of gold. The proposed railway line from
Clermont to Blair Athol would cross several patches of
worked-out surface gold-dabs ; and any amount of untried
country lies between these patches ; so, if the parallel
side-ditches and small cuttings, when the railway is made,
fail to show anything, the value of trenching as an adjunct
to successful mining will be (at any rate on Clermont
goldfield) problematical, as the line is to be 12 miles long.
• • • «
Official return of alluvial gold won on Peak
Downs fields for May of this year, 5720 z. Reputedly over
1000 miners at work ! Looks as if a big percentage were
living on their kidney-fat.
* * * *
Good seams of anthracite coal exist at M'Kenzie
'River (0.Q.), but 60 miles of rough country intervenes
"twixt them and the railway line. Boring is going on close
to the railway in hopes of picking up continuation of
aforesaid seams. If they are picked up, Rockhampton
will need to be tied down for a week, as the bores are
only about 70 miles out of that town.

A correspondent, who apparently ought to
know, writes cautioning the public against swallowing
whole the gaudy stories now being press-and-prospectus
circulated re the richness of the property of the Dawes
Range Copper-Mining Co., Glassford Creek, Q. “I
know,” he says, “ of no lode at Glassford Creek outside
the Bompa Property that can be said to have been
proved, either by sinking or driving. lamin a position
to speak positively on mines and mining in that particu-
lar district, as I managed the ‘ Great Bompa ’ Property,
during the development of their South Mine.”

Squatter Fairbaim asks in the Pastoral
Gazette : “ Has anyone ever met a man who has taken
money out of squatting in Queensland by legitimate
grazing and not out of another man’s pocket by buying
and selling?” None the less there are numbers of financial
institutions that contrive to screw a very fair annual per-
centage out of Queensland pastoral properties.

Hercules, Tas. : Arrangements made with Tas.
Smelting Works to take 10,000 tons of sulphide ore on
terms said to be £1 per ton better than obtainable at
Dapto. Last return from latter place gave a profit of
£l7OO, clear of all charges, from 362 tons.

Macdonnell Ranges: The Warden writes:—
“ Your par. of May 4 needs correction. The English
crowd (Central Australian Exploration syndicate, not
Mines Selection Co.’s) well equipped party did not prospect
a foot of ground in these ranges, but about 300 miles
north, only crossing the ranges along the road. They
found several reefs of joz. quartz, and raised over 500 tons
of it which is lying there still, and may be crushed some
years hence when better facilities offer. The Arltunga
goldfield has not been half prospected, leaving the whole
of the Macdonnell ranges out of question. Native
name for gold-bearing quartz ‘ Aljura.’ No stations are
deserted, country in splendid condition owing to good
summer rains, although some losses of stock occurred
through previous drought.” Particulars are given of the
crushing of four parcels of ore—4B tons for 119 oz. of gold.

Northern Territories Goldfields, aptly known in
London as “The Northern Tig-itors.” It was loudly
averred at last meeting of sharehiold’ers that at the time
Bottomley was fluently urging shareholders to “ keep
their eyes on ‘ Northern Terrors ’ ” he was quietly sliding
out. The meeting was very noisy and the chairman,
Robert Smith, died in a chair at home a few hours after-
wards, the excitement having been too much for him.

Henry Hess, of London Critic, has lost another
libel action. Only the second out of 38. A great re-
cord. In this he has been called upon to pay £3OO
damages to a man prominently associated with some
Westralian flotations. Hess is by no means satisfied, and
has called on the readers of the Critic to say : (1) Whe-
ther the Judge’s comments were fair or grossly unfair.
<2) Whether they, sitting as jurymen, would have allowed
him to dictate their verdict to them. Both the actions
lost by Hess were tried before the same Judge, of whom
Hess says : “ I do protest, and shall never cease doing so
until the cause is removed, that his Honor is unfit to pre-
side on the bench. I know not whether it be incompet-
ence, or whether it be gross prejudice on his part, to which
is due the extraordinary fact that he is the only Judge
on the bench before whom I have lost the two libel cases
which went against me. I have so repeatedly called
attention to the manner in which he contrives to get ver-
dicts for city men accused of financial wrong-doing (and
several of the verdicts thus secured by him have been
upset on appeal) that I cannot be charged with raising
my voice against his retention only at a time when he
had mulct me in damages.” Fancy an Australian editor
talking like this 1 Hess admits his paper to be in financial
difficulties, the boodle-gang against which he has made
the most valorous fight on English record having been
too much for him.
■ • • *
The Jungle Fever ! Few people here realise the
extent of the present London boom in West African mines.
The total nominal par.-capitalisation in 232 companies is
such that to pay a yearly div. of 10 per cent, would need a
profit on gold-mining in that country (the Gold Coast
region) of £2,300,000 per annum, which is just one-half
of the divs. paid by the Rand mines in 1898, before
they closed-down on account of the war. And there it took
the crushing of over 7,300,000 tons of ore to produce these
divs. Attention to the mining possibilities of West Africa
commenced in ’B2, but, up to ’99, less than 30 companies
had been floated. Last year, 119 new co’s were formed,
and, in the first four months of this year, 74 have been
got off. The mad gamble in “ Jungles” is still progress-
ing, and several real and rotten Westralian co’s are ope-
rating in that direction. No less a person than Charles A.
Moreing, of the eminent firm of Bewick Moreing, has joined
the army of “ Jungle ” promoters. Of the concession
owned by the Continental and Westralian Trusts,
Limited, Mr. Moreing is reported as having said that it
was equal to 1300 Rand claims. “To the co. they were
valued at £9O a claim, which, as you see, was a very
absurd price. Suppose we were to value them at only
£lOOO a claim, which is an equally absurd price, the
value of the property would then he £1,300,000 ; but
£lOOO is simply ridiculous for claims like these. Even
£lO,OOO would be a very low figure compared with
S’African properties and at £lO,OOO each the value would
be £13,000,000. We know that the Johannesburg claims,
even the worst of them, are valued at £29,000 a claim,
and some of them work out at £8),000 a claim, and they
are no richer than ours.” Jehosaphat! There was no
applause ; perhaps the shareholders were too dumb-
founded for that, or perhaps they remembered what
Moreing had said about certain Westralian shows. Mr. M.,
by the way, is said to hail from Braidwood (N.S.W.)
district. The locality of this gold-boom is probably the
unhealthiest in the world. British soldiers receive life-
pensions for a few years’ service there. If a dozen men
went out to verify Mr. Moreing’s glittering optimisms
there s a fair chance that not one would return to
personally hand in his report.

Some weeks ago it was expected that a few of
the Rand mines would get to work again almost im-
mediately, and that 350 stampers would soon be in
operation, but news lately arrived that the scheme didn’t
come off. It went to pieces when the mine-owners pro-
posed to fix the maximum wage to be paid to all employes
at five shillings per day. Wages were about four times
that figure before the Blessings of British Freedom fell on
the country, and 30s. a week isn’t a cheering rate of re-
muneration up on the veldt.

Malodorous Footscray has belied itself,
and has sent into Vic. State Parliament as
Mauger’s successor the nattiest of dandies in
Jacob Fotheringham. The new member, who is
still among the twenties, beat another “boy” in
John Lemmon, the Trades Hall nominee. The
A.N.A. ran Fotheringham for all it was worth,
and lie just pipped the Laborite on the post. It
was his third try for the seat. The new member
is an ironmonger’s salesman, and shines as an
amateur reciter and “ pretty talker ” generally.
The last straw laid by N.S.W- Minister
O’Sullivan on the back of Capital is his placing of
the Govt, docks at Sydney at the disposal of the
strikers from the privately-owned iron-foundries
and docks, to enable them to carry on operations
“upon their own.”
Ex-Ambassador Malet, in his just-pub-
lished “Recollections,” says that Bismarck’s
favorite story was an English one, about a
traveller in the Shires objecting to an inn land-
lord’s account. Traveller: “What’s your name.
Landlord: “Partridge.” Traveller: “ I thought
it was Woodcock by the length of your bill.”
The complete list of casualties by the re-
cent coastal storm will not be known for some
time—probably never. At time of writing, at
least nine men have gone under—five with the
tug Protector, at Ballina, and four with the drogher
Alexander Berry, at Shell Harbor. The ferry
steamer Manly was only saved from disaster by a
stroke of good luck and a handy sister steamer.
The Sydney life-boat and pilot service proved
woefully and disgracefully inefficient during the
storm. And it was a storm. Writer was on a
steamer from Melbourne to Sydney, with sea and
gale astern, during the height of the rough weather.
Met several vessels plunging Southwards, _ and
wouldn’t have been aboard some of them for ail the
wealth of the Commonwealth. There was quite
enough danger and discomfort on the northward
voyage; the opposite track looked like a certain
road to Kingdom Come.
In expelling Labor member Findley from
Yic. Assembly the Jingoes seem to have
succeeded in advertising the “ Tocsinner ’
better than they anticipated. With the Par-
liamentary action as a precedent certain pursy
persons of Fitzroy rushed to the local Mayor
with a petition for a Town Hall meeting, whereat
to haul Eitzroy representatives Billson and
O’Connor over tne coals for voting in Parliament
against Findley’s expulsion. O’Connor had been
elected by a big majority but a few days
previously, and as some defeated candidates
were understood to be the prime movers
in the projected condemnation, O’Connor’s
numerous friends and supporters attended the
meeting in force, and unanimously upheld his
vote, condemning those who voted for the
expulsion of Findley with a voice loud
enough to make Peacock cling to his perch
in terror. Parliament’s evident desire to Rus-
sianise Victorian institutions, as shown by its
highhanded expulsion of Findley, was further
stressed by the marching into the meeting of a
posse of police to guard against a “ possible up-
rising.” If Premier Peacock hopes to cow
Victorians in that way, he will probably
wish he hadn’t taken the Argus for
his mentor so early in his career. Ihe ancient
organ of fossildom is anything but a reliable
authority for a young Premier to follow in these
democratic times.
“ Castro’s Last Sacrament and Other
Stories,” by Albert Dorrington (“ Alba Dorion ”)
is the best book of short stories yet produced in
Australia. The S. Daily Telegraph recognises
theauthor’s “absolutegenius.” TheS.M. Herald
says the volume “ contains dramatic pictures
which the best of our artists in words might well
envy.” A handsome cloth-bound volume, 350 pp.,
post-free from Bulletin office for 4s. 6d.

Round about Warrnambool (Vic.), the
Parliamentary candidate generally has an exciting
time. There is a heavy Irish vote in that district,
and from the moment a candidate announces him-
self _ weird rumors bearing on sectarian matters
circulate. If he be a R.C. it is whispered
that he is “ a Jesuit, ” and that his platform pro-
mises are like pie-crust; if he be Protestant, it is
industriously circulated that he was once Deputy-
Olnef-Flag-Waver of an Orange lodge somewhere.
In the Leg. Council contest, just decided, between
squatter Manifold and lawyer Horwitz, of Hamil-
ton (the squatter won by a small majority),
the system was in full vigor. Manifold pub-
licly advertised that he “ never said that he could
get elected without the Catholic vote ” ; and this
was followed by a signed letter declaring that
writer heard Manifold say “he thanked God he
was not an Irishman.” If Horwitz thanked God
for anything, it was probably because he is a
Hebrew, and so was nicely outside the poisonous
sectarian trouble, anyhow.'
“ Veteran ” on a military matter :—
I found this notice in a recent London Gazette :
“jEssex Regiment. The appointment to a second lieu-
tenancy of Sergeant E. Fitzgerald from 2nd New Zealand
Contingent is cancelled at his own request.” And no
wonder! He would receive the £100 allowed to all
“ rankers ” for outfit, and his pay would be 5s. 3d. a day,
with quarters, fire and light. Out of that he would have
to pay 2s. 6d. a day for his mess dinner, whether he ate it
or not; four to six days’ pay monthly for mess and band-
subscriptions ; an uncertain amount for “ entertain-
ments,” and 10s. a month to his servant; also to provide
said servant w'ith a suit of livery, and another of plain
clothes, to subscribe to various regimental funds, and
generally live up to the regimental standard. C ompare
this with the French sous-lieutenant without private
means, w'ho gets about 4s. a day, dines at the caf6 for
9.Jd., always wears uniform so that he need buy no plain
clothes, and spends his evenings off duty at the caf6, sip-
ping eau sucrde and playing dominoes. Society doesn’t
trouble him, or he it. If some of the other fellows in his
regiment are rich they go their way, and he his. That is
what the British army has got to come to sooner or later.

Cardinal Rampolla is virtually Pope. For
IS vears, as Secretary, he has been Leo s right-
hand man, never leaving Rome, and doing all t e
real work of late. It is a pleasant fiction that, by
the grace of God, a Pope never fades, even at 91.
Popes and Queen Victorias live for ever.
At Essendon (Vic.) a butcher was sued
under the Factories Act for having an excess of
improvers in his employ. His defence was that
two of the young fellows-one was earning 30s. a
week and his keep, and the other 10s. a week and
his keep-engaged in the shop were his sons. Lhe
Anti-Factory legislation J.s.P. promptly dismissed
the case. The Crown appealed to the run
Court consisting of Madden, C.J., and Justices
Williams and A’Beekett. After hearing argu-
ments, the Court held that the two sons ranked as
improvers, but in giving judgment Madden
showed that lie was on the side of the anti-factory
legislation crowd. He remarked “that it was a
startling result that the defendant was not per-
mitted to employ his sons in this way. The effect
would he that the sons would not he able to find em-
ployment elsewhere ” —(in the name of wonder, why
wouldn’t they) —“ so that they would have to go on
the streets with the chance of being ruined .” And
so Madden, J.O., lays it down as a rule in life
that unless a man is able to employ his own sons
himself, nobody else will employ them (i.e., no
man employs any males except his own sons), and
they must go on the streets with the chance of
beinsr ruined. If Madden Wasn’t blinded with
crusty Tory ideas he would notice that not one
man in a dozen employs his own son in his busi-
ness or profession, and that not one man in a
dozen is in a position to do so if he were so in-
clined. Still the sons seem to live somehow with-
out going on the streets to be ruined.

ELECTRO-MEDICAL & SURGICAL INSTITUTE.
The Two Eminent Specialists
who have the Largest Practice
in Australasia.
And the Only Specialists in Australia who have
Received Highly Commendable Press Notices
in Leading Australian Journals.
Specialties = Nervous and Chronic Complaints, Blood and Skin Diseases,
Heart, Kidney, Liver, Bladder, Rheumatism, Sciatica, &c., &o.
Nine Diplomas and Certificates of Qualification and Registration by the Govern-
ment Medical Boards of New South Wales, Victoria, South Australia, Great
Britain and America, open to inspection at the Consulting Rooms
by our Physician-in-Chief.
TT is an acknowledged fact the world-wide over that no
single individual or combination of men can be ab-
solute masters of every phase and branch of art, science,
medicine, or other professed avocation of a limitless
sphere of operation and magnitude. At best the greatest
scientists of the day—men like Lord Kelvin and Edison,
. . i
ar
Yours truly . . .
HO WA Tt T) FR EE3IA N
Sydney’s Leading Specialist.
Professor of Electricity as applied Medically, Member
Dr. Harmon’s College, Washington Cor., Cal., U.S.A.
Director and Founder of the Institute.
for instance, whose fame is universally known—class
themselves as infants in scientific invention and research,
and modestly tell us that they have but a smattering of
knowledge of the possibilities, extent, and development
We have only to look at the fine buildings that occupy
the corner of Elizabeth and Bathurst streets to gain some
idea of the extent and importance of their professional
work. But to have the privilege of being shown through
the building by the Specialists themselves, and to see and
learn something of the real character of their wide prac-
tice, is a treat that only comes the way of a fortunate in-
. dividual like the writer.
The instrument-room was, as usual, one of particular
curiosity. Laymen always have a penchant for eyeing
and examining the polished steel articles with which so
many extraordinary acts are performed upon the human
body. The collection in this room is in every way up-to-
date. Whenever a new instrument comes to the front,
and its worth is proved beyond question, it is added to
this rare assortment. Another room full of absorbing in-
terest is that devoted to the galvanic batteries, of which
there are several magnificent kinds. The noted Gaitfc
batteries, the finest French makes, are here in perfection,
irrespective of their high cost. If any article or mechani-
cal and electric contrivance is found necessary in main-
taining the fame of this institution, the price is never
allowed to stand in the way.
The correspondence department is a veritable eye-
opener. Its sacredness is a bar to a stranger’s admission,
but a full view of the work going on is had through the
glass doors. Half-a-dozen typists are seen driving their
machine-writers at electric speed. To the principals only
are know'll the namesof the persons throughout Australia
who are being treated as skilfully in their homes about
the metropolis and away in the interior as if they were
present in personal consultation at the institution.
The packing-rooms are likewise shut off from curious
and prying glances, and the work of one department is a
mystery to the others. No better system for holding
secret the nature of sufferers’ troubles, for safe-guarding
their interests, and giving due heed to the feelings and
sensitiveness of patients, could be imagined. Packages
are not plastered with advertising labels, they are put up
in such a way that only the names and addresses are in
view.
What a practice is here revealed and what a consola-
tion must it be to these eminent specialists to know that
they are permanently curing and restoring to health and
happiness thousands of people w'hose lives were a burden
to them. The young, the middle-aged, and the healthy
are treated with the same amount of success. The pro-
digious nature of the work accomplished by these
specialists necessitates —not the stereotyped morning and
evening two hours’ consultation ticket, but their constant
attention at the institution from early morning till late
into the night. “ I can assure you,” said Dr. Wallace,
“ we don’t put in these long hours because we are fonder

(All Trams Stop at the Door).
of electricity, and only a fringe of imagination of the un-
told stores of strength and wealth wrapt up in this
phenomenal power.
Therefore it requires no argument to convince the
most pronounced of optimists that the field in any one of
the great and honored professions of the day is'far too
vast for the thorough comprehension of the most per-
fectly brain-endowed man, or body of naturally-gifted
men. Yet in respect of the most delicate and difficult of
all professions—that of prescribing for the ills and
diseases of mankind—we find doctors, scattered over the
globe, off-handedly and without careful, minute, and
searching examination and diagnosis—without any know-
ledge of the past lives of patients—flippantly dashing off
prescriptions for every class of complaint, as if a cure was
as simple as falling over the proverbial log.
Thanks to the march of civilisation, culture, and edu-
cation, people are getting to know that modern medical
men who are worth consulting confine their practice to a
certain class of cases. Realising the utter hopelessness of
mastering and curing everything under the sun, and find-
ing that “gullibles” are not so plentiful as they used to
be, the wise and progressive men of medicine devote
themselves to one or other class of cases, and work out
the remedies for them to the best of their skill and
ability.
What better and more striking illustration of the suc-
cess of Specialists in Skin Diseases, Heart, Kidney, Liver
Affections, Rheumatics, Sciatica, Nervous Diseases, could
the people of this magnificent continent, Australia, have
than the enormous practice of the famous practitioners of
the Electro-Medical Institute. Their skill, attainments,
position, and renowned reputation stamp them as the un-
doubted masters of the classes of diseases and complaints
which have been the study of their professional lives.
When we speak of electricity we think of Edison. Why ?
Because he dwarfs other men in this branch of science.
In the same way, when we talk of specialists, we at once
associate the names of the Freeman and Wallace Insti-
tute. So it is in every branch of scientific medicine;
some one or two men stand head and shoulders above
their fellows.
It is the aim, object, and devotion of certain doctors to
he pre-eminent in some defined class of disease rather
than to tinker with a host of complaints about which
they can have but a hazy notion, and superficial know-
ledge. To use a hackneyed but expressive phrase, they
are “ Jacks of all trades and masters of none.” Never -
since the time of Ricord, the noted French Specialist,
have any men conducted so gigantic and successful a
practice as that conducted by the Freeman-Wallaee
Electro Medical and Surgical Institute.
of hard work than any other medical men ; we do it in
the best interests of sufferers. We don’t keep them
waiting longer than we can help, and we suffer many in-
conveniences for our patients’ sakes.”

for his departure, and the Specialists returning to their

Sydney Telegraph is in a difficult situation when
it talks about N.S.W. finance. As an intensely
parochial organ it has to say N.S.W. is getting on
well just because it is N.S.W. Also, it has to
say that the N.S.W. Treasury is overflowing with
wealth, because under the Reid Foreign-trade
tariff it couldn’t be otherwise. And it has to say
that N.S.W. is ruined, because under Protection-
ist Lyne (even though he left the Foreign-trade
tariff untouched) and See it couldn’t be otherwise.
So the Telegraph says both things. In its
leader of 3rd June it spoke of the “ satisfactory
condition of the finances.” “ New South Wales,
it said, “is at the present moment the most pros-
perous State of the Union”; “Apart from the
Lpecial causes of expenditure during the past
year arising in connection with the war, the
plague, and the Commonwealth celebrations, the
country has been more than paying its way,’ and
so on. And then on 12th June it said the accounts
were “a sham,” and the deficit was being added
to every year, and the shortage was a disgrace to
N.S.W. As there are only two tilings that can
be said on the subject, and the Telegraph has
now said both of them, there is a fair chance that
one of its statements is true.
What is the betting about anybody being
able to find reliable corroborative evidence of the
story told by the servant girl, Minns, that on the
night before the disastrous Grand Hotel (Auck-
land) fire she discovered a man in the pantry,
with gunpowder and fuse? He exclaimed : “By
!I am caught ! ” Then he seized her by the
wrists, and threatened to kill her if she opened
her lips. Two other men appeared, and they
went upstairs with the gunpowder. One told her
that he would have revenge on Johnston, the
licensee, and they produced a pistol and knife,
threatening to kill- her if she spoke. Eventually
the men let her go, and she heard one say:
“Things will be blown sky-high to-morrow night;
we will make it warm for them.” Minns added
that she told no one, being frightened. She
heard their voices again the next night, shortly
before the fire broke out. She had seen them
several nights previously, looking in at the win
dow. Hum!

“S.S.” ; No more cowardly act than the
casting out of Findley, who represented Mel-
bourne City in Vic. Assembly, has ever disgraced
an Australian Parliament. In that one act Pea-
cock exposes himself as a political worm in the
eyes of every intelligent and manly democrat.
The hysterically-worked-up implication that Find-
ley had committed some atrocious crime, in pub-
lishing the Irish People’s article is a gross and
insolent effort to bulldoze the masses. The same
charges have, off and on for years, been levelled in
print against Albert Edward, and a few years
back the respectable Age itself was not above
lending its righteous countenance to Grundyish
insinuations that A.E. was a very bold, bad
man. Even the gutter - blood loyalty of
our time, O Lord, does not explain the
true inwardness of this latter-day casting-out
of a devil. That lies in the fact of a Labor member
representing Melb. City, long considered a Tory
stronghold. The Labor member for Melbourne
was a horror and a shame to the Argus,
which paper seized upon the “Foul Libel on the
King” with the avidity of a starved cannibal
dropping on a fat orphan. It was the Argus’s
determination to have the city constituency in
Fatman’s hands again, combined with Premier
Pappycoek’s craven desire to please Chamberlain,
and his pusillanimity in the face of the excitement
of a greasy paper and a few oleaginous Jingoes,
that resulted in Findley’s firing-out. This is the
sort of thing the brain and backbone of the Demo-
cracy never forget—it leaves the jackdaw Pre-
mier stripped of his feathers.

NFRINGEMENT OF TRADE MARK.
PEARL CREAM.
IN THE SUPREME COURT,
1901, No. 392,
Between
BARCLAY and COMPANY,
Plaintiffs,
and
W. H. NEWLANDB,
Defendant.
Mr. Justice Holroyd.—ln Chambers.
Upon hearing the Solicitors for the plaintiffs and de-
fendant respectively and by consent, it is ordered that
the defendant, his servants and agents, be and they
are hereby perpetually restrained from infringing
the plaintiff’s registered Trade Mark No. 2318, con-
sisting of the words, “ Pearl Cream,” by use of
the words “ Pearl Cream,” in respect of goods
not of the plaintiffs’ manufacture or merchan-
dise, and from cutting, engraving, casting, or making
and from causing to be cut, engraved, cast, or made, and
also from using or permitting to be used, and from selling
or otherwise disposing of or parting with, any blocks or
plates adapted for printing labels or sheets of labels in
imitation of the label of the plaintiffs used by them in the
get-up of their goods sold under their said Trade Mark or
differing only colourably therefrom, and from printing or
selling or exposing for sale or procuring to be printed or
sold any labels similar to those in use by the plaintiffs, or
any other papers or labels so printed or contrived as by
colourable imitation or otherwise to represent or lead to
the belief that the preparation put up by other parties
under the title of “ Pearl Cream " was the preparation or
merchandise of the plaintiffs put up under their said
Trade Mark.
And it is further ordered that the said defendant de-
liver up for destruction any of such blocks or plates as
are now in his possession, custody, or power to the plain-
tiffs or such person as they shall appoint to receive the
same.
And it is further ordered that the defendant pay to the
said plaintiffs or their Solicitors, Messrs. Waters and
Crespin, the sum of £5 damages for the said infringement
and the sum of £lO 10s. costs.
Dated this 24th day of June, one thousand nine hundred
and one.
E. D. HOLROYD.
WATERS and CRESPIN, 414-418 Collins-street, Mel-
bourne, Solicitors for BARCLAY and CO,, 44 Stone-
street, New York, Wholesale Druggists.

“ The Best Australian Book of Short Stories."
Castro’s Last Sacrament
By ALBERT DORRIN GTON. 4s 6d.
“ A wealthy Melbourne girl was married last
year on the continent of Europe. Ere she left
Yarrapolie she had beautiful golden hair of a
delicate shade. She has lately returned on a visit
with hair almost black—whereupon several for-
merly devoted bachelors have murmured their
thankfulness for their escape, and all the lady’s
friends wonder what her husband said when he
found it out. And nobody suspected it —not even
those who now say they knew it all the time.
“ The letters of the Duchess of Teck, Cornwall’s
late mother-in-law, are rather interesting reading.
Their writer was very chummy with the Lady
Hopetoun of those days, our Governor-General’s
mother. She speaks of sitting up with her crony
after a ball, talking of 4 Hopie’s speech.’ Said
4 Hopie,’ now Australia’s Mogul, was at the time
an unlessoned boy, confronted with the task of a
speec ito the tenantry. The young Tecks liked
staying at 4 Hopetoun,’ and altogether the clans
were so friendly that it seems as if there might be
a grain of truth in the statement that there was a
time when motherly matrimonial schemes were
brewing a joint future for the wealthy son and
heir of the one family and the pretty, high-con-
nected only daughter of the other.
“Since Major Tim MTnerney returned from
the war, his big brother, the Doctor of the clan, is
so proud of him that he will never let him out of
his sight. Tim now recalls Mary’s little lamb. In
the streets the brothers are eternally side by side.
They visit the Federal Houses together, and when
the Docther takes his seat in the State Assembly,
where Tim cannot accompany him, Tim occupies
the front row of the Speaker’s Gallery and stays
there until the show is over.. Strangers to Mel-
bourne, hearing their names for the first time,
imagine them to be knockabout comedians, and
look vainly through Rickards’ bill for—
Tim —Tub M'lnernies—Tom.
It was the Doctor who marched by the side of the
first contingent (including Tim) the day it left
Melbourne, with his umbrella held in a military
position.
44 George Jenkins is not, after all, going to re-
main Clerk of the House of Representatives. The
4 great sacrifice ’ the podgy one was going to make
in taking a billet worth £9OO a year and giving up
his clerkship of Yic. Leg. Council, worth £l2OO,
is at an end. The fact is, George expected that
the Representatives would be so glad to get a man
with his appetite and beard, that they would in-
sist on Govt, giving him the £l2OO. But no—the
callous legislators did nothing of the kind. On
the contrary, some of these have not hesitated to
express doubts as to whether he is a suitable man
for the billet. Doubts about Jenkins—fancy !
The situation has evidently worried the poor
man. He sometimes, worn out with labor, falls
asleep in his chair in the House. The other night
he went to sleep and his head lolled over to one
side, when a waggish member seized a top-hat
and put it on the head of the somnolent one.
Thereupon the ladies in the gallery tittered, and
Jenkins woke to find himself an object of laughter.
Nobody ever laughed at Jenkins in Vic. Council.
People would as soon have thought of laughing at
the Ten Commandments. It is better he should
go back. No official who has served so long in
the Council ought to be transferred to any office
in an up-to-date body.
“Melbourne girls are rolling up to see and hear
Senator Smith orate in the Upper House. Years
ago, this new member from the West—then a
clean-shaven, quiet-spoken fellow—was a familiar
figure among ball-room Johnnies. Five years in
the West have developed him physically and
mentally. Now he is the best-looking Senator of
the crowd, bearded, bland, broad-shouldered and
fairly oratorical. He is still a bachelor and still,
they reckon, an easy victim to a siren’s smile.
“A N.S.W. member of the House of Reps.
(Mr. Conroy) has already been elected by an
enormous vote of the Gallery Girls a gallant and
ohampion of the Sex. Last week the clement
Conroy was almost cheered by the listening
maidens for his ad vocacy of the cause of the Post-
office petticoat-brigade.
Writes a Maoriland friend :
“ I hear the Maoris up north are distinctly not
pleased with the Royal visit. It is etiquette for a
chief, when visiting another, to take his most costly
possessions and lay them at his friend’s feet. Chief
No. 2 picks out something inexpensive, expresses
in the natural extravagance of the native lingo
how overwhelmed he is by attentions of chief No. 1, but
could not possibly dream of taking presents of so great
value, &c., &c. Now, when this pretty pageant was
enacted with the Cornwalls, they took Everything. Took
all and considerable, and gave in return—the usual auto-
graph photo. The daughter of Major Kemp took up
about 70 mats, ranging in value from £lO to £25 ; one
kiwi mat was valued at £35, but really irreplaceable. They
were all accepted—every one —a sort of * let-’em-all-come
game.’ Now the noble native is thinking very hard.
“On Thursday Ro3 r a!ty was billed for a railway trip
across the Rimutakas, where the Fell engines are used.
That morning writer received a letter from a lady in
S3’dne3 7 . Extract: 4 He is small, weak and nervous .. .
a bigger and stronger man is wanted for the part. . . .
His duchess is the better horse. ... If they take him
over the Rimutakas he’ll be scared to death, &c.’ Picked
up the morning paper and read : ‘ It has been decided
to forego the trip over the Rimutakas to Featherston. A
short run up the Hutt Valley has been arranged instead.’
“The Parliamentary reception at Wellington on Thurs-
day night got through 70 cases of champagne. One mem-
ber was in a conspicuous condition of mops and
brooms and about 30 tickets for the function were mis-
appropriated, which possibly accounts for the presence of
a lady ‘ got up regardless,’ but who, at the time, was un-
known to those present. She impressed her personality
very loudly upon those present, finally attracting the
attention of Royalty. A self-sacrificing personage took a
seat alongside to keep her down. Soon she gently
drooped her head until it reposed comfortably upon the
shoulder of the personage, and she slept.
From Brisbane
“ Miss Lesley Madden, daughter of his Waxellency
of Melbourne, has arrived in Brisbane, and is
being received with joy. She strikes one as a pretty,
plump little maiden with gracious manners, smart clothes,
and a very fascinating streak of grey hair straight in the
middle of her forehead. It leads up through the softly-
waved dusky coiffure to a lovely diamond crescent, and
already anyone who possesses a grey streak is considered
fortunate indeed. She has struck one of Brisbane’s few
cold snaps, and is able to air a remarkably well-cut black
serge coat and handsome grebe at Polo and other out-
door functions.
“Lord Lamington was lavish in his invitations to 4 be
sure and see us at Lamington when you come home ’—
he scattered the precious words as he waved adieu—and
methinks t’will be a motley crowd who finally arrive at
4 the cradle of his race.’ Governors who were hail-fellow-
well-met in the kawlinies are kittle oattle to catch at
iQme. Was it a true bill of one of them that he left strict
orders to the guardian of his doors that 4 no dogs, tramps,
or Australians,’ were to be admitted ?
“ Lady Lamington’s tears have left a good impression
behind her. She cries becomingly, too, and her coun-
tenance was not so swollen or distorted as it might have
been considering that she cried buckets-full. A better
impression still was made by a kindly deed at the last
moment—that of using her influence to have a certain
ex-hotel servant reinstated. His Ex. showed signs of
suppressed feeling, say the Brisbane papers, and Brisbane
immediately wept also and quite forgave him the yawns
(not always suppressed) in which he sometimes indulged
during Govt. House functions. Doubtless the Laming,
tons beoame mueh more popular as time west on, and
their star has especially ascended ever since the Yorks
visited Queensland with only two out-riders and no
State carriage, or knighthoods, or silver spoons, or
anything.

44 Melb.” : Bertram ig unimpeachable, but his tricks are
surprisingly old. Give us more matter, with less patter
—though that is clever in its unctuously self-satisfied
way. The fact is, these wizards carry too far the doing
without apparatus. People go to be amused, not to see
how far a conjuror can go in little miracles, only to be
rightly appreciated by other sleight-of-hand professors.
The yore time men, such as Anderson and Heller, built up
their stage for hours before the performance, and the
whole unfolded like a sunflower. Cumbrous, say the
moderns. But the performance was followed through-
out with exhilaration. The show never dragged.
Widely printed in the Yankee press that a well-adver-
tised Australian songstress has given a lover of hers a
beautiful pair of black eyes.
Died recently in Sydney, Nellie Lambert, a charming
and kind-hearted little woman, and a well-known and
well-liked operatic artist, who first appeared with Lyster’s
opera co. in contralto parts. She was very successful in
“ boy ” characters, such as Lazarillo, in “ Maritana.” She
was also competent in more serious roles. While with
Lyster, Miss Lambert became the wife of baritone Charleß
Templeton, and consequently stepmother of Lempriere
Pringle, at present with Musgrove’s opera co. After
Templeton’s death his widow married Mr. T. Falconer, a
professor of music in Sydney. She leaves one daughter,
Miss Ethel Templeton, an accomplished pianist, at pre-
sent in Queensland.
Flora Anstead’s retirement to Ivew Asylum is not the
result of poisonous hair-dye, as has been stated. Poor
Flora, now 06 years of age, is suffering from senile
dementia, and her troubles will soon be over. The Dis-
tressed Actors’ Fund had charge of its old friend for some
time before she became a case for asylum treatment, so
she will not be lost sight of until her name is listed with
the Bygones in the Fund’s books. All the property left
to Flora Anstead, who was a saving woman when she had
the chance, is the remains of a theatrical wardrobe and a
gold watch and chain, presented to her by Aarons, on the
occasion of the opening of the old Melb. Bijou, originally
known as the Academy of Music. Flora Anstead was a
popular leading lady in those days—the ’7o’s. As one of
Two Roses, and the principal one at that, she identified
herself with the first Australian production of Byron’s
mascotte comedy. In her private life she was the unfor-
tunate heroine of a tragedy. The husband, who had been
a costly incubus, disappeared, and after some considerable
lapse of years, the missing waster was reported dead
Flora, in due course, married Jerry Dodge. Probabty it
was the announcement of her marriage that brought No.
1 husband on the scene again. Jerry Dodge died, in the
most considerate manner, soon after the other fellow’s
return, and the other fellow subsequently vanished, os
before.
Jessie Williams, the pretty young dancer of Rickards’
Co., provides an occasional sensation by swooning on the
stage, or at the wings after her pas de seal. The foolish
damsel is evidently a martyr to her pluck. Not content
with a graceful dance, she sometimes adds the contortion-
ist trick of running round herself like a spider whilst her
head rests upside down upon the boards. Then she
struggles in a dazed manner to her feet, gives a final kick,
and—likely as not —falls a victim to her rashness. Jessie’s
troubles are of the cataleptic kind, yet she recovers
consciousness and elasticity in time for the finale of the
first part. Her reappearance, white-faced and tired-
looking, is rather pitiable, but then the young enthusiast
would probably have another fit right away if the stage-
manager stopped her from going on to complete her share
of the contract.
“ MacDonald, of Ladysmith,” writes Tub Bulletin’s
London correspondent, “ delivers his lecture on
Whit-Monday and the two following evenings at the
Polytechnic Hall, Regent-street. He has been received
here with open arms and has his time so occupied with
supplying articles to order that he complains he has not
had much chance to see “ the Village.” As it was ‘ down-
below,’ crowds called upon him claiming relationship or
acquaintance, and attempted to ‘ bite his ear’ with much
rapacity. Geach is hustling like a ‘ brickfielder," and
sandwich-men peramble the main thoroughfares with
Mac. depicted ‘ before ’ and ‘ after ’ the siege. The war
is pretti' dead here, so D.M. may boom or have but
moderate luck. The 4 ear-biters ’ aforesaid —who literally
live upon arriving Australs—have not found Mac.’s ears
too chewable, but they’re a tough and hardy class, and
one wins occasionally.”
The most entertaining member of the Chinese opera
co., which has just concluded a highly successful season
at Melb. Demo., is the tall, stolid Chow who impersonates
the lady’s maid. Rouged and powdered he makes a
fairly presentable Chinese gal and smirks at the male
characters just after the fashion of the lady’s maid of
European countries. The most striking feature of his
acting is the swing of his hips as he crosses the stage. To
see him swagger, it is easy to imagine that he modelled
his stride on the little seventeen-year-old Bourke-street
Saturda3 r night donah.

year.
