Great Weakness and Anaemia
An Interesting Case
And a Perfect Recovery.
The Case of
MRS. SARAH DILLON.
(by our own reporter.)
As our reporter was one day pursuing his
usual mission, involving the collection of
items of interest to the reading section of
the community, he happened to hear of the
remarkable recovery from weakness and
continuous headaches of Mrs. Sarah Dillon,
who was then residing at 5 Norton-street,
Surry Hills. He accordingly called upon
that lady, who cheerfully offered to give
him all tne information he desired.
“My case must have been a particularly
interesting one to the doctors who attended
upon me,” began Mrs. Dillon, “for the very
simple reason that they were of the opinion
that I was beyond the scope of medical
treatment. I can assure you it is exceed-
ingly dispiriting to be told by your physician
that your condition is the natural result of
old age creeping on, and anything he
prescribed for you could not possibly do any
good. And it is especially so for a woman
like myself, who had previously enjoyed the
best of health. I had never before that
time known what it was to have a day’s
sickness, excepting as a child, when I met
with an accident to my hand, which soon
got all right again ; but I do not call that
sickness. • And then to be suddenly
conscious of an ailment which was gradually
drawing all the vitality from me, and to be
told that nothing could be recommended by
mj doctor to cure me, you can easily
imagine how despondent I became.”
“What were the symptoms of your
malady, Mrs. Dillon?”
“Well, I cannot very easily describe them
at the commencement of my illness, because
they were altogether too complicated. I
knew that my blood was not in good order
on account of the alteration in the colour of
my complexion. My lips assumed an un-
healthy paleness, and my eyes became dull
and heavy. I cannotsay that I suffered a great
amount of pain, although very frequently I
would become possessed of a sickly feeling
which left me languid and extremely weak.
It would have given me a great amount of
relief if I could have slept when I retired to
bed at night; but even that luxury was
denied me, for I would turn in feeling so
jaded and worn out that I felt quite sure of
getting a little repose; but I was invariably
disappointed. As a result, it was almost an
impossibility to get up in the mornings, and
when I did manage to do so, after much
perseverance, I was too weak and exhausted
to stand. During the day I would be
afflicted with a feeling of a drowsy languor,
as though everything taking place around
me was no interest or importance; and the
next development in my failing health was
something entirely new. . I had never
before known what it was to faint; but now
it was a frequent occurrence for me to go
right off without any apparent cause, and
when I regained consciousness I would be in
ft depressed, state of mind, for a long time.
It was enough to frighten any woman at my
time of life to experience such threatening
indications of a general break-up of the
system; and when I tell you that my
appetite had abandoned me altogether, you
can understand how dejected I was. The
most tempting food failed to make me eat,
and the robust appearance I had so long
been proud of gave way to a condition ol
emaciation.* The sight of food was a source
of trouble to myself, as my inability to eat
was an additional reminder that I was
gradually wasting away. And the hopeless-
ness with which I thought of the future was
brought about through the doctors’ opinion
that there was nothing in the way of
medicine which could possibly restore me to
health. The idea of being in a state of such
utter abandonment occasioned me no end
ofworry and anxiety, which, I’msure you will
agree, was quite natural. No matter how ill
a person may be, I think they can always
keep up their spirits to a certain extent if
they are following out a course of treatment
which will encourage them to believe that
their health may ultimately be regained.
But in my case I was apparently left without
a chance. However, I made my mind up
that it would be foolish to continue in such
a state of misery without making an effort
in some direction, because I did not think
anything in the way of physic could have the
effect of making me worse, mv condition
then being so forlorn. By a stroke of good
luck I secured the right remedy at the first
attempt.”
“How were you so fortunate?” enquired
the reporter.
“ It was through listening to the advice of
a lady friend of mine, who had benefited
through the same medium, that I was so
favoured by Dame Fortune. She strongly
recommended me to try Clements Tonic,
assuring me that if I did so there would be a
decided change for the better in a very short
space of time. It was with a certain
amount of doubt and misgiving that I con-
sented to give it a trial, although it was a
pleasure to think that there was something
which might have the effect of recuperating
my wasted system. I was really too
dejected to place confidence in any medicine;
but I sent for a bottle of Clements Tonic by
way of experiment, and, would you believe
me ? I felt myself improving after the first
few doses. Day by day my appetite gained
ground, and I was soon able to sit down
and enjoy a comfortable meal. The old,
worn-out, despondent feeling of weakness
entirely left me, and the vigorous blood
that now ran through my veins had such a
stimulating effect upon my spirits that I
soon became quite cheerful again. The
racking headaches from which I had
suffered were but a memory of the past; and
I can tell you they used to be very severe
indeed. They never attack me now,
however, for I continued taking Clements
Tonic till my vitality was thoroughly
restored, and my health is now as good as
ever. I think in a country like this, where
the heat is so oppressive in summer,
Clements Tonic is a grand thing to have in
the house, and I always have it on hand
during the hot weather to take as a stimu-
lant. I believe that is why my strength
keeps up so well.”
“Do you give your w consent -to the
publication of this interview?”
“With the utmost pleasure. I owe my
recovery exclusively to Clements Tonic, and
now I always recommend it to jjy friends.”
STATUTOIIY DECLARATION.
I, Sarah Dillon, of 5 Norton-street, Surry Hills
Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have carefully
read the annexed document, consisting of four folios
and consecutively numbered from one to four, and
that it contains and is a true and faithful account
of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also
contains my full permission to publish in any way
my statements—which I give voluntarily, without
receiving any payment; and I make this solemn de-
claration conscientiously believing the same to bo
true, and by virtue of the provisions of an Act made
and passed in the ninth year of the reign of her present
Majesty, intituled “An Act for the more effectual
abolition of Oaths and Affirmations taken and made
in the various Departments ot the Government of
New South Wales, and to substitute Declaration®
in lieu thereof, and for the suppression of voluntary
and extra-judicial Oaths and Affidavits
Deolared at Sydney, this 30th day of July, on*
thousand nine hundred, before me,
CHAS. E. WIGZELL, J.P.

INVALIDS
\ AND
THE AGED.
Concerning the late James M‘Kean
Obit. : Solicitor James M'Kean, biggest man in Mol
bourne and probably the longest lawyer in Australia,
Standing 6ft. Gin. high, with snow-white hair and beard.
Jimmy was a striking figure ot Melb. courts, and looked
muoh.more than the 69 years of his obituary notices. A
North of Ireland man, he arrived in Melbourne as a lad of
20, and worked for a few years (as he was always fond of
relating) as miner, shearer, cook and rouseabout. His
first step upward was to the editorship of a small country
newspaper and thence to the proprietorship, where he
found time to become a solicitor. Jimmy was in Parlia-
ment about 20 years ago, and, for a brief period, was
Minister for Lands. Though short, his Ministerial career
was lively, for he got into a fight with Melb. City Council
about Carlton Gardens, where the Exhibition building
now stands ; and, one Saturday, proceeded to settle the
argument by bringing up a body of axemen, chopping
down the gates, and driving a cart through the central
path, as a declaration that the council had no right to
block it up. But, after all, the council won the day, and
at the present time the Gardens remain closed to traffic.
FitzGibbon, then town-clerk of the city, was M'Kean’s
opponent in the row, and the people of Melbourne have
to thank the pompous old bard that the big man did not
get his way and destroy the Gardens. Shortly after this
sally, M‘Kean, defending a prisoner at Collingwood court,
was told by the Bench to behave himself, as he was not
in Parliament then. Mac retorted that his fellow-members
were a lot of low ruffians, and the man who behaved in
their company would not be understood. For this, an
indignant and virtuous Leg. Assembly arose and sent
Jimmy to the leads of the House, where he remained a
prisoner at a cost of £1 a day, which he was expected to
j ay. But he would neither pay nor apologise, and, as he
showed no signs of surrender, the House overcame an
awkward situation by allowing him to escape. A poor
pleader, he got the reputation of a good police-court
lawyer solely by his immense presence, his bull-like voice,
and his power of giving the other man “ rats.” He also
had the reputation, given by people on the strength of
one or two appearances, of being a terrific bully, destitute
of feeling. But this was altogether erroneous. The
writer remembers having seen big Jimmy, a couple of
years ago, crying like a baby, as he was defending two
siilv married women—utter strangers to him—accused,
under pitiable circumstances, of shop-lifting.


THE PIANOLA
Will enable yon or any member
of your family to play the Piano.
There are no conditions.
It makes no difference if you literally
do not know one note from another.
All Classes of Music
Accessible.
Operas, Overtures, Symphonies, Ora-
torio, Dance Music, Song Accompani-
ments, are at your immediate
command.
You regulate the
Expression.
By means of levers you control the
“touch,” “accent’’and “tempo,” the
same as if you struck the keys with
your own fingers. You may thus
give your own individuality to your
playing.

human fingers.”

Finis.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In deep? of space alone,
Beyond the starry sea,
God sate upon His throne ;
The Earth was on His knee.
Musingly He said,
Turning the small globe o’er,
“ I tire of Men I made ;
They please me now no more.
“ I gave them this green earth,
With all its streams and seas,
Whereon to dwell in mirth,
And pleasantness and ease.
“ I made the sun arise
Each morning in the East;
I lit with stars the skies
At night, as for a feast.
“ And, when to Heav’n above
For more gifts they did call,
I sent my Angel Love
With my best gift of all.
“ They are consumed with greed,
And eaten up with pride ;
Each little, paltry creed
Counts Me upon its side.
“ And, when they go to fight,
Each party calls on ME
To aid the Right —its Right—
And give it victory.”
Then God the Earth surveyed
Once more, and thus spake He:
“I tire of Man I made” —
And brushed it off his knee.
With all its glories ripe
The Earth passed, like a spark
Blown from a sailor’s pipe
Into the hollow dark.
N.S.W.
V. J. D.
The Jealousies.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
George Chappell died in great mental agony
and mutiny. If ever man had found the world
beautiful, and the mere contemplation of the end
of it for him—terrible ; it was this man. The
mateship of an ideal wife —the perfect complement
of him—had made life a poem. Their married
years had not brought that boredom which affected
writers say is the governing condition of mar-
riage; a statement more often false than true.
The love of their courtship—begun as a raindrop,
had become a deluge—rising as a brook in the
hills of youth, and growing to the maturity of a
great sea.
And in the years that spun to them, and through
them and beyond, her feet went always in the
ways of constancy ; and although there was all
the ginger of a man in him, he had an anchor-love
to his heart.
He had worked hard and knowledgeably, and
so carried her through the four streets of
Poverty—Hunger, Cold, Wailing, and Con-
tumely—in which so much Love has come to .In-
difference, and his ambition, being linked to a
great affection for the One, had no boundaries.
To consummate that ambition he had turned
aside from the straight way that should have been
his into the roads where travelling becomes a
terrible task—calling for unslackening energies
and constant watchfulness so that one may win
by the fall of another. And then his one great
fault was born and waxed strong enough to kill
him ; for, whosoever it is who brings the message
of Alcohol to the man constitutionally used to
being thorough in all things, death sooner or
later pays the postage.
And, when dying, he racked himself with re-
grets for the time lost from her in money-making
and wassailing—in stimulating his tired, disgusted
soul to pile much money by selling that he had
not, and buying that he did not want—and in eat-
ing up his own energy in devouring the energies of
some other.
And until quite recently Fortune had even
robbed him of the wages of his prostitution to
lucre—and luck had given him mostly blows and
boot-favors.
And now, just when the sanguineness he had
held for many years had, by a sudden market
turn, materialised itself into coinage, and he had
achieved an influence whose outward and visible
sign was the turning of envious heads after him,
and that wealth which can gratify every unreason-
able hope, the Undesirable Creditor had come.
In these last moments of his he had no thought
of any 7 life to come. Even death, which is but a
matter of a few hours at most, cannot upset the
habits of a lifetime. Chappell also was satisfied
that- any world but her’s could only be a trivial
hell, and his eyes did not regard the voraginous
future.
He could see through the death-damp only that
beloved head—that dear figure as he had seen it
at their first meeting ; he could only hear—above
the welcoming voices of the dead —her voice, and
the clamor of the mine-batteries applauding her
promise to marry him.
But the Voices of the Dead called louder, and,
suffering a torture greater than fire or famine, his
heart for all its bitter mutiny, stopped beating.
His yet-numbed soul stayed with her, and it
saw that she smothered her grief until she had
streeked him, for, with her, emotion had always
given place to duty, and she would allow no other
to do his last offices.
That done—she gave way to almost a heart-
break. The pity of it all !—the husband dead,
and a child to come.
The soul of George Chappell could not witness
the first grief his wife had known which he had
not been able to soothe—and so departed, travel-
ling in a swift, vagrant manner that only uncon-
trolled thought can know, and beset by hosts of
souls—silent souls that had no ties of earth, con-
tent to stay where they were placed ; souls whose
thoughts were all of Love; starved souls vvhose
ideals were the mean ways of money; ambitious
souls clamant for world-power, and all clamorous
as young eagles for new homes in the bodies of
the Unborn. . ... , ,
As a man has done m life, so does he after it.
The soul of the stay-at-home pulseless man edges
closer to its corrupting body because that soul
never lived outside of its body’s needs. The soul
of the Very Man —of the Shakespeares, of the
Drakes, of Columbus, and of all the adventurers
since the music of the sea charmed the Argo to
her own launching—as their souls did somewhat
soar above their clay when that clay was sentient
—so do they soar and voyage when their clay is
tenantless.
So Chappell’s soul roved as his life had done,
and made great and wonderful journeys on old
tracks and new, visiting the places She had hal-
lowed with her presence. For old sake’s sake, he
remembered to be at Albany when the police-boat
goes out to the departing mail, when the anchor
is coming to the peak, and all the drift-weight of
the ship is held by a buoy, a cable, and a bollard.
He remembered to insulate his 5000 miles of way
to the Barrier Reef in the winter, the season She
had called “God’s Month,” when the offshore
wind puffs the pineapple scent in the face of the
sea. He remembered to repeat their last
hansom-drive in Melbourne, even to the rattle of
the wheels over the strip of stones in Collins-
street, at the corner of Swanston - street
—the little strip between the wood blocks
and the macadamised road. He remembered to
travel his old track again on sea, salt-lake, and
desert—through black wood bush, and dense
jungle—over silver-grassed pocket, spinifex-plain,
and limestone-range—in the deep levels of mines
and the galleries of great ant-like communities of
men. He translated himself to ways yet unknown
of men—to goldfields and treasure-houses undis-
covered, and made himself almost contented with
the thought that one day all this should be for
Her.
But, if love gives all, it also takes all; and,
although the Soul of Chappell had travelled dis-
tances that mean to men the work of many life-
times, the parson had not finished droning sonor-
ous nothingness over Chappell’s body ere the soul
was overwhelmed by waves of affection for the
wo than who had had to stay behind. Fearing a
return to the torture —for a soul does not lose
its selfishness when it leaves its coat of flesh —
he fought against his sea; but the last and
greatest wave—the strongest of all the Seven
Brothers of Memoi-y —poured its great waters
upon him and carried him to her.
And then he knew suddenly that his soul was
really all the appreciable part of him—something
more than an It—something of gender : and that
the old body of his had not even been the house of
his soul, but only its scarf-skin.
He found her great grief had caused almost a
metamorphosis, and rebelled against the limita-
tions that only allowed him to comfort her by sug-
gestion.
As the week went by, he saw that time and the
new habit of doing without him had lightened her
burden of sorrow, and at that he was filled with
black wrath, and jealousy, and fear that, by-and-
by, she should forget him. When these forces
were strongest in him he avoided her—as he had
done in life, when his mO|it unreasoning jealousies
had scourged him until her love and trust had
cured his wounds secretly.
* * • •
It was night at Necropolis. In the crowded
streets of the dead city were all sorts and condi-
tions of souls—generous souls—mean souls—souls
filled with all the ingenuity of vice—souls whose
Arctic chastities were Himalaya-high—the souls
of them whose beauty and of them whose loneli-
ness (the result of their ugliness) had proved an
equal snare the souls of all the inglorious
martyrs that great cities produce without think-
ing. Many souls in patient hope for the time
that should bring the chance of perfect life again
—many clamorous for immediate appointments—
and all waiting for positions as souls in the bodies
of the Unborn.
And then, from the jealous soul of Chappell,
came a cry : “ The dust ere now has found a voice
—shall my love be ever dumb?” and to his
cry an inspiration answered. Inspiration
being the Supreme made articulate, has no law.
Even in the worldly sense of the argument, to
succeed in anything, however seemingly im-
possible, it is only necessary to be inspired, and
then to be determined. And Chappell’s soul de-
termined to be the soul of Chappell’s son.
And to his inspiration and determination, add
supreme pity, which is undeniably a force. Pity
gave him his desire, and in that instant the
protein of Chappell’s wife determined its sex.
* • * •
“ Womb-photography ” —“ Maternal Impres-
sion ” —said her friends, when Chappell’s widow
bore a boy, a replica of his father, even to the
repetition of a great scar on the chin, and the
absence of the little finger on the left hand, and
sundry tricks of voice. But when a stupendous
mother-love she had never known for any of her
elder children came to her—when she felt herself
regarding herself as still George Chappell’s wife,
(and not his widow) and indignantly refused all
offers of re-marriage as if they were insults; when
she found the boy with such a fierce love and
jealousy of her that he sulked for days after seeing
her pet his brothers; when, man-grown and sought
of women, he said he had “ married his mother ” ;
when she marked that, although gifted with an
iron constitution and the strength that spurs a
man to excesses, he had all the hatred for alcohol
of the teetotaller who dare not be anything else ;
when, as a boy of twenty-two, he made a journey to
the centre of the continent and travelled direct to
the discovery of a great goldfield, which only
his father’s soul had seen before him; when she
heard the surprise of his men at his knowledge of
a track where none existed, and of creeks and
soaks and gnamma-holes that saved the party a
dozen times from death ; when he told her once
vaguely that, no matter how wealthy they were, he
had had enough of ambition ; she began to wonder.
But when he spoke of incidents of her court-
ship and of the early days of her marriage which
she knew that only herself and the dead man knew,
her eyes filled with affection and affright, and her
intuition grasped something of the truth.
So in her old age (which was only forty-six, by-
the-way) she had her youth and her lover again,
as if Time had never moved ; and he, although
the memory of his actual transition had disap-
peared, was to her lover and son in one.
But to everybody else who remembered the
father, the son’s likeness to George Chappell the
Elder was merely an extraordinarily perfect
maternal impression ; a wonderful redintegration
of even the scar on the dead man’s chin, and of
his blue speedwell eyes.
Adelaide.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The matriarchal Dr. Logan Campbell’s
splendid gift of Cornwall Park to Auckland city
is worth about £27,000. It consists of 230 acres of
the famous One Tree Hill estate purchased by the
venerable Mayor some years ago. The Doctor
originally meant to build there, and had the place
extensively planted. It lies right round the north
side of One Tree Hill, an old crater, or rather
union of three craters, from the summit of which
an unrivalled view of the Tamaki Strait and
Waitemata, and Manakau Harbors can be ob-
tained.

Hotels in Australasia.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I think of the fare one gets in the average
Australian hotel, my liver turns a back hand-
spring and then gets me down and kicks me; and
after that I feel light-headed all the day. In fact,
I didn’t own a liver till I went travelling about
the N.S. Wales country-towns. Whose liver
Would not get the jumps after a course of meals
something like this ? Breakfast: Chops, steak,
and sausages, all fried. You may have tea ot
coffee. Sometimes I say to the waitress “ I said
coffee.” “Well, you’ve got corphey, ain’t yer?”
she snarls. The country waitress must have a
liver too, by the way she snaps at you. Then I
take another sip of the stuff in dispute, and I say
nothing. All I can do is to stare hard at it and
scratch my head.
Sometimes fellows try to get humorous at the
hard fare, but you can’t knock any humor out of
fried chops, steak, and sausages when they look
you hard in the face every morning of the week,
barring Sunday. Then you get ham-and-eggs—
and everybody says “ Mine’s ’am an’ lieggs ! ” If
the ham runs-out you get round of corned beef
fried in bacon-fat.
* * * •
However, they give you a comfortable bed in
the country hotels. In Westralia they treat you
like a prince at table, and maybe do-up the menu
in French ; but you are a hog when you go to
bed. On the goldfields there are invariably three
beds in a room, and the rooms are often so small
that there isn’t space for any furniture or for your
boxes. Then you often intend to go to bed, and
find somebody lias got there before you. One
night I found a lot of long hairs spreading over
the pillow. So I went to the landlord and told
him there was a woman in my bed. He said,
“Oh, no; that’s a member of Parliament; we’ll
put you in another bed.” You get charged for
this sort of thing at the rate of 10s. a day.
There is no country like Westralia for combines.
There is no need for the high prices, but
the butchers agree to charge Is. 6d. per lb. for
meat, and the hotelkeepers agree to charge Is. for
drinks, and so on ; and if some enterprising indi-
vidual opens a hotel and cuts prices, the butchers,
the brewers, the spirit-merchants, and the bakers
refuse to serve him, and he is ruined. Same if a
butoher cuts the price of meat; in this case the
carcase-butchers won’t serve him. And all the
time the public is being robbed, because wages are
tending to climb down. But shilling drinks don’t
help the temperance party at all. In fact, I’ve
not heard of a temperance party in Westralia. It
is the land of glorious drunks.
I reached a mining town at midnight, and
walked across to the first hotel. After dodging
prostrate men through the bar-parlor, and finding
that they were too busy to attend to me, I piloted
my way back and accosted a policeman who was
leaning against the verandah post very limply.
"Where is there a decent hotel?” I asked.
"Thish ishdeoench hotel,” he said fiercely. “Ish
the besh in town. ” So I went back to the bar
• j ■, • ~ . .
again and possessed myself m patience until they
had lirae to f„ r a man who merely wanted
to get bed and mbals there _
** t a a- a, , ,
At last 1 got to bed. Sometimes these build-
ingfJ are made of wood) aometimea of galvanised
iron . You can hear the con{idences of the two
Servants tbree partitions away, and you would
tbink that the woman in the next room who is
giving her mere man a cul . t ain-lecture, is in your
own roo m-Babel is all over the house That
night two men started fighting in the bar-parlour,
and after a few rounds they did a retiring battle
down t he passage. Nobody interfered ; they just
went lurching from side to side _[_/ >_
a nd then a bedroom door would burst open and
they would fall through. At last they came to
my room. There was an extra bang, and the door
)urst m ’ fought a round and a half in
my room, and then retired down the passage
furt her. I was mad, and jumped up out of bed
and slammed the door, and said, “ You might
have shut the door after you, any way ! ”
At 7-30 a.m. a girl gives a quick knock at your
< C *” r ’ aTI A COI Hes in with a tray and hands
you a cup of tea and some thin bread and butter,
That is the rising-bell in Westralia. Everybody
is up for breakfast at eight, unless suffering from a
sore head. You can forgive Westralian pubs, a
great deal for fche way tbey servt) you in the
matter of meals ; the tables are the best in Aus-
tralia, on an average. They are often very good
in Queensland, north of Brisbane. New South
Wales is the worst of all, and Victoria is verv
little bettei , South Australia should not beex^
pected to have very good country hotels, as the
average S.A. farmer and bush - worker is the
meanest man under the Southern Cross. I knew
a n ? an start / e( l hotel-keeping in South Aus-
tralia. 1 he country folks used to drive in and put
tbe ir cattle in his stable, and feed them with their
own fodder, and bring in their own lunch tied up
in a piece of newspaper, .and wash it down at the
hotel pump. Sometimes they would go inside
and write letters with the hotel stationery', and
eyery tirae tbe old man had a beer tbe whole
family took on such airs you would suppose he
had helped the publican ..into a fortune. You
can’t expect much of a hotel in a country like that.
~ , . .. * - * , * .
My advice to the fellow starting travelling, who
wea / a goqd boofcs and is proud b f hia foot^earj
j s to clean them himself. The first rouseabout he
strikes will spoil them, and every rouseabout
afterwards will go on ruining them until nothing
under the|un wdl get a polisTi on them. I won’t
go so far as to say that the girls use your tooth-
bruBb) bu t they help themselves to the paste and
powder, and also leave long hairs on your brush,
But the abomination of the country hotel is the
house with two tables.
You have all seen the long, low hotel with the
picket _ fence enclosing half the verandah. That
fence encloses the precincts of the parlor-boarders’
section. There are a lot of green-painted kerosene-
tins with ferns and flowers growing in them, and
staghorn ferns are nailed on to boards which are
hung on the wall, and there are a few broken
basbe t-chairs or blistered wooden ones always
distributed about, where the parlor-boarder is
supposed to sit and have a smoke for the proper
advertisement of the establishment. At the table
inside there is a great show of serviettes, done up
like bishop-caps, lilies, or two straight horns
stioking out of a wine-glass. You pay Bs. a day
for these luxuries. A dean serviette is a luxury,
anyway.
Across in Maoriland, every time a man gets up
from a hotel table, his serviette is made into a lily
or a bishop’s cap at once —ready'for the next man.
If you are not in a hurry over your meals, of
course you can look out the more pallid spots on
the serviette, but when there is a train or steamer
to be caught you lose a heap of time missing the
grease and egg-marks. But the 4s. -a-day boarder
in N.S.W. doesn’t get any serviette at all.
There is a pub. the travelling neophyte ought to
avoid, and that is the bank-clerk house. I never
stay in one. When I get into a town I always
ask, “Where do the bank-clerks live?” and
when I am told 1 give that place a wide berth.
If I can’t learn, I go to the lowest-looking place in
the town to make sure that I don’t get mixed up
with them. I prefer farm-laborers any day. I’d
just as soon go and have my meals in a wax-works
as with a number of bank-clerks. You see, a
publican wants to give his house Tone and build
up a parlor-boarder trade. Almost everybody is
impressed by Clothes, and the bank-clerk is always
well-dressed. So he goes to the bank-clerks and
says, “ How much do you pay a week where you
are staying?” “ Sixteen shillings a week,” they
say. “Well, I’ll take you in for twelve-and-six,”
and, of course, the poor devils can’t resist the bait,
as they have very little pocket-money by the time
they have paid for their time-payment clothes
and squared their tennis-club fees, etc. Now, the
big pawnbroking business has somehow already
made the clerk something of a snob, so when he
gets in as a parlor-boarder he is absolutely unap-
proachable, and he sits at meals with a back like a
board and that curious sensitiveness about the
finger-tips which we call “ lady-like.” That is
all very well, but he won’t talk.
In my verdant travelling days, I often struck
a bank-clerk hotel. In those days, if I were
silent two hours’ conversation would be simply
bursting out of me, so, while I would be waiting
for the second course, I’d say, “ It’s a nice day if
it turns out fine?” There would be a dead and
unearthly silence but for the clatter of cutlery. I
was hard to beat, however. Looking round me, I
would pick out the fellow who looked most like a
sheep, and stare at him. “ That was a terrible
storm we had Thursday three weeks?” Spoken
to, lie would give a preoccupied sort of stare and
grunt, “Eh?” “Do you think England will go
to war with China?” I would say. “Pass the
salt, George! ” he would answer to the other
clerk.
One likes to see girls stand-offish, but you will
understand that a man doesn’t care to bump up
against fellows who want an introduction before
they will help beguile the tedium of the poor
traveller’s lonely existence. The bank-clerk pub.
of N.S.W. is the most unsociable place for a
stranger to strike in the two hemispheres.
* * ♦ *
Maoriland hotels are almost invariably one-table
houses. You may know what class place it is,
and be sure that it is only one class—4/6 house,
8/- house, or 10/-. The N.S.W. hotelkeeper might
travel Maoriland for points. The meals are the
best I have got outside Westralia. But the great
point the traveller notices is the fish and milk and
cream that you get every day. You may have
your fried flounder for nearly every meal, and a
jug of pure cow’s milk is put before each diner to
use ad hbttum. The commercial-room is an insti-
tution, and stationery and the latest news ar*
always to hand, besides the best weeklies,
The Mainland hotel is usually a huge wooden
building. You are sometimes put into a room
down a long corridor, where you seem a sort of
Robinson Crusoe, all alone. You wonder how it
pays ; but you don t wonder when you strike them
at race-time, or when the local show is on. Then
people are being turned from the door in scores,
In each bedroom is a coil of knotted rope by which
you escape if the house catches fire. In the
better-class hotels there is a rope-ladder, some-
thing after the style of that one Juliet used to
hang out of her window for Romeo. Then there
are notices tacked-up in all the corridors—“ Fire
escape at end of passage,’' and in the centre of the
hotel is a firebelk You read—“ This bell must
only be rung in case of fire.” You get quite scared
of fire at last, and you can’t sleep waiting for that
bell to ring-out.
• * • •
